Definition. Store-to-load forwarding [GXCN]

  • May 13, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

The mechanism by which a younger load on the same core observes the value of an older store that is still sitting in the Store buffer and has not yet drained to L1. The load-store unit checks the store buffer for any in-flight store to the same address; if one exists, the load's data comes directly from the buffer entry rather than from the cache, satisfying the same-core sequential semantics that the program expects.

Forwarding is the reason the commit/drain split does not break the single-core illusion. Within one thread, a store followed by a load to the same address must read the just-stored value — and would, on a machine without a store buffer, because the cache is the single source of truth. With a store buffer, the cache is briefly stale relative to architectural state; forwarding patches that hole.

The structural consequence shows up in Total store order: store-load reordering across cores is permitted precisely because forwarding makes same-core reads see the new value, while cross-core reads continue to see the old one until drain. This asymmetry is the entire point of TSO's relaxation — the store buffer can hide latency from the issuing thread without forcing other threads to wait.

Remark. Address matching in the store buffer is partial: most implementations check enough low-order bits to detect a hit but fall back to a stall (or a re-execution) on aliasing or partial-overlap cases. Reliable wide-then-narrow forwarding is one of the harder corners of memory disambiguation.

Definition. Memory fence [R03A]

  • May 13, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

An instruction that forces an ordering constraint between memory operations on either side of it. Fences exist because Relaxed consistency hardware reorders accesses aggressively for performance, and the programmer occasionally needs a point in the instruction stream past which prior accesses must be globally visible before subsequent ones may issue. A fence is the explicit knob for "wait until the world catches up here."

The x86 family carries three:

  • sfence — store fence. All prior stores must drain from the Store buffer before any later store may issue. Used to publish a buffer whose writes must be visible to consumers in order.
  • lfence — load fence. All prior loads must complete before any later load issues. Less commonly needed under TSO, but useful as a speculation barrier (Spectre mitigation cases) and on architectures with load-load reordering.
  • mfence — full fence. Drains the entire store buffer and serializes all prior memory operations against all subsequent ones. The hammer.

What a fence actually does in hardware is force the store buffer to drain (or wait for it to drain) rather than just waiting for Reorder buffer commit. This is the distinction worth keeping straight: ROB commit makes a store architecturally real but invisible to other cores; the fence is what makes it visible. The cost is whatever latency the cache hierarchy adds — fences are expensive precisely because they undo the latency-hiding the Store buffer exists to provide.

Under Sequential consistency every memory operation behaves as if a full fence sat on either side of it, which is why SC is fast to reason about and slow to implement. Real-world consistency models pick relaxations and require fences only at the points the programmer marks as ordering-sensitive — acquire and release Half fences (see Release consistency) are the common compromise.

Remark. A fence does not flush caches or invalidate anything — coherence traffic does that. A fence only constrains the issue order observed by the rest of the system.

Data race [P0NQ]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

Two accesses to the same memory location, from different threads, where at least one is a write and there is no happens-before relationship between them. The happens-before relation is what synchronization primitives like Locks and atomics induce — when one access happens before another, they are not racing.

Remark. Competing for the same lock is not a data race. Whichever thread acquires first happens before the next; the access inside each critical section is sequentially ordered.

Data-race freedom (DRF) is the implicit contract behind every relaxed memory model: if your program has no data races, Relaxed consistency hardware behaves as if it were Sequential consistency. Conversely, in the presence of a data race, the result is undefined by the language and the model.

Problem. Writing data-race-free programs is hard. Treating every shared access as a synchronization point destroys performance.

SC-preserving compilers approach. forbid the compiler from reordering shared accesses, leaving the hardware free to do TSO-style reordering. The optimization most disrupted is common-subexpression elimination across shared accesses — solved by inserting a "monitor" check on the coherence bit between expressions.

Hardware approach. Treat all pages as local/safe by default and extend page protection bits to the thread level: a non-owning thread accessing the page triggers a fault, exposing the data race at runtime.

ILP vs TLP

  • February 8, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

Definition. Instruction-level parallelism [JAZI]

  • May 13, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

Parallelism extracted from within a single thread by executing independent instructions concurrently. The instruction stream is sequential as written, but a sufficiently aggressive back-end can issue, execute, and complete instructions in a different order than they appear, as long as data dependencies are respected and the visible architectural state still looks in-order.

The machinery a CPU spends on ILP is the bulk of its area and complexity:

  • out-of-order execution — issue instructions whenever their operands are ready, not in program order.
  • register renaming — break false dependencies by mapping the same architectural register name to different physical registers.
  • Reservation stations + Reorder buffer — hold dispatched instructions while waiting on operands, then commit them in original order.
  • Speculative execution and speculation — keep the pipeline fed across control-flow boundaries by predicting which way a branch will go.

ILP runs into hard limits: dependency chains, branch mispredictions, and the quadratic scaling of issue-queue wakeup-select all cap how wide a single-threaded window can grow. Beyond that ceiling, more silicon stops buying more performance, which is the structural reason the industry pivoted to Thread-level parallelism for throughput-bound workloads.

Remark. ILP is what classic uniprocessor texts mean by "out-of-order superscalar execution"; see Notebook: Inside the Machine for the unwound history.

Definition. Thread-level parallelism [G4ML]

  • May 13, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

Parallelism extracted by running multiple independent threads concurrently and switching between them whenever any one of them stalls. Where Instruction-level parallelism mines a single instruction stream for hidden concurrency, TLP gives up on that ambition and instead assumes that enough independent work exists in the program (or workload) that some thread is always ready to make progress.

TLP makes the per-thread microarchitecture cheap. There is no need for a deep issue window, aggressive speculation, or CAM-based wakeup-select: if the current thread can't proceed, the scheduler picks another. The cost shifts from per-instruction machinery to context-holding — registers, program counters, and stack pointers for every resident thread must live somewhere fast.

Two dominant implementation styles:

  • Coarse-grained TLP. Conventional multicore CPUs and SMT: a handful of threads share a core, switched on long-latency events or interleaved cycle-by-cycle.
  • Massive TLP. GPUs keep dozens of warps resident per GPU architecture SM and switch on any stall. The GPU register scoreboard is the readiness check that makes this cheap.

TLP fails when the workload genuinely has only one thread of useful work, or when the threads contend on shared state (locks, Cache coherence traffic). It wins overwhelmingly on data-parallel kernels where the threads are independent by construction.

ILP vs TLP [RJEB]

  • May 13, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

The two strategies a microarchitecture can use to keep arithmetic units busy in the face of memory latency and dependencies. Instruction-level parallelism mines parallelism from within a single instruction stream; Thread-level parallelism mines it across many. Every modern design is a position on this axis, and the position determines the rest of the microarchitecture.

The choice is not really a choice — it is forced by the workload. ILP works only when the single thread has independent work to find; once dependency chains and branch mispredictions saturate the issue window, no amount of silicon spent on reorder buffers, wakeup logic, or speculation buys more throughput. TLP works only when many independent threads exist; throwing more warps at a single sequential dependency chain accomplishes nothing.

The downstream consequences split everything else:

  • Control overhead. ILP machines spend most of their area on out-of-order execution machinery — Reorder buffer, Reservation stations, register renaming, CAM-based wakeup-select. TLP machines spend almost none; the schedule is "pick any ready thread."
  • Caches. ILP needs large caches because a single stalled thread blocks the whole pipeline. TLP can afford small caches because the latency is hidden by switching threads.
  • Register file. ILP keeps one (renamed) register set hot. TLP keeps every resident thread's register set hot simultaneously — GPU register files are an order of magnitude larger than CPU register files for this reason.
  • Readiness tracking. ILP pays O(N²) for CAM-based wakeup-select across N in-flight instructions; TLP pays O(1) per instruction via a per-register scoreboard.

The slogan: CPUs spend a lot of silicon to find parallelism in one thread; GPUs spend almost none because they have many threads. Everything downstream — pipeline depth, cache hierarchy, scheduling policy, energy per operation — falls out of which side of this axis the chip sits on.

Data-level parallelism

  • February 8, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

Vector ISA / SIMD [Q7ZD]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

Single instruction, multiple data. Extend the Fetch-execute loop so that one fetch+decode applies the same operation to a vector of operands. The fetch-decode overhead amortizes over the vector width; loop bookkeeping (increment, compare, branch) is replaced by a single vector op.

Concretely, a vector ISA adds:

  • A vector register file — wider than scalar registers; specify the data width per element and total bits per register.
  • Vector load/store instructions for moving contiguous chunks of memory to/from vector registers efficiently.
  • Vector predication (masking) — apply the op only where the mask is true, allowing data-dependent control flow without scalar branches.

Trade-offs:

  • Need the array length to be divisible by the vector width for perfect speedup; tail handling is annoying.
  • Vector pipelines cannot do Forwarding cheaply — would need \(O(n^2)\) connections to every ALU.
  • Wider hardware vectors can be supported on narrower hardware by microcoded decomposition; backward compatibility falls out for free.

In practice, vector code is generated by:

  1. Intrinsic functions (architecture-specific C builtins).
  2. Vector data types in a language extension.
  3. External libraries (BLAS, etc.).
  4. Compiler auto-vectorization.
Problem. Aliasing: modifying a vector you're also reading from causes a race within the operation.
Remark. SIMD is hardware-driven data parallelism. The GPU descendant is SIMT, which softens many of SIMD's rigidities by giving each lane its own program counter and active mask.

GPU architecture [82U4]

  • May 13, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

A GPU sacrifices single-thread latency for aggregate throughput. The whole machine is built around the Thread-level parallelism design point — keep many threads resident, switch on every stall, spend almost no silicon on per-instruction control. The opposite end of the axis from a CPU:

CPU GPU
Caches Large Small (boost memory throughput)
Control Sophisticated (branch prediction, OoO) Simple (no branch prediction, no forwarding)
ALUs Powerful Energy-efficient, heavily pipelined
Optimizes for Single-thread latency Aggregate throughput

The GPU equivalent of a core is a streaming multiprocessor (SM). Each SM executes the same instruction across many threads in lockstep — the SIMT programming model — and uses a per-register GPU register scoreboard rather than CPU-style wakeup-select to decide which warp can issue next.

Definition. SIMT [7F19]

  • May 13, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

Single instruction, multiple threads. A GPU streaming multiprocessor issues one instruction per cycle to a group of threads — a warp — but each thread within the group keeps its own program counter and register state. The hardware enforces lockstep execution within the warp while preserving the illusion of independent threads to the programmer.

SIMT is the GPU's answer to a question the Vector ISA / SIMD world struggled with: how do you get the throughput of wide vector execution without forcing the programmer to manually vectorize? Two mechanisms make this work.

Warp divergence serializes both sides of a branch [OGCSM]

  • July 6, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

When threads in a warp take different branches of a conditional, the warp does not split into smaller warps. Instead, the active mask selects which lanes execute each branch; lanes on the inactive side run dummy instructions until the warp reconverges at the post-conditional join point. The programmer writes ordinary scalar code with if/else, and the hardware silently masks the inactive lanes.

This is the chief ergonomic win over SIMD: the compiler does not have to prove that all lanes will take the same path. The cost is that divergent code pays for both sides — a 32-wide warp where 16 threads take the if-branch and 16 take the else-branch executes both branches sequentially with half the lanes idle each time.

The hardware sequences the two paths with a reconvergence stack — or, on recent GPUs, a barrier-based equivalent that avoids its deadlocks. The standard software mitigation is Warp Specialization: group threads with the same control-flow trajectory into one warp so the branch never diverges in the first place.

Grouping flexibility

  • May 13, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

Threads are assigned to warps based on warp-ID, but the hardware and compiler are free to regroup. Warp Specialization re-bundles threads with similar control-flow trajectories into the same warp so that future branches stay non-divergent. This is a class of optimization that pure SIMD cannot express — a SIMD ISA fixes the lane assignment at compile time.

The hardware cost of SIMT relative to SIMD is the per-thread PC and register state. The benefit is that a single binary runs efficiently across a wide range of dynamic control flow, which is why the model has dominated GPU programming since CUDA. The scoreboard mechanism described in GPU register scoreboard operates per-warp rather than per-thread precisely because SIMT treats the warp as the unit of issue.

Remark. SIMT is sometimes described as "SIMD with a per-lane PC," which captures the implementation but understates the programming-model impact: vectorization becomes a runtime, not a compile-time, decision.

Warp scheduling hides memory latency by switching among resident warps [1PWL]

  • July 11, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

A GPU keeps many warps resident on each Streaming Multiprocessor (SM) and switches between them on every stall. When a warp issues a memory op that misses cache it is dead for hundreds of cycles — but with dozens of resident warps the scheduler always has a runnable one, so aggregate throughput stays high even though single-warp latency is awful. This is the Thread-level parallelism design point cashed out on the GPU: spend silicon on resident warp state rather than on latency-reduction machinery. The switch is free because each SM's register file is large enough to hold every resident warp's registers at once, so a context switch is just changing which warp the scheduler picks — not saving state to memory the way a CPU context switch must.

Two things bound how well this works. Occupancy — how many warps are resident — sets the ceiling on latency hiding: with too few warps the scheduler runs out of runnable candidates. And the scheduler itself must stay cheap enough to choose a warp every cycle, which it does with a two-loop scoreboard check, marshalling each issued instruction's register reads through an operand collector.

Definition. GPU register scoreboard [XPAA]

  • May 13, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

The readiness-tracking structure that lets a GPU issue instructions out of program order without any of the CAM machinery a CPU needs. A scoreboard is a small table — typically one busy bit per architectural register — that records whether some in-flight instruction is still computing a value that something else might want to read.

The protocol is trivial. When an instruction issues, it sets the busy bit on its destination register. When it writes back, it clears the bit. Before any instruction can read its sources, the hardware checks the corresponding bits: if any source is busy, the instruction (or its entire warp) waits. The check is a single table lookup per source, so the per-instruction cost is \(O(1)\) regardless of how many other instructions are in flight.

This is enough because GPUs lean on Thread-level parallelism rather than Instruction-level parallelism. When a warp's next instruction reads a busy register, the GPU architecture scheduler does not stall — it picks a different ready warp from the dozens of resident warps and runs that one instead. The scoreboard's job is only to answer "is this warp ready? yes/no," which is far cheaper than the broadcast/compare a CPU Issue queue wakeup-select does to find dependency satisfaction in a single thread's instruction window.

The size difference falls out of this. CPU issue queues hold tens of entries; GPU instruction windows can hold many warps' worth of in-flight work, because every additional warp costs a row in the scoreboard and a register-file allocation, not a quadratic CAM expansion. See ILP vs TLP for why the design fork sits where it does.

Remark. The scoreboard's simplicity is what lets GPUs spend their transistor budget on ALUs and the Register file instead of control logic. It is one of the most visible places where the Thread-level parallelism design choice cashes out as silicon savings.

Warp [X9VZ]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

A warp is the unit of scheduling on an NVIDIA GPU: a group of ~32 threads that issue one instruction per cycle in lockstep. Each functional unit in the SM has as many lanes as a warp has threads, so a single fetched instruction fans out across all 32 lanes at once. The warp — not the thread — is what the hardware fetches, schedules, and tracks; the per-thread SIMT programming model is an illusion built on top of it.

Because the warp is the atom of execution, the two facts that dominate GPU performance are both properties of the warp: it hides memory latency by switching among resident warps instead of stalling, and it serializes both sides of a branch when its threads diverge.

CUDA Hierarchy [OGCS4]

  • June 1, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

Grid: Top-level define how many blocks are needed to cover the computation across SMs.

Block: Ensures all warps in a block are resident on the same SM and have the same shared memory.

Warp: A group of 32 threads that execute the same instruction.

Thread: A single thread within a warp.

Shared memory (GPU) [8GZN]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

Shared memory on a GPU is a small (per-block) on-chip scratchpad with latency comparable to L1. Software-managed — the programmer explicitly stages data through it, the hardware does not cache for them. Used to:

  • Avoid non-coalesced global accesses by reorganizing data layouts on the fly.
  • Reuse loaded data across threads within a block.
  • Communicate between threads within a block.

Bank conflicts serialize shared-memory accesses to the same bank [OGCRU]

  • May 25, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

Shared memory is divided into banks (typically 32). Each bank can service one access per cycle. If two threads in a warp access different addresses in the same bank, the accesses serialize — a bank conflict.

Three behaviors when warp threads access shared memory:

  • Different banks: full parallelism. No conflict.
  • Same bank, same word: hardware broadcasts the word to all threads. No conflict.
  • Same bank, different words: serialize.
Question. How to avoid bank conflicts?
Answer. Change the access pattern so threads hit different banks (often by transposing or by adding padding to break the stride that causes the conflict).
Remark. The GPU memory hierarchy is software-managed at L1 (shared memory) and coherent only at L2 — by restricting L1 to local-to-block scope, the architecture sidesteps the cost of a global Cache coherence protocol on a thousand-way-parallel chip.

Memory coalescing merges a warp's accesses into one transaction per cache line [U1FO]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

The hardware optimization on GPUs that combines simultaneous memory accesses by threads in a warp into a single transaction — when the accessed addresses fall in the same cache line.

Global memory latency on a GPU is 400–800 cycles. Coalescing is the primary way to keep the SM fed:

  • If all 32 threads in a warp access addresses within one cache line: one memory transaction, full bandwidth.
  • If addresses span multiple cache lines: the hardware serializes into multiple transactions; bandwidth drops proportionally.
Remark. Address permutations within a cache line don't hurt utilization — coalescing is about which lines are touched, not which exact bytes.

Coalescing is why memory layout matters so much for GPU code. The standard idiom is structure-of-arrays (SoA) instead of array-of-structures (AoS): threads accessing the same field of consecutive records hit consecutive bytes, which coalesce; threads accessing the same struct don't.

Question. Should you always cache on the GPU?
Answer. No. If you're memory-bound and have no Spatial locality, cache is wasted on data you won't reuse. CUDA exposes the option to disable L1 caching for these cases, which lets misaligned loads transfer only the bytes needed instead of full cache lines.
Remark. Use GPUs for parallel parts where throughput dominates. Use CPUs for sequential parts where latency dominates — the ILP vs TLP axis is the right frame for deciding which.

Graphics Processing Cluster (GPC) [OGCS7]

  • June 1, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

A Graphics Processing Cluster (GPC) is a top-level hardware partition inside an NVIDIA GPU. It's one level up from the SM in the chip's hierarchy.

As of Hopper, SMs located in the same GPC are connected via an SM-to-SM network, enabling DSMEM.

Streaming Multiprocessor (SM) [OGCS6]

  • June 1, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios
🌱 Stub — this concept is referenced but not yet written.

Cooperative Thread Array (CTA) [W2D5]

  • June 1, 2026

NVIDIA's hardware-level term for what CUDA programmers call a thread block.

A CTA is a group of threads that execute on the same Streaming Multiprocessor (SM), can synchronize via barriers (__syncthreads()), and share fast on-chip shared memory (Shared memory (GPU)). Threads within a CTA are scheduled and executed in groups of 32 called warps.

ASIC accelerators [OGCRS]

  • May 24, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

Application-Specific Integrated Circuits fuse multiple operations into hardware that a general-purpose pipeline would have to schedule sequentially. The motivating intuition: if you frequently multiply-accumulate, the multiply and the add each traverse the entire pipeline separately. A dedicated multiply-accumulate ALU does both in one cycle.

The cost-of-operation gap is large — 32-bit vs 8-bit addition has roughly an order of magnitude difference in energy per op. ASICs win by matching width to need.

TPU Architecture [NZZ7]

  • March 24, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

Definition. TensorCore (TPU) [OGCRQ]

  • May 22, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios
  • Vector register file each register is 8x128 vector registers with 8 horizontal lanes and 128 vertical lanes. There are typically 64 of these registers.

Definition. Vector Processing Unit [OGCRN]

  • May 22, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

A core component of the TPU architecture responsible for elementwise operations.

  • 2D SIMD execution, where each of 1024 sites execute same program in lockstep.
  • Each site contains 4 ALUs, enabling 4096 FLOPs.
  • Fed a 8x128 Vector Register in one cycle.

Definition. Matrix Multiply Unit (MXU) [OGCRP]

  • May 22, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

A core component of the TPU architecture responsible for performing matrix multiplication via a systolic array.

  • 256x256 totaling 65,536 multiply accumulate units i.e. each unit can multiply and add in one cycle.
Remark. Can only do 256x256 matrix multiplication, so the order is
  1. Tiling the matrix to load from HBM to VMEM
  2. Compiler tiles from VMEM to 256x256 tiles to load into MXU.

Processing in Memory for ML [2BGU]

  • April 6, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

Notes on guest lecture by Yufeng Gu titled Architecture and System Co-Design for Scalable Large Language Model Inference

TFLOPs increasing much faster than DRAM bandwidth -> Hardware scaling is hitting the memory wall.

Data center power consumption also increasing \(\approx \) 10% a year.

GPUs are under low utilization for LLM inference

  • Prefix stage: parallel
  • Decoding stage: sequential -> bottleneck!

As batch size increases, the KV-cache dominates memory usage.

  • KV cache size grows with context length.
  • Tradeoff between context length and batch size.
Remark. Unlike fully-connected cannot batch self-attention because it depends on the individual prompts.

Recall. operational intensity is \[\frac {\text {compute throughput}}{\text {memory bandwidth}}\]

Inference much lower operational intensity than GEMM!

  • Inference only doing matrix \(\times \) row
  • If embedding dim is \(D\), then this is only \(D\) ops / byte.

Processing in memory (PIM) enables faster memory bandwidth.

Problem. Has lower memory capacity (fewer GB) due to added processing units (PU).
Solution. Use Compute eXpress Link (CXL) to do memory expansion to get high memory bandwidth + expend memory capacity.
Remark. Put high use operations i.e. vector multiplication, element-wise multiplication in PIM module (99% usage). Put more complicated operations on PNM accelerator. TODO: ISAs for each specified on slides (not available yet).
Remark. CENT supports inter and intra-device communication via connecting communication controller to shared buffer and CXL port.

Communcation primitives. multicast and gather

  • Multicast: send to all CXL devices.
  • Gather: receive data sequentially from all CXL devices.
  • Multicsat-gather -> tensor parallel.

Peer-to-peer: can also support pipeline parallelism.

Remark. For fast communication, use tensor parallelism i.e. if connected via NVLink. If slower communication use pipeline parallelism. Can mix.
Remark. Longer context -> better performance for PIM (TODO: why?). HBM + PIM does not scale. Cannot arbitrarily add PIM.

On-chip interconnection networks [667T]

  • April 8, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

The network connecting cores, L2/L3 banks, and memory controllers on a Multicore chip. The same abstractions that govern off-chip networks (topology, routing, flow control) apply, but the constraints are different: wires are abundant but die area is scarce, latency is dominated by hops rather than propagation, and the network is part of the Cache coherence critical path.

On-chip network metrics [OMZU]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

The measurements used to compare network topologies and traffic patterns.

Traffic-independent

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios
  • Degree: number of links at each node. A proxy for router complexity — each port costs a buffer, crossbar input, state machine.
  • Bisection bandwidth: bandwidth across a cut that splits the network into two equal-sized halves. The minimum over all such cuts. A proxy for peak achievable throughput, especially for uniformly random traffic.
  • Diameter: maximum hop distance between any two nodes. The worst-case hop count.
Remark. Bisection bandwidth is less useful for on-chip networks than off-chip because global on-chip wiring is plentiful.
Remark. Ring bisection is 2 (two cuts to bisect); torus bisection is 4.

Traffic-dependent

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios
  • Hop count: hops between two specific nodes. The average over a traffic distribution is a useful proxy for latency.
  • Maximum channel load: the most-loaded link's load relative to per-node injection bandwidth, for a given traffic pattern. The proxy for sustained throughput.
  • Path diversity: number of distinct shortest paths between two nodes. Higher path diversity allows adaptive routing to spread load.

Performance regimes

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios
  • Zero-load latency: average hop count with no contention.
  • Saturation throughput: the injection rate at which latency curves upward exponentially.
Remark. Hop count is not the same as latency. Per-hop pipeline depth and link propagation matter — more short hops can beat fewer slow ones. Router pipeline depth isn't always known in advance.

Direct vs indirect networks [95AC]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

Two organizational families for on-chip networks.

  • Direct — every terminal node (core, memory controller) is associated with a router. Most on-chip designs are direct because area is precious and there's no point spending a router with no compute attached. The "\(k\)-ary \(n\)-cube" family (Ring topology, Mesh topology, Torus topology) is direct.
  • Indirect — routers may exist that connect only to other routers, with no terminal attached. Butterfly network and Clos network are indirect; a router in their middle stages just shuffles bits between input and output stages.
Remark. Indirect networks can shorten the diameter for the same node count, but they pay for extra silicon area for routers that don't serve traffic locally. Often the right call off-chip; usually the wrong call on-chip.

Topologies

  • April 8, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

Crossbar [YV5J]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

The simplest indirect network: an \(n \times m\) grid of switch points connecting \(n\) inputs to \(m\) outputs. Any input can be connected to any free output simultaneously — non-blocking.

  • One hop per transit (minimum possible).
  • Serializes if multiple senders target the same receiver.
  • Switch area grows as \(O(n \cdot m)\) — the dominant cost as systems grow.
Remark. Crossbar router area scales as the square of the port count, which is why high-radix routers are dramatically more expensive than low-radix ones.

Crossbars are common as the inner switch of larger structured networks (Clos network, Fat tree) — large flat crossbars are impractical at scale, but small crossbars are cheap.

Ring topology [UEA0]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

A direct network where each node connects to two neighbors, forming a closed loop. Bidirectional rings allow either direction of travel; unidirectional rings allow only one. The simplest topology to implement and reason about.

In the \(k\)-ary \(n\)-cube family, a ring is a \(k\)-ary 1-cube.

  • Degree 2 (every node).
  • Diameter \(\lfloor k/2 \rfloor \) for a bidirectional \(k\)-node ring.
  • Bisection bandwidth 2 — cut the ring at exactly two links.
  • Hardware overhead very low: routers have two ports.
Problem. Worst-case hop count is \(O(k)\). Doesn't scale beyond a small number of nodes.
Remark. Despite the poor scaling, rings are common in commercial CPUs (Intel's L3 ring bus on Sandy/Ivy Bridge) because they're cheap and 8–16 cores is enough.

Mesh topology [9SA2]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

A 2D grid of nodes with neighbors connected on the four compass directions, no wrap-around. The dominant on-chip topology in commercial designs (Xeon Phi, recent server x86 chips) because it lays out naturally on a 2D die.

  • Interior nodes degree 4; edge nodes degree 3; corners degree 2.
  • Average hop count for an even-\(k\) mesh: \[H_{avg} = \frac {nk}{3}\]
  • Maximum channel load for uniform random traffic: \(k/4\) — twice that of a Torus topology.
Problem. Non-uniform node degree. Edge nodes carry less traffic than they could, while interior nodes bottleneck on traffic flowing through them.
Remark. Mesh dominates Torus topology in practice despite the topology metrics being worse, because torus wrap-around wires are physically long and force expensive folding to equalize link lengths. Manufacturing wins over theoretical performance.

Torus topology [DDKT]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

A Mesh topology with wrap-around links connecting the ends of each dimension. In the \(k\)-ary \(n\)-cube classification, a \(k\)-ary \(n\)-cube. Uniform node degree (every node has degree \(2n\)), uniform load distribution, half the hop count of a mesh.

  • Every node has degree \(2n\).
  • Average hop count for even \(k\): \[H_{avg} = \frac {nk}{4}\]
  • Maximum channel load for uniform random traffic: \(k/8\) — half that of a Mesh topology.
  • Good path diversity: many shortest paths between any two nodes.
Problem. Wrap-around links are physically long. To equalize wire delays, the torus must be folded, doubling effective wire lengths and link area. The wrap-around also doubles the link count versus a mesh of the same dimensions.
Remark. Despite the theoretical advantage, mesh wins on real chips. Tori show up in HPC interconnects (Cray) where the wires can run on dedicated cables.

Variants:

  • \(k\)-ary \(n\)-cube: \(k\) nodes along each of \(n\) dimensions, all ends wrapped.
  • Asymmetric: \((i, j, k)\)-ary — different node count per dimension.
  • Mesh: same as torus but without wrap-around.
  • Ring: \(k\)-ary 1-cube.

Butterfly network [5WX9]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

A multistage indirect network. A \(k\)-ary \(n\)-fly has \(k^n\) terminal nodes connected through \(n\) stages of \(k \times k\) crossbar switches. At each stage, the routing decision selects which of \(k\) sub-groups to enter; after \(n\) stages, every terminal can reach every other.

  • Hop count \(n - 1\).
  • Under uniform random traffic, maximum channel load is 1 (perfectly balanced).
  • Channel load drops further for non-uniform traffic that respects the natural partitioning.
Problem. Zero path diversity. There is exactly one path between any source and destination. A single hot link wedges traffic.
Solution. Add shuffle stages that permute paths after each routing stage, so the same source-destination pair can be reached by multiple routes.

Clos network [YKAN]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

A three-stage indirect network parameterized by \((m, n, r)\): \(r\) input switches and \(r\) output switches each of size \(n \times m\), with \(m\) middle-stage switches each \(r \times r\). Total terminal nodes \(N = nr\). Non-blocking when \(m \geq 2n - 1\).

  • Number of total switching elements scales as \(N^{1.5}\) versus \(N^2\) for a flat Crossbar while preserving non-blocking.
  • Path diversity is \(m\) — multiple distinct paths between any source and destination.
Proof. There are \(rmn\) input-switch elements, \(mr^2\) middle-switch elements, and \(rmn\) output-switch elements: \[S = 2rmn + mr^2.\] With \(r = N/n\): \[S = 2Nm + m(N/n)^2.\] Choosing \(n = \sqrt {N}\) and \(m \geq 2n - 1 = O(\sqrt {N})\): \[S = 2mN + 2m\sqrt {N} = O(N^{1.5}).\]
Remark. A folded Clos network — input and output stages combined, making \(n\) input ports and \(m\) output ports bidirectional — is structurally the same as a Fat tree.

Fat tree [DFC7]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

A tree topology with bandwidth that doubles at every level toward the root. Each interior switch has logical degree 4, but as you climb the tree, link width doubles to absorb the combined traffic of its subtree — preserving bisection bandwidth.

Structurally, a fat tree is a folded Clos network: the input and output stages of a Clos are merged into a single bidirectional stage at each level of the tree, and the middle stage becomes the higher levels of the tree.

  • Each switch has logical degree 4 (2 down, 2 up — with link doubling).
  • Bisection bandwidth scales linearly with the number of leaves.
  • Path diversity grows toward the root.
Remark. Fat trees are the dominant topology for off-chip HPC interconnects (InfiniBand fat-tree clusters). Less common on-chip because the doubling links are hard to lay out on a 2D die.

Routing

  • April 8, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

Network routing strategies [VTS4]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

Given a topology and a packet's source and destination, which path through the network does it take? Three classes of answer:

  • Greedy (shortest-path) — always pick the shortest available route.
    • (+) Minimizes latency under light load.
    • (-) Overloads central links; worst-case throughput poor.
  • Uniform / oblivious random — pick a direction at random.
    • (+) Spreads load; avoids systematic hotspots.
    • (-) Wastes bandwidth on non-minimal paths; latency unpredictable.
  • Adaptive — at each hop, pick the outgoing link with the lowest local congestion.
    • (+) Dynamically balances load; best worst-case throughput.
    • (-) Routers need congestion state; risk of livelock without care.
Example. Tornado traffic on an 8-node bidirectional ring: node \(i\) sends to \((i+3) \bmod 8\).
  • Greedy: all packets travel counter-clockwise. Bidirectional bandwidth wasted; \(\frac {1}{3}\) utilization.
  • Uniform: randomly clockwise/counter-clockwise. Counter-clockwise gets \(\frac {2}{3}\) utilization, clockwise \(\frac {2}{5}\); worst-case \(\frac {2}{5}\).
  • Adaptive: sends \(\frac {5}{8}\) counter-clockwise to match traffic exactly. Best.
Remark. Adaptive routing tends to need Network backpressure signaling to know where congestion is, and care to avoid livelock when many adaptive decisions chase each other.
Remark. Oblivious strategies like Dimension-order routing and Valiant's routing algorithm give deterministic guarantees (deadlock-free, bounded path length) at the cost of giving up the load-balancing of true adaptive routing.

Dimension-order routing [83P2]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

An oblivious Network routing strategies for \(k\)-ary \(n\)-cube networks (Mesh topology, Torus topology): always exhaust the offset in one dimension completely before changing to the next. E.g. in 2D mesh routing, route X-then-Y — never turn back to X after starting Y.

The dimension order prevents routing deadlock: cycles in the channel dependency graph are impossible because no packet ever requests a channel in a "lower" dimension once it has started using a higher one. The dependency graph is therefore acyclic, and the standard cycle-equals-deadlock argument applies.

  • (+) Provably deadlock-free without virtual channels.
  • (-) Zero path diversity. Hot spots can't be routed around.
  • (-) Unaware of current network state — oblivious.

The standard remedy for the path-diversity problem is Valiant's routing algorithm, which keeps the deadlock-freedom of dimension-order routing while spreading load with a randomized intermediate hop.

Valiant's routing algorithm [XLDA]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

An oblivious Network routing strategies that achieves provably optimal worst-case throughput by introducing randomness in path selection:

  1. For a packet going from \(s\) to \(d\), randomly pick an intermediate node \(w\).
  2. Route \(s \to w\) using Dimension-order routing.
  3. Eject the packet at \(w\), then re-inject it.
  4. Route \(w \to d\) using Dimension-order routing.

To preserve deadlock-freedom, the intermediate node must remove the packet from the network before re-injecting — otherwise the channel dependencies of the two halves could interlock.

Remark. Restricting \(w\) to lie in the sub-quadrant defined by \(s\) and \(d\) keeps paths near-minimal while still spreading load — the practical compromise between guaranteed throughput and added latency.
Remark. Valiant's algorithm trades average-case latency (paths are longer) for worst-case throughput (any traffic pattern looks roughly uniform once randomized). The right call for adversarial workloads; overkill for uniform traffic.

Flow control

  • April 8, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

Routing deadlock [DWV1]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

Routing deadlock occurs when a packet cannot make progress because the buffer at its next link is full, and the packet holding that buffer is itself blocked waiting on a buffer this packet holds — a cycle in the channel dependency graph.

Two structural responses:

  1. Avoid deadlock by construction. Dimension-order routing enforces an ordering on dimension transitions so the dependency graph is acyclic by design.
  2. Detect and recover from deadlock. Allow all turns. Detect cycles via timeouts. Drain stuck packets onto a backup network (a "slow channel" — physical, or more commonly an escape virtual channel) that uses dimension-order routing.

The relationship to other related concepts:

  • Backpressure (Network backpressure) is the signaling mechanism that lets a router downstream tell upstream it is congested, before the upstream actually fills the link.
  • Adaptive routing avoids deadlock locally; backpressure lets adaptive routing react to remote congestion.

Network backpressure [EZDD]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

A signaling mechanism that lets a downstream router tell upstream "I am congested — slow down" before its buffers actually overflow. With backpressure information, adaptive routing can route around remote congestion, not just local.

The mechanism propagates: a congested link signals back to its sender, which then knows its next link will fill, which signals further back. Useful information flows in the opposite direction of the data.

Problem. Acting only on local state can cause livelock — packets bounce between hotspots, never converging.

Backpressure breaks the livelock by giving local routers global-ish information without an actual global view.

Remark. Backpressure is mechanism; adaptive routing is policy. The two together are what real high-radix on-chip networks use.

Interface with the cache hierarchy

  • April 8, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

The on-chip network connects each core (with its L1) and the L2 cache banks (which may be shared or private). Each L2 bank has a cache controller (manages Cache coherence) and, at memory boundaries, a memory controller (talks to DRAM).

Two cache-organization choices interact with the network design:

  • Private L2 caches: each tile owns its L2 slice; cross-tile traffic happens only on private-L2 miss. (+) Lower hit latency; less interconnect traffic. (-) Replicated data; more off-chip traffic on misses.
  • Shared L2 cache: a single logical L2 split into banks, with each address mapped to a home bank. (+) Better utilization, no replication. (-) Every L1 miss goes on the network.

The interface to the network sits at the cache controller:

  • Miss status handling register (MSHR): on a cache miss, records what request the cache is waiting on. Matched against the reply when it arrives.
  • Transaction status handling register (TSHR): tracks outstanding memory transactions to off-chip memory from the directory cache.
Remark. Memory controllers are commonly placed at the chip perimeter, near the I/O pads. A memory controller can share a router with a core/cache tile (saves area; needs arbitration) or have its own (more area; no contention).
Remark. Hardware-managed shared memory is one design point; another is explicit message passing, where software (not hardware) is responsible for moving data between processes.

Implementation considerations

  • April 8, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

Links. Thicker wires with larger spacing → lower resistance, higher speed, better energy efficiency. The wire budget determines how many ports you can afford per router.

Routers. Each port costs a buffer, a crossbar input, state logic, and a link to its neighbor. Crossbar area scales as the square of port count, so high-radix routers are dramatically expensive. Rings have very simple routers but poor performance.

Floorplan. Tori need to be folded to equalize wire lengths, doubling effective wire length; meshes don't. Tori also have twice as many links as meshes, which may force narrower links and lower per-link bandwidth.

Folding can also equalize path lengths inside a network at the cost of increasing average latency.

Concentration allocates more cores per router. (-) Decreases throughput per node. (+) Programs with high locality do little remote communication, so concentration is a good fit there.

Custom and hierarchical topologies

  • April 8, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

General topologies aren't always optimal. Two construction strategies:

  • Splitting: start with a large crossbar; split into smaller switches until constraints are met.
  • Merging: start with many small switches and combine.

Hierarchical topologies use one topology within clusters and another across clusters — common when traffic has locality.

Notebook: Stanford CS336 [0085]

  • July 1, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Formal notebook for my journey in Stanford's CS336 as part of my LLM self-study endeavors.

Tokenization [0087]

  • July 1, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Introduction

  • July 1, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

A tokenizer is responsible for taking text and turning it into a numerical representation we can pass to a neural network i.e. \[\mathsf {encode}(\text {hello world}) \mapsto \begin {bmatrix} 24912, & 2375 \end {bmatrix} \] However, we also need \[\mathsf {decode}(\begin {bmatrix} 24912, & 2375 \end {bmatrix}) \mapsto \text {hello world} \] When defining a "good" tokenizer, we are concerned with the compression ratio i.e. the # of bytes per token.

Character-based tokenization

  • July 1, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Trivially, we can do character-based tokenization i.e. mapping each character to a unique number. For example, \(\mathsf {chr}(97) = \text {``a''}\) and \(\mathsf {ord}(\text {``a''}) = 97\). The compression ratio will be \(> 1\) because each token corresponds to a character potentially consisting of more than one byte.

Problem.
  1. Very large vocabulary
  2. Many characters are quite rare.
Remark. There is a fundamental tradeoff between compression ratio and vocabulary size. As an example, suppose we map every sequence of \(n\) bytes to a token. This would achieve a compression ratio of \(n\). However, this would require \(256^n\) tokens in our vocabulary.

Problem 2 was not entirely straight-forward to me. This actually reminds me of an idea from my information theory course, where we can assume we are given a "prior" over human language then we want to map low-probability characters to large strings in order to minimize the expected length (see huffman coding).

Byte-based tokenization

  • July 1, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

In byte-based tokenization, each byte is a token achieving a compression ratio of exactly \(1\), where a byte \(\mapsto [0, 256]\) . This is very elegant in the sense that our vocabulary size is fixed at \(256\).

Problem. However, with this, the length of our encoding is the number of bytes in the string, which will become too long.

Word-based tokenization

  • July 1, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

In word-based tokenization, we simply split the string into the corresponding words then map each of these to a unique integer.

Problem. The vocabulary size is "unbounded" i.e. if we see a word we have not seen before then we need to extend the vocabulary size.

Byte Pair Encoding

  • July 1, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

For the byte pair encoding (BPE), we instead train the tokenizer on raw text to automatically determine the vocabulary.

Remark. In GPT2, they first split the raw text into words and then do this byte pair encoding.
Algorithm. while true:
  1. Count occurences of byte pairs. Store in dict \(D\) with \(\{(b_1, b_2): \text {count}\}\)
  2. Find \((b_1, b_2)\) with highest count.
  3. Merge \((b_1, b_2)\) by \((b_{1}, b_{2}) \mapsto b'\)
  4. Replace all instances of \((b_{1}, b_{2})\) in string with \(b'\) i.e. \[b_{1} b_{2} b_{3} b_{4} \mapsto b'b_{3} b_{4}\]
Remark. There are a few additional implementation details to consider for project 1.
  1. \(\mathsf {encode}\) loops over all merges. Only loop over merges that matter.
  2. Detect and preserve special tokens (e.g. <|endoftext|>)
  3. Use pre-tokenization (GPT2 regex)

Motivating Questions

  • July 1, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

We start by discussing a few essential questions one hopes to answer when they set out to train a large model.

Question. How long to train a 70b parameter model on 15T tokens on 1024 H100s?
Answer. Mysteriously, we spawn from the ether (will be explained later) that total_flops = 6 * 70e9 * 15e12. Intuitively, we know that we need to perform some computation for each parameter for each token. However, the 6 is still an ominous mystery. Given the total flops, we then can just take the GPUs FLOP/s multiply by the number of seconds in the day and divide total_flops by this number i.e. days = total_flops / flops_per_day
Remark. In practice, we must also consider the model flop utilization (MFU). Basically, our algorithm cannot utilize the entirety of the maximum FLOP/s provided by the hardware due to things like memory bandwidth, etc. To account for this in the calculation, they just take flops_per_day / 2.
Question. What is the largest model that you can train on 8 H100s using AdamW?
Answer. An H100 has 80gb of shared memory i.e. h100_bytes = 80e9. The calculation then proceeds as 
 # parameters, gradients, optimizer state
bytest_per_param = 4 + 4 + (4 + 4)
num_parameters = (h100_bytes * 8) / bytes_per_parameter
what we need each of parameters, gradients, optimizer state will become clear later.
Remark. In practice, we also need to account for activations, which will depend on both batch size and sequence length.

Memory Accounting [DEQU]

  • July 3, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Tensor Memory. Tensors are stored as a floating point number. Memory is determined by (i) number of values in tensor and (ii) data type of each value.

Low Precision Data Types [DEQV]

  • July 3, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

float16: here, we decrease both the fraction and exponent bits. Importantly, there are only \(5\) exponent bits making the effective range \((2^{-6}, 2^{6})\). The dynamic range for small numbers is pretty bad. This can cause small numbers to "die" effectively killing these neurons for the remainder of learning due to backpropagation.

bfloat16: to fix the problems with float16, they instead keep \(8\) exponent bits and reduce the fraction bits to \(7\). This maintains the same dynamic range as float32 while still only using \(16\) bits.

fp8: Two variants

  1. E4M3: has 4 exponent bits and 3 mantissa bits
  2. E5M2: has 5 exponent bits and 2 mantissa bits
Requires modern Hopper Architecture i.e. H100 to perform operations. Also supported by TPUs.

Training Implications.

  1. float32 training works but requires lots of memory
  2. Using lower precision data types allows you to train larger models due to lower memory requirements, but can cause instability
  3. To optimize this tradeoff, use mixed-precision training.

Torch Tensors [DYIV]

  • July 3, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

In pytorch, tensors are contiguous blocks of memory, where their shape is controlled by strides. A stride basically just refers to how far you have to jump in physical memory to correspond to incrementing the logical index i.e. in a (2, 3) matrix each row requires jumping \(3\) spots in physical memory. Thus, stride[1] = 3.

Remark. Calling .view((m, n)) just changes the stride but does not modify the physical memory. If you call .view that results in a non-contiguous view, then you cannot call .view again because it assumes contiguous indexing to properly adjust the strides. To deal with this, they have .contiguous() and .reshape() is basically .contiguous().view().
Remark. All elementwise operations make a copy i.e. x + y. A useful function for masked attention is triu().

Einops

  • July 3, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios
 Einops is a useful python library to keep track of high-dimensional matrix operations i.e. matmuls over batch_size, seqlen, etc. High level overview
  • Initialize like X[float.tensor, "batch seq hidden"] = torch.tensor((2, 3, 4))
  • Can perform matmul with named dims like
    • z = einsum(x, y, "batch seq1 hidden, batch seq2 hidden -> batch seq1 seq2")
  • reduce: x = reduce(x, "x y z -> x y 1") is just like mean(x, axis=-1)
  • rearrange: x = rearrange(x, "... (heads hidden1) -> ... heads hidden 1")

Computation

  • July 1, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Definition. Floating Point Operations [4K5H]

  • July 12, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

We refer to the total number of floating point operatings as FLOPS. Alternatively, we refer to the number of floating point operations per second as FLOP/s.

Definition. Model FLOP Utilization (MFU) [99G8]

  • July 12, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Given a promised FLOP/s, the model flop utilization is given by \[\frac {\text {actual FLOP/s}}{\text {promised FLOP/s}}\]

Example. Performing a matrix multiplication \(A \cdot B\) for \(A \in \mathbb {R}^{(B \times D)}\) and \(B \in \mathbb {R}^{(D \times K)}\), requires \(2 \times B \times D \times K\) FLOPS

Optimizers

  • July 1, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

I hope to do a formal blog touring optimizers because I have written a paper on deep learning optimizers for my optimization course. Due to this, I will leave this blank for now. A brief list of the covered concepts are below:

  • momentum
  • adagrad
  • RMSProp
  • Adam

A key formula to remember: \[\text {total memory} \approx 4 \times (\text {params} + \text {activations} + \text {grad size} + \text {optimizer state size})\]

Remark. Another useful trick is to use low precision for forward pass activations, then use float32 for gradients and network parameters.

Architecture Variations [W4WB]

  • July 13, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Norms [7JRP]

  • July 16, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

Pre-norm vs. Post-norm. The first architecture variation discussed is when to apply the layer norm. As can be seen in the figure below, rather than apply the layer norm in the residual stream. They instead place it before the FFN and MHA layers.

They actually found that adding layer normalization before and after the MHA and FFN works the best. This is known as double norm.

RMSNorm vs. LayerNorm. Another useful trick is to use the RMSNorm instead of LayerNorm.

Definition. RMS Norm [1XNO]

  • July 14, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

The RMS norm is simply the layer norm without the added bias term and centering i.e. \[\mathrm {RMSNorm}: \mathbb {R}^d \to \mathbb {R}^d, \quad \mathbf {x} \mapsto \frac {\mathbf {x}}{\sqrt {\mathrm {Var}[\mathbf {x}_i] + \epsilon }}*\gamma .\] This is used to spare memory movement of layernorm, where the bias term \(\beta \in \mathbb {R}^d\) and has been found to be empirically almost if not as good.

One might wonder if matrix multiplies are the only thing that matters what can such a small change really accomplish.

  • Due to memory movement, despite being .17% of FLOPS, provided 25% speedup in runtime!!
Remark. Most people apparently just drop the bias term and keep the centering i.e. subtracting the mean. This makes sense because computing the mean does not require loading any additional information back to memory.

Activations [9X1X]

  • July 16, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

Despite a long list of activations, the two focused on are ReLU and GELU.

Definition. Rectified Linear Unit (ReLU) [BEA2]

  • July 14, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

The rectified linear unit(ReLU) is defined as \[\mathrm {ReLU} : \mathbb {R}^d \to \mathbb {R}^d, \quad \mathbf {x} \mapsto \max (0, \mathbf {x}),\] where the \(\max \) is done elementwise i.e. \(\mathrm {ReLU}(\mathbf {x})_i = \max (0, x_i)\) This provides "nice" gradients, making it common when training neural networks.

Definition. Gaussian Error Linear Unit (GELU) [5SPM]

  • July 14, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

The Gaussian Error Linear Unit (GELU) is a slight modification to the ReLU to account for the non-differentiability at \(0\). Namely, \[\mathrm {GELU}(\mathbf {x}) : \mathbb {R}^d \to \mathbb {R}^d, \quad \mathbf {x} \mapsto \mathbf {x} \cdot \psi (\mathbf {x}),\] where \(\psi (\mathbf {x}) = \mathrm {CDF}(\mathcal {N}(\mathbf {x}, \mathbf {I}))\)

Definition. Swish Activation [OZD8]

  • July 14, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

The Swish activation is given by \[\mathrm {Swish}(\mathbf {x}) : \mathbb {R}^d \to \mathbb {R}^d, \quad \mathbf {x} \mapsto \mathbf {x} s(\mathbf {x}),\] where \(s : \mathbb {R}^d \to \mathbb {R}^d\) is the sigmoid function.

Remark. The extra computation required by GELU or Swish is a non-factor because FLOPS are dominated by matrix multiplication and there is no increase in memory pressure.

Definition. Gated Linear Unit (GLU) [K2N7]

  • July 14, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

A gated linear unit is an activation function combined with an elementwise multiplication i.e.

  • \(\mathrm {ReGLU} = \mathrm {ReLU}(\mathbf {x}) \otimes \mathbf {V} \mathbf {x},\)
  • \(\mathrm {SwiGLU} = \mathrm {Swish}(\mathbf {x}) \otimes \mathbf {V} \mathbf {x},\)
where \(\mathrm {ReLU}\) is the ReLU activation and \(\mathrm {Swish}\) is the Swish activation respectively.

Remark. The matrix \(\mathbf {V}\) introduces additional learnable parameters. Therefore, an architecture that uses gating typically reduces their other parameters by a factor of \(\frac {2}{3}\).

Computing FFN and MHA in parallel is free speedup [ZAR4]

  • July 16, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

Another small trick is to parallelize computation. Traditionally, one computes the \(\mathrm {MHA}\) output and then passes this to the \(\mathrm {FFN}\) i.e. \[\mathbf {y} = \mathbf {x} + \mathrm {FFN}(\mathrm {LN}(\mathbf {x} + \mathrm {MHA}(\mathrm {LN}(\mathbf {x})))).\] However, this requires waiting for the \(\mathrm {MHA}\) to complete before performing the \(\mathrm {FFN}\) computation. It has been found that performing these in parallel does not cause any severe degradation in performance. Explicitly, instead they do \[\mathbf {y} = \mathbf {x} + \mathrm {FFN}(\mathrm {LN}(\mathbf {x})) + \mathrm {MHA}(\mathrm {LN}(\mathbf {x}))\]

Question. What is the role of weight decay in training large models? [U52K]

  • July 16, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

Since there is so much data, it is not feasible to train your model for multiple epochs. This is actually nice in the sense that we do not have to worry about overfitting and performing regularization.

However, many of the large models are still trained with weight decay. Tatsu claims that this is not to do with regularization but actually due to some weird interaction with the learning rate schedule. I am not sure I entirely understood this part.

Rotary Position Embeddings (ROPE) [TOD6]

  • July 14, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios
Notation. For this section, our notion of an embedding is a function \(f\) that takes a token and a position i.e. \(f(x, i)\) is the embedding of token \(x\), occurring at position \(i\).
💡 Insight. The inner product of two embeddings should only need relative positioning i.e. for any two \(\mathbf {x}\), \(\mathbf {y}\) with positions \(i\) and \(j\) respectively, there should exist some \(g\), such that \[\langle f(\mathbf {x}, i), f(\mathbf {y}, j) \rangle = g(\mathbf {x}, \mathbf {y}, i - j)\] Namely, this can be written as a function of just the relative positioning between the embeddings.

The simplest transformation that only preserves this relative information are rotations.

However, rotations are only easily defined for \(\mathbb {R}^2\). To get around this, they simply partition their input 2d slices and apply the rotation via \[\mathrm {ROT} = \begin {bmatrix} \cos m \theta _{1} & - \sin m \theta _{1} & & & \\ \sin m \theta _{1} & \cos m \theta _{1} & & & \\ & & \cos m \theta _{2} & -\sin m \theta _{2} & \\ & & \sin m \theta _{2} & \cos m \theta _{2} & \\ & & & & \ddots \end {bmatrix} \]

Remark. The \(\theta _i\)'s are fixed and chosen according to some schedule to capture different "frequencies".

The embedding are used by appling them to the query and key matrices separately i.e. \[W_K = \mathrm {ROT}(W_K), \quad W_Q = \mathrm {ROT}(W_Q)\] These are then what is used in self-attention.

The little details

  • July 1, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

A lot of deep learning is empirical magic. A quick look behind the curtain into this magic awaits below...

Hyperparameter Rules (CS336) [9VZH]

  • July 14, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Rule 1. \(d_{\text {ff}} = d_{\text {model}}\)

  • \(d_{\text {model}}\) is the input dimension
  • \(d_{\text {ff}}\) is the hidden dimension

Exception 1. in the case of GLUs, we recall that to account for extra parameters we scale \(\frac {2}{3}\). Therefore, \(d_{\text {ff}} = \frac {8}{3}d_{\text {model}}\).

Exception 2. In T5, they use \(d_{\text {ff}} = 64d_{\text {model}}\). The logic for this was that we could maximize the MFU by increasing matrix size. Ended up going back to small \(d_{\text {model}}\).

Rule 2. \(d_{\text {head}} = d_{\text {model}} / \text {num heads}\)

  • I believe the \(d_{\text {head}}\) is the output dim of each head.
  • This just says the total output dim for \(n\) heads is the same as if we did one head.
  • Means there is something important about splitting up the heads.

Rule 3. Aspect ratio = \(\frac {d_{\text {model}}}{n_{\text {layer}}} \approx 100-200\)

  • Pipeline dependent: if network speed is fast than parallelizing is easier and shallow networks are better.
  • If poor network speed then pipeline parallel might be more viable and deeper networks would be more parallelizable.

Rule 4. Vocab size

  • For single language models, vocab size is typically \(30-50k\)
  • For multi language models, vocab size is typically \(100-250k\)

Stabilizing Softmax in Attention [7TMH]

  • July 16, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

The softmax is ill-behaved due to the exponentials. In the transformer, we have two softmaxes: one at the end and one in the attention.

  • For the first, note \[\log \sigma (\mathbf {x})_i = \log (x_i) - \log \underbrace {\sum _{j=1}^{d} e^{x_j}}_{D(\mathbf {x})}\] The only problem is the denominator term. The idea is to enforce the \(D(\mathbf {x}) = 1\) by regularizing via a penalty on \(\log D(\mathbf {x})\) ie. \[\mathcal {L}_{\text {aux}} = 10^{-4} \log ^2(D(\mathbf {x}))\] With the \(\nabla D(\mathbf {x})\) should be \(0\) and we are effectively only considering the non-exponential term.
  • For the attention, they primarily operate on the logits prior to the softmax. One way is via layer norm before the softmax. Another is via Softcapping

Multi Query Attention [W4YP]

  • July 14, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Notes on the paper Fast Transformer Decoding: One Write-Head is All You Need.

For,

  • \(b\) batch dimension,
  • \(n\) sequence length,
  • \(d\) hidden dimension,
  • \(h\) number of heads.
The total FLOPS for attention are roughly \(bnd^2\). This comes from
  1. \(3nd^2\) FLOPS to compute \(Q = XW_Q, K = XW_K, V = XW_V\).
  2. Need to do this \(b\) times for each input in batch.
and the total memory accesses are roughly \(\underbrace {bnd}_{X} + \underbrace {bhn^2}_{\text {softmax}} + \underbrace {d^2}_{\text {projection}}\). This gives high arithmetic intensity \[O\left (\left (\frac {h}{d} + \frac {1}{bn}\right )^{-1}\right )\]

However, if we do incremental inference then we must multiply our total number of memory accesses by \(n\) i.e. \(bn^2d + nd^2\). This gives arithmetic intensity \[O\left (\left (\frac {n}{d} + \frac {1}{b}\right )^{-1}\right ),\] which requires large batch and short sequence length.

Remark. I believe we just ignore the softmax memory contribution in the inference case because it does not scale with \(n\) anymore as we are computing the logits just for the next token. Therefore, it becomes a \(\frac {d}{h}\) term, which we can safely ignore?

The key is that the \(\frac {n}{d}\) term comes from the \(bn^2d\) term, where we are loading \(h\) \((b \times n \times d / h)\) \(K\) and \(V\) matrices \(n\) times. Thus, we can improve the \(\frac {n}{d}\) term by a factor of \(h\) by simply not using a different \(K\) and \(V\) matrix for each head. This is the entire idea behind MQA.

Remark. Similar to the KV-cache, we still can use \(h\) \(Q\) matrices because we do not need to load \(n\) of them into memory because only the last one matters for inference.

Group Query Attention [XUVV]

  • July 14, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Group query attention is the same idea as MQA but instead of using one \(K\) and \(V\) for every head, they use a group of them. Where obviously the number of groups needs to be less than the number of heads.

Also inspired by this idea of reducing the dependence of \(K\) and \(V\) on the sequence length is sliding window attention.

Sliding Window Attention [WM3M]

  • July 14, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

The idea behind sliding window attention is to ensure the number of weight matrices needed for the keys \(K\) and values \(V\) does not scale with the sequence length. Intuitively, this means allowing each word to "attend" to only some fixed number of previous words rather than the whole sequence

Notebook: Three Easy Pieces [0084]

  • July 1, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Part of my revisiting CS fundamentals journey

Processes [SE66]

  • August 16, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

The process abstraction, the syscalls for working with processes on Unix, the hardware-software contract that makes time-sharing safe, and the scheduling policies that decide who runs when.

The abstraction

  • August 16, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Process [FCHM]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

A running program, plus all the state the OS needs to pause it and resume it later. A process consists of:

  1. Memory: an Address space holding the program's code, stack, and heap.
  2. Registers: general-purpose registers carrying current computation, plus special-purpose registers:
    • Program counter (PC): the next instruction to run.
    • Stack pointer and frame pointer: where the process sits on its stack.

A process moves between three states:

  • running: currently on a CPU.
  • ready: runnable, waiting for the Scheduling to give it a CPU.
  • blocked: waiting on I/O or another event, not runnable.
Remark. Threads (see Concurrency Intro) share an address space within a process but each have their own PC, register set, and stack.

Process API [JJ45]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

The minimum operations any OS must expose for working with Processes:

  1. Create: load a program from disk, set up its address space (stack, heap, code), and jump to main. The OS also initializes argc/argv.
  2. Destroy: kill a misbehaving or unwanted process.
  3. Wait: block until another process completes — useful for ordering dependent work.
  4. Miscellaneous control: e.g. suspend / resume.
  5. Status: report on running processes.
Remark. The stack is allocated up front. The heap starts small and grows via malloc, which calls into the OS (brk/sbrk) when it runs out of headroom.

On Unix, the concrete realization of this API uses fork(), wait(), and exec() — covered separately.

Process control block [JE53]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

The kernel-side data structure holding all per-process state. There is one PCB per Process, threaded together into the process list. The PCB is what the OS reads and writes during a Context switch: the saved register set lives here, as does the process state, the open files, the parent pointer, and so on.

An example from xv6:

// the registers xv6 will save and restore
// to stop and subsequently restart a process
struct context {
  int eip;
  int esp;
  int ebx;
  int ecx;
  int edx;
  int esi;
  int edi;
  int ebp;
};
// the different states a process can be in
enum proc_state { UNUSED, EMBRYO, SLEEPING, RUNNABLE, RUNNING, ZOMBIE };
// the information xv6 tracks about each process
// including its register context and state
struct proc {
  char *mem;                 // Start of process memory
  uint sz;                   // Size of process memory
  char *kstack;              // Bottom of kernel stack for this process
  enum proc_state state;     // Process state
  int pid;                   // Process ID
  struct proc *parent;       // Parent process
  void *chan;                // If non-zero, sleeping on chan
  int killed;                // If non-zero, have been killed
  struct file *ofile[NOFILE]; // Open files
  struct inode *cwd;         // Current directory
  struct context context;    // Switch here to run process
  struct trapframe *tf;      // Trap frame for the current interrupt
};
Remark. Threads need an analogous thread control block; see Concurrency Intro.

Unix process syscalls

  • August 16, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

fork() [O1S9]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

The Unix system call that duplicates the calling Process. After fork() returns, two processes exist where there was one. The child gets its own copy of:

  1. Address space.
  2. General-purpose registers.
  3. Program counter — the child returns from fork() at the same instruction the parent does.

The two processes are distinguished by fork()'s return value:

int main()
  int rc = fork();
  if (rc < 0)
    printf("error with fork");
  else if (rc == 0)
    printf("in child");
  else
    printf("in parent");        // rc holds child PID
Remark. Which process is scheduled first after fork() is non-deterministic. Use wait() if you need ordering.
Remark. The copy is logical, not physical: real implementations use Copy-on-write so the duplicated address space only allocates fresh pages when one side writes to them.

wait() [J6VZ]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

Blocks the calling Process until one of its children terminates. The OS marks the child's PCB ZOMBIE on exit, keeping enough state (exit status, resource usage) for the parent to retrieve via wait(); only when the parent collects it is the child fully reaped.

wait() returns the PID of the child that died. Variant waitpid(pid, ...) blocks on a specific child — useful when a parent has multiple children and only one is interesting.

Used to enforce ordering between parent and child code paths after fork(), or to know when an exec()'d program finishes.

Remark. Calling wait() with no children to wait on returns -1.

exec() [YHJR]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

Replaces the calling Process's program with a new executable. exec() does not return on success — the original code, data, stack, and heap are discarded and the new program runs in their place. The PID is unchanged.

What exec() does internally:

  1. Loads the new executable's code and static data, overwriting the current.
  2. Re-initializes stack and heap.
  3. Jumps to the new program's entry point with arguments passed as argv.

The canonical Unix pattern is fork() then exec() — the shell uses this to launch a command without becoming that command:

int main()
  while (true) {
    command = getcommand();
    int rc = fork();
    if (rc < 0)
      printf("error with fork");
    else if (rc == 0)
      exec(command);            // child becomes the command
    else
      int rc = wait(NULL);      // parent waits for it
  }

Redirection is implemented by opening a file in the child between fork() and exec(), attached to STDOUT_FILENO:

if (rc == 0)
  close(STDOUT_FILENO);
  open("output.txt");
  exec(command);
Remark. The many variants — execl, execle, execlp, execv, execvp, execvP — differ in how they take arguments (list vs. vector), whether they use the PATH environment variable, and whether they take an explicit environment.

Unix signals [9V3H]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

An asynchronous notification delivered to a Process. A process sends a signal with kill(pid, signum) (the name is misleading — most signals don't kill); the recipient can install a handler with signal(signum, handler) to "catch" it, otherwise the default action runs (often termination).

Signals are the OS's mechanism for events the recipient didn't ask to be told about: SIGINT from Ctrl-C, SIGSEGV from a Page fault on an invalid page, SIGCHLD when a child exits, SIGTERM from an administrative kill.

Who can signal whom depends on the user owning the process — a user can signal their own processes; the superuser can signal any process.

Limited Direct Execution [VE8C]

  • July 19, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

The OS lets user code run directly on the CPU for speed, but with hardware-enforced limits that prevent a buggy or malicious program from compromising the rest of the system. Two questions motivate the mechanism:

Question.
  1. How do we make sure a program doesn't do what we don't want it to do?
  2. How do we enable time-sharing?

The first question is answered by hardware User mode vs kernel mode enforced via the Trap table, with a controlled Syscall mechanism as the only path from one to the other. The second is answered by periodic timer interrupts that trap to the OS, which performs a Context switch and runs the scheduler.

The whole stack is bootstrapped during the Boot sequence, when the kernel installs the trap table and starts the timer before handing control to user space.

User mode vs kernel mode [QUQC]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

Two privilege levels supported by the CPU. User mode restricts the running process to a limited set of instructions and a limited view of memory — usually just its own address space. Kernel mode runs the OS with full access to all instructions and all of physical memory.

Switching between modes is mediated by hardware via a Trap table populated at boot. The OS sets up the table; the CPU enforces it. A process cannot promote itself to kernel mode arbitrarily — it must invoke a Syscall mechanism, which traps to a fixed entry point in the OS.

Remark. Kernel mode is dangerous because the address space is effectively the entire machine. The mode bit + the trap table together are what makes it safe to time-share a CPU between mutually distrusting programs.

Trap table [0NVY]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

A hardware-readable table installed by the OS at boot that maps each kind of exceptional control-flow event (syscall, interrupt, page fault, illegal instruction, ...) to the address of an OS handler. When the CPU takes a trap, it consults this table to decide where to jump, switches into kernel mode, and runs the handler.

The table is what makes it safe to expose privileged operations to user mode: user code never gets to pick the destination of a mode-switch, only the trap number. The OS controls every entry point, the CPU enforces the indirection.

Remark. Installing the trap table is itself a privileged operation. It happens once, early in boot, before any user process exists.

Syscall mechanism [A5S6]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

A syscall is a user-mode-initiated request for a privileged service from the kernel. Because user code cannot jump into kernel code directly, the mechanism is built around a single trap instruction plus a pre-agreed register convention identifying which service to perform.

The end-to-end sequence:

  1. User code places the syscall number in a pre-defined register and invokes the trap instruction.
  2. Hardware saves enough state to resume the process and switches to kernel mode.
  3. Hardware jumps to the OS-installed handler via the Trap table.
  4. The handler reads the syscall number from the agreed register and dispatches to the corresponding OS routine.
  5. The OS validates the request, performs the work, and arranges return values per the calling convention.
  6. The OS restores user state and returns the process to user mode.
Remark. Because the register convention is fixed by ABI, the user-mode side of a syscall is typically hand-written in assembly within libc.
Remark. Interrupts work via a similar trap-handler mechanism, but are initiated by hardware (timer, device) rather than user code.
Remark. The kernel must validate any pointers or arguments the user supplies — user code cannot be trusted not to ask the kernel to read kernel memory on its behalf.

Context switch [OKCI]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

The operation of saving the full execution state of one process (or thread) and restoring another's so the CPU can resume it as if it had never been interrupted. On a trap into the kernel, the hardware stashes the user-visible state (PC, condition codes, stack pointer) into a known location, and the OS saves the remaining general-purpose registers as part of the switching code.

Two flavors of state are involved:

  1. User registers: the current program's context (general-purpose registers, stack vars).
  2. Kernel registers: meta-information used to resume the trap correctly (PC at trap, syscall number, etc.).

Context switches are not free — they incur the cost of cache and TLB pollution in addition to the direct cost of saving/restoring state. This is the cost amortized by larger scheduling quanta and a recurring concern for any Concurrency system.

Boot sequence [XSFO]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

The fixed series of steps a machine performs from power-on until a usable operating system is running. Each stage runs from a known location and hands off control to the next.

  1. Power signal resets the CPU to a known state.
  2. The CPU fetches from a hard-wired address — the BIOS (or UEFI firmware). The BIOS runs basic hardware checks (POST).
  3. The BIOS loads and jumps to the bootloader (e.g. GRUB) from a known location on disk.
  4. The bootloader places the kernel image at the correct address and jumps to its entry point.
  5. The kernel performs early setup: installs the Trap table (a special privileged instruction), starts the timer interrupt, sets up memory management, and finally enters user space.
Remark. The hardware is hard-wired only to find the BIOS. Everything past that is software the user can replace, which is why dual-booting and custom bootloaders work.

Scheduling [B8GC]

  • July 26, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

The scheduler decides which runnable Process to put on the CPU at each moment. There is no single right answer because the metrics conflict:

  1. Turnaround time: time from arrival to completion, \(T_{\text {completion}} - T_{\text {arrival}}\). Minimized by running short jobs first.
  2. Response time: time from arrival to first scheduling. Minimized by frequent rotation.
  3. Fairness: every job makes progress; nobody starves.

FIFO scheduling [JA6Z]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

First-in, first-out. Jobs are run to completion in the order they arrive. Trivially fair under any reasonable notion of fairness, but suffers from the convoy effect: a single long-running job at the head delays every shorter job behind it, inflating average turnaround time.
