Problem. If the first arriving job is much longer than the rest, all subsequent jobs wait at least its full duration before getting any CPU.

SJF scheduling [VJ4F]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

Shortest job first. Run the job with the smallest total work first, run-to-completion. If all jobs arrive at the same time, SJF provably minimizes average turnaround time.

Problem. Real workloads don't all arrive at \(t = 0\). A long job that arrives slightly before a short one still gets to monopolize the CPU until it finishes — SJF degenerates to FIFO scheduling under sequential arrivals.

Removing the run-to-completion constraint and allowing preemption gives STCF scheduling.

STCF scheduling [7R07]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

Shortest time-to-completion first. A preemptive refinement of SJF scheduling: whenever the scheduler is invoked, it picks the job with the least remaining work. A short job arriving mid-execution can preempt a long one.

STCF is provably optimal for minimizing average turnaround time over all preemptive schedulers.

Problem. Long jobs starve in response time. The lowest-priority work might not get a CPU slice for a very long time, which is unacceptable for interactive workloads.

Round-robin scheduling [NEL8]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

Cycle through all runnable jobs, giving each a fixed time slice — the stride, time slice, or quantum length — before preempting and moving to the next. The best you can do at minimizing response time: every job sees the CPU within at most \(N\) quanta where \(N\) is the runnable-job count.

The quantum length is a tradeoff:

  • Short quantum: better response time, more frequent context switches, worse cache locality.
  • Long quantum: amortizes context-switch cost, worse response time.

Worst-case response time for \(N\) jobs at quantum \(S\) is \(S(N-1)\).

Remark. Round-robin minimizes response time but is poor for turnaround time relative to STCF scheduling. A scheduler that wants both has to combine ideas — see Multi-level feedback queue.

Multi-level feedback queue [4WG5]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

A practical scheduler that approximates STCF scheduling's turnaround properties and Round-robin scheduling's response properties without requiring foreknowledge of job lengths. Maintain multiple priority queues; within a queue, schedule round-robin. Never service a lower-priority queue while a higher-priority queue has a runnable job.

The priority of a job is not assigned by the user — it is learned from behavior:

  • New jobs enter at the top priority.
  • Each priority level has an allotment — the total CPU time a job may consume before being demoted.
  • Once a job exhausts its allotment at a level, it drops to the next level.

Short jobs that finish quickly never leave the top queue (good turnaround). Interactive jobs that yield often stay at the top (good response time). CPU-bound jobs sink to the bottom and get scheduled when nothing better is runnable.

Problem. Gaming the allotment: a job can issue I/O just before its allotment expires to avoid demotion, since I/O time doesn't count.
Solution. Charge the job's allotment for total time at the priority level, including time spent blocked.
Problem. Starvation of low-priority queues if higher queues never empty.
Solution. Periodically reset all jobs to the top priority. Cheap and effective.

Lottery scheduling [AM8U]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

A proportional-share scheduler. Each runnable job holds some number of tickets, drawn from a fixed total. On each scheduling decision, the scheduler picks a random ticket and runs the job that owns it. Over time, a job receives CPU time proportional to its share of the tickets.

The implementation is trivial: one random number per decision, plus a sum table. No global state about historical CPU usage is needed.

Mechanisms layered on top:

  • Ticket currency — give each user a fixed number of tickets to split among their processes however they like.
  • Ticket transfer — a client process can hand tickets to a server to boost its priority while servicing the request.
  • Ticket inflation — a process can grow its share temporarily, with bounds set by trust.
Remark. The unsolved problem is how to assign tickets in the first place. The mechanism is simple; the policy is not.

A deterministic version of the same idea is Stride scheduling, which avoids the variance of random sampling at the cost of needing global state.

Stride scheduling [HCJ7]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

A deterministic proportional-share scheduler. Each job has a stride inversely proportional to its ticket count (the more tickets, the smaller the stride) and a running pass counter that accumulates its stride every time it's scheduled.

The decision is:

  1. Pick the job with the lowest pass.
  2. Run it for one quantum.
  3. Increment its pass by its stride.

A job with twice as many tickets has half the stride, so its pass grows half as fast, so it gets scheduled twice as often. Same long-run behavior as Lottery scheduling, but with zero variance.

Problem. New jobs need an initial pass value. Setting it to 0 lets them dominate; setting it to the global minimum lets them merge in cleanly but requires tracking that minimum.
Remark. Each context switch is not free. Choosing a longer time slice amortizes that cost at the price of response time.
Remark. I/O-bound jobs are commonly handled by splitting a job into its non-I/O segments and scheduling them individually — turning one long-but-mostly-blocked job into many short, runnable ones.

Memory [TQSZ]

  • August 16, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

The address-space abstraction, the translation schemes that implement it, the algorithms that manage free space within a process, and what happens when physical memory is not enough.

We are now moving on from scheduling into the wonderful world of all things memory. The starting point is the address space abstraction — each process is handed its own private virtual memory and the OS, with hardware help, manages the mapping from virtual to physical addresses.

The progression of translation schemes covered in TPE:

  1. Base and bounds — one base+bound pair per process. Simple but wasteful.
  2. Segmentation — per-region base+bound. Removes internal fragmentation, introduces external fragmentation.
  3. Paging — fixed-size translation units. Eliminates external fragmentation at the cost of a translation table.

Address space [7MH4]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

The chunk of memory the OS gives a process to use at its own discretion. Each process has its own address space, so that one process cannot read or overwrite another's program state. An address space typically contains three regions:

  1. Code: the program text.
  2. Stack: local variables, function arguments, return values.
  3. Heap: dynamically allocated memory.

Every address a process sees is virtual — not the location in physical memory. The hardware translates virtual addresses to physical ones at access time. Approaches to that translation include Base and bounds, Segmentation, and Paging.

Remark. The API the user sees is tiny: a way to get memory and a way to release it, exposed as malloc and free. The common bugs are forgetting to initialize, forgetting to free, freeing too early (dangling pointer), freeing twice (double free), and freeing memory not from malloc (invalid free).
Remark. malloc and free are library calls, not syscalls. The heap is pre-allocated; malloc extends it by invoking brk/sbrk when it runs out.
Remark. When a process dies, the OS reclaims its memory, so a bounded leak is not catastrophic — just bad practice.
Remark. You can also acquire an anonymous heap-like region directly via mmap.

Base and bounds [9MPY]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

The simplest hardware mechanism for Address space translation. The CPU has two dedicated registers per process — a base pointing to the start of the process's region in physical memory and a bound marking the end. Translation is just phys = base + virt, with a check that the result stays below bound. The unit performing this translation in hardware is the memory management unit (MMU).

The hardware must expose privileged instructions for the OS to set base and bound on a context switch, and trap on out-of-bound accesses.

Problem. To support a large address space, base-and-bounds suffers significant internal fragmentation — the unused gap between the stack and heap is allocated but wasted. With 16KB total memory and 4KB-but-mostly-empty processes, you can run 4 processes when you'd really have room for 8.

Segmentation is the natural next step — give each region (code, stack, heap) its own base and bound so the gaps don't have to be allocated.

Segmentation [REKW]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

An Address space-translation scheme that gives each logical region of a process (code, heap, stack, ...) its own base and bound. Instead of allocating a single contiguous chunk with empty space between regions, the OS allocates each segment separately and on demand. A virtual address is split into a segment number and an offset within that segment:

\[\underbrace {0 1}_{\text {segment}}\underbrace {0 1 \cdots 11}_{\text {offset}}\]

The hardware uses the segment number to index a segment table, retrieves that segment's base and bound, and adds the offset. Per-segment protection bits make read-only code and no-execute data straightforward.

Segmentation also enables memory sharing — two processes can map the same physical segment (e.g. shared code) into both their address spaces.

Remark. The stack grows downward, so the bounds check has to be direction-aware based on the segment number.
Remark. Segmentation does not solve fragmentation; it pushes it from internal (per-process) to external (per-system). Variable-sized segments leave variably-sized holes in physical memory. The fix that eventually won is Paging.

Paging [SB61]

  • July 31, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Instead of allocating memory in variable-sized chunks (which fragments badly), paging divides virtual and physical memory into fixed-size pages and maps virtual pages to physical pages independently. This is the address-translation scheme that won.

The mechanism rests on three concepts:

  1. The Page table — per-process map from virtual page number to physical page number.
  2. The Translation lookaside buffer — small hardware cache that absorbs the cost of consulting the page table on every access.
  3. The Multi-level page table — the page table is itself paged, so processes only pay for the parts of the address space they use.

When a process touches a virtual address whose page isn't in physical memory, the page table's present bit is unset; the hardware traps to the OS as a page fault, and the OS retrieves the page from swap. The eviction policies that decide what to swap out are covered by Page replacement.

The two-level decomposition did not arrive fully formed. The Segmented page tables design tried to give each named segment of the address space its own variable-sized page table; the fragmentation that produced is what motivated the move to fixed-size paged page tables.

Page table [WALE]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

The per-process data structure that maps each virtual page number (VPN) to a physical page number (PPN). A virtual address is split into a VPN and an offset within the page; the page table translates the VPN, the offset is appended unchanged.

\[\underbrace {01}_{\text {VPN}}\underbrace {110\cdots 01}_{\text {offset}}\]

Each page-table entry (PTE) carries more than just a PPN:

  1. Valid bit: whether this VPN has memory allocated. Gaps between stack and heap need not be allocated.
  2. Protection bits: read/write/execute permissions.
  3. Present bit: whether the page currently resides in physical memory (vs. swap).
  4. Dirty bit: has the page been modified since being paged in.
  5. Reference bit: has the page been accessed (used by eviction policy).
Problem. The table gets very large. For a 32-bit address space with 4KB pages and 4-byte PTEs, a linear page table costs \(2^{32}/2^{12} \cdot 4 = 2^{22}\) bytes — 4MB per process.
Problem. Translation requires accessing the table in memory, which is slow on every memory reference. Mitigated by the Translation lookaside buffer.

The size problem is mitigated by Multi-level page table, which only allocates the parts of the table that are actually used.

Translation lookaside buffer [FGOK]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

A small hardware cache of recent virtual-to-physical translations. Without it, every memory reference would incur the cost of walking the Page table in memory.

An access proceeds as:

  1. Extract VPN from the virtual address.
  2. Check whether the VPN is in the TLB.
  3. TLB hit: read PPN from the entry, append offset, access memory.
  4. TLB miss: walk the page table (in hardware on CISC, by trapping to OS on RISC), insert the translation into the TLB, retry.

A TLB entry holds the VPN, the PPN, a valid bit (separate from the PTE valid bit — invalid on init, eviction, context switch), and protection bits.

Problem. Each process has different translations for the same VPN, so a naive TLB must be flushed on every context switch.
Solution. Add an address space identifier (ASID) to each TLB entry and an ASID register set on context switch. Now entries from different processes coexist.

Definition. Culler's Law [P9UM]

  • July 31, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

"Ram is not always ram". Referring to the fact that a physical memory access takes a substantial different amount of time depending on whether it hit in the TLB.

Remark. In real software-managed TLBs, an entry has global bits for shared pages, an 8-bit ASID, coherence bits, and a wired register reserving slots for code that cannot afford a TLB miss (e.g. the TLB-miss handler itself).
Remark. For very fast caches, even a TLB access is a bottleneck. Virtually indexed caches sidestep the TLB on the index path.

Definition. Segmented page tables [HBFS]

  • May 13, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

An early translation scheme that splits the virtual address space into a small number of named segments (code, data, stack, heap, ...) and gives each segment its own Page table sized only to the range of addresses that segment actually uses. The virtual address is interpreted as a triple: segment number, page number within the segment, and offset within the page.

Translation is a two-step walk. The segment number indexes a segment table, which yields the base and length of that segment's page table. The page number then indexes that page table to find the physical frame, and the offset is appended. Empty or unused segments need no page table at all, so a process pays for memory it actually uses — the same motivation that drives every non-flat translation scheme.

The mechanism has appealing side benefits. Each segment carries its own protection bits, so the architecture can enforce no-execute on the stack or read-only on the code segment at the segment-table level without per-page complexity. Translation overhead is bounded by the segment count, which is typically very small.

The problems are what killed it. Page tables are now variable-sized objects that still have to live in contiguous physical memory. The stack segment's page table might be small; the heap's might be large and grow over time. Allocating, growing, and freeing variably-sized tables in kernel memory produces external fragmentation — exactly the headache pure segmentation has at the user level, now pushed down a layer. If the heap outgrows its current page table, the kernel has to allocate a larger contiguous chunk and copy.

Secondary issues compound. Translation costs two memory references (segment table, then page table) before reaching data, which hurts without a Translation lookaside buffer. The segment count is fixed by the architecture, which is awkward for programs whose layout does not fit "code/data/stack/heap" — a sprawling memory-mapped region or a JIT wanting many small protection domains has nowhere to go. And segmentation tends to leak into the programming model: x86 carried segment registers around for decades after they had stopped being useful.

The fix that won is the Multi-level page table — page the page table itself, in fixed-size chunks, and recover the "only pay for what you use" property without variable-sized contiguous allocations. Every modern architecture descends from that decision.

Multi-level page table [VX1M]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

A page table that is itself paged: instead of one linear array of PTEs, the table is split into page-sized chunks indexed by a top-level page directory. Subtrees of the address space that are entirely unused get a single null entry at the top level — no second-level page is allocated for them. This is the design that displaced Segmented page tables — it preserves the "don't pay for empty regions" property while keeping every piece of the structure page-sized, eliminating the variable-allocation fragmentation that doomed the segmented version.

Advantages over a linear Page table:

  1. Fits easily in memory — only allocate a second-level page when an entry in it is used.
  2. Memory cost is proportional to the amount of address space actually in use.
Problem. On a Translation lookaside buffer miss, the walk now costs two memory loads — page directory entry first, then the PTE.
Remark. Real architectures push this further (x86-64 uses 4 or 5 levels) and lean on the TLB to absorb the walk cost.

Page replacement [HK73]

  • August 8, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Once swap space is in play, the OS must decide which pages to evict from physical memory. The mechanism is the Page fault machinery; the question of policy is what this hub covers.

Page fault [CXFM]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

Physical memory is finite, so the OS reserves a region of disk — swap space — to hold pages that don't fit. Pages currently on disk have their present bit cleared in the Page table entry; the PTE instead holds a disk address.

When a process touches a virtual address whose page is not present, the hardware traps to the OS. This is a page fault. The handler:

  1. Looks at the PTE: if invalid, the access is illegal — terminate the process.
  2. If valid but not present: read the page in from swap, update the PTE, retry the access.
Remark. The hardware never handles page faults itself — that would require it to understand disk I/O and the OS's swap layout. Page-fault handling is always in software.

The three cases on every memory access:

  1. Present + valid: hardware reads the PPN from the PTE and proceeds.
  2. Not present + valid: hardware invokes the page-fault handler to retrieve from swap.
  3. Not valid: hardware raises an exception; the OS typically kills the process.
Example. What happens when you dereference a nullptr? The TLB misses on VPN 0, the page-table walk finds the PTE marked invalid, and the hardware traps to the OS — which delivers SIGSEGV.

LRU and the clock algorithm [7WG2]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

Least recently used is the standard heuristic for picking a page to evict: throw out the one that has been untouched the longest. It works because of the principle of locality — recently-used pages are likely to be used again soon (temporal locality).

Pure LRU requires updating a data structure on every memory access, which is too expensive. The clock algorithm approximates it cheaply:

  1. Maintain a reference bit per page, set by hardware on access.
  2. Loop a pointer around the pages. If the reference bit is 1, clear it and advance.
  3. If the reference bit is 0, evict that page.
Remark. LRU is not optimal. The pathological case is the looping workload: if the working set is one page larger than the cache, LRU misses 100% of the time.

The quality of any replacement policy is measured by its average memory access time:

\[AMAT = (1 - P_{miss}) T_P + P_{miss} T_D\]

where \(T_P\) is physical-memory access time and \(T_D\) is disk access time. The disk gap is so large that even tiny miss rates dominate AMAT.

Belady's anomaly [LQV3]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

A desirable property of a page-replacement policy is the stack property: increasing the cache size never makes the miss rate worse. Surprisingly, not every reasonable-looking policy has it.

FIFO eviction violates the stack property — on certain access sequences, growing the cache strictly increases the number of misses. This is Belady's anomaly. It is what motivates LRU and the clock algorithm-style policies, which do have the stack property because they only depend on relative access recency.

The optimal eviction policy (offline) is to evict the page whose next use is farthest in the future. Unimplementable in practice (you don't know the future), but useful as a theoretical upper bound to compare against.

Two refinements make eviction practical on real workloads. A pure global policy lets one greedy process hog memory, which Segmented FIFO fixes with a per-process resident-set cap; and writing victims back one page at a time wastes disk bandwidth, which Page clustering amortizes by batching the dirty list.

Segmented FIFO [HF6H]

  • July 11, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

A process-aware refinement of LRU and the clock algorithm. Pure FIFO is a global policy — it does not distinguish memory by owner, so one greedy process can evict everyone else's pages. Segmented FIFO instead maintains a per-process FIFO queue capped at that process's resident set size, bounding how much physical memory any single process can hold.

Evicted pages are not discarded outright; they move to one of two secondary lists:

  1. Clean page free list — evicted pages that were never modified.
  2. Dirty page list — evicted pages that must be written back before reuse.

The secondary lists act as a victim cache: if a process faults on a page still sitting on the dirty list, it can be reclaimed without going to disk. The resident-set cap gives fairness; the secondary lists soften the cost of capping it too aggressively.

Copy-on-write [DEJB]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

An optimization where two address spaces share the same physical page until one writes to it. Both processes have PTEs pointing at the same PPN, but with the write bit cleared. The first write traps to the OS, which allocates a fresh page, copies the contents, points the writing process's PTE at the new page (with writes re-enabled), and resumes.

Used pervasively:

  • fork() — the child shares the parent's pages until either modifies one. Avoids the cost of copying an entire address space the child often immediately overwrites with exec().
  • Shared libraries — many processes map the same library text pages.
  • Kernel mapped into every process — same physical kernel code seen from every address space.

On-demand zeroing [PW7Q]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

When a process asks for new heap memory, the OS must guarantee the returned pages contain zeros (to avoid leaking other processes' data). The naive implementation zeroes the page at allocation time. On-demand zeroing defers that work to first touch: until the process actually accesses a freshly-allocated page, no zero-fill happens.

The OS marks the page as not-present in the Page table. The first access traps as a Page fault, the handler zeroes a physical page, installs it in the PTE, and resumes. Heap memory that is allocated but never used costs nothing.

Page clustering [YMHF]

  • July 11, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

An amortization trick for the write side of page replacement. Writing one dirty page to swap at a time pays a full disk I/O per eviction, and small random writes are the worst case for a disk. Page clustering batches them: the OS collects a group of dirty pages from the global dirty list and writes them out together in a single larger, more sequential I/O. The per-page cost of paging out falls because the fixed overhead of a disk write is spread across the whole batch.

Remark. A common convention is to map the kernel into every process's address space (via Copy-on-write) so kernel code and structures appear at the same virtual addresses everywhere.

Free-space management [67X5]

  • July 29, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Once we have an Address space, we still have to actually allocate physical bytes within it. Free-space management is the discipline of tracking which bytes are free, picking which free chunk to satisfy a request, and merging adjacent freed chunks back together. Two fragmentation hazards to watch:

  1. External fragmentation: many small unallocated non-contiguous chunks.
  2. Internal fragmentation: allocated chunks larger than what the requester uses.

Simplifying assumptions for the algorithms below: allocations are immovable once placed, total memory is fixed, and the user does not specify a size in free. The last assumption is handled by storing the size in a header preceding the returned pointer.

Malloc placement policies [1ABF]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

Given a free list of unallocated memory chunks and a request of size \(n\), which chunk do you carve the allocation out of? Different answers trade off fragmentation against search time.

  1. Best fit: find the smallest chunk \(\geq n\). Minimizes wasted space per allocation but tends to leave many small unusable fragments behind, and requires scanning the whole list.
  2. Worst fit: find the largest chunk. Aims to leave usefully-sized leftovers but also requires a full scan.
  3. First fit: take the first chunk that fits. Fast (early exit) but biases small fragments toward the start of the list.
  4. Next fit: like first fit, but resume the search from where the previous allocation ended. Spreads small fragments more evenly.

On free, the allocator searches the list for adjacent free chunks and coalesces them into a larger chunk. The size of each allocation is stored in a header immediately preceding the returned pointer so free doesn't need a size argument.

Segregated lists [FB66]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

A data-structure-level optimization for allocators that maintains separate free lists per common object size. Instead of one global free list scanned by Malloc placement policies, you keep a list per size class. Allocations of that size pop straight off the matching list — no search.

  • Grow a per-size list by requesting larger segments from the main allocator and chopping them up.
  • Drain back to the main list when no references remain.
  • Optionally leave objects pre-initialized on the list to skip constructor/destructor calls.
Remark. This is the structure behind kernel slab allocators and many object-pool patterns. It works when the workload allocates many objects of a few common sizes.

Buddy allocation [8M72]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

An allocator for memory of total size \(2^n\) that maintains a binary-tree of free blocks at every power-of-two size. To service a request, walk down to the smallest block \(\geq \) requested size; if no block of that size exists, split a larger one (and its "buddy" becomes free). On free, check whether the freed block's buddy is also free, and if so, coalesce upward — recursively.

The key trick is that buddies differ by exactly one bit in their address at each level. To check whether a block's buddy is free, flip that bit and consult a bitmap. To coalesce upward, mask off the bit and recurse.

Remark. Internal fragmentation is bounded by 2× the request size (you may have to round up to the next power of two), but external fragmentation is essentially eliminated within the buddy region.

Concurrency [5P43]

  • August 16, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Threads, the hazards that come with sharing state between them, and the primitives that make sharing safe.

Threads and shared state

  • August 16, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Concurrency Intro [16TG]

  • August 16, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Concurrency comes from sharing: multiple Threads within a single process share the address space, which is what makes them cheap to spawn and to communicate between, and is also what makes them dangerous. When threads interleave on a shared variable, you get a Race condition. The fragile region is the Critical section, protected by synchronization primitives like Locks and Barriers.

Thread [63U3]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

An independent unit of execution within a Process. Each thread has its own program counter, register set, and stack — but shares the surrounding address space, file descriptors, and other process-level resources with its siblings.

To support a context switch between threads, the OS keeps a thread control block (TCB) per thread — the per-thread analog of Process control block. Each thread also gets its own stack within the process's address space.

Why threads instead of processes:

  1. Intra-process parallelism: parallelizable work within one program, without paying the context switch cost of separate processes.
  2. Avoiding blocking I/O: one thread blocks waiting on I/O while others keep running.
Remark. Sharing an address space is exactly what makes threads cheap to communicate between and exactly what makes them dangerous — see Race condition and Critical section.

Race condition [VOQN]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

When the correctness of a program depends on the order in which concurrent Threads schedule, you have a race condition. The classic example: two threads each execute

mov addr reg     // load
add 1 reg        // increment
mov reg addr     // store

intending to increment a shared variable twice. If the OS interleaves the threads at the wrong instruction boundary:

  1. Thread 1 stores its incremented value.
  2. Thread 2 stores its incremented value.
  3. Thread 1 increments (in its register).
  4. Thread 2 increments (in its register).

The result is a single increment, not two — both threads loaded the same starting value and the second store clobbered the first.

The fix is to prevent interleaving inside the code region that touches shared state — the Critical section — using a synchronization primitive like Locks.

Critical section [KG2K]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

A region of code that accesses shared state in a way that is incorrect if more than one Thread executes it concurrently. Critical sections are exactly the regions that need protection by a synchronization primitive: a Locks excludes other threads while one is inside; a Barriers forces all threads to reach the same point before any may continue.

The art of writing correct concurrent code is to identify critical sections and protect them — without making them so large that you lose all parallelism.

Synchronization primitives

  • August 16, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Locks [Q2DT]

  • March 9, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

A lock is a synchronization primitive that grants mutually exclusive access to a critical section. The implementations below trade off coherence traffic, fairness, and memory in different ways. The naive locks (Test-and-set lock and Test-and-test-and-set lock) are simple but generate \(O(n)\) coherence traffic per release; queue-based locks (Ticket lock, Array-based lock, MCS lock) scale better at the cost of extra state per waiter.

Test-and-set lock [MJ5L]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

The simplest spin lock. The acquire loop spins on an atomic test_and_set: it writes LOCKED and reads the previous value in one step. If the previous value was UNLOCKED, the caller has the lock; otherwise it tries again.

acquire(lock_ptr):
  while (true):
    // Perform "test-and-set"
    // UNLOCKED = 0, LOCKED = 1
    test_and_set(old, lock_ptr)
    if (old == UNLOCKED):
      break // lock acquired!
    // keep spinning, back to top of while loop

release(lock_ptr):
  store[lock_ptr] <- UNLOCKED
Problem. Major coherence traffic. Every spinning core must acquire write permissions and invalidate everyone else on each test_and_set, so contention scales badly with the number of waiters.

Test-and-test-and-set lock [PXY9]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

A refinement of the Test-and-set lock that reduces coherence traffic during the spin. Threads first perform a plain load to check whether the lock looks free, and only attempt the atomic test_and_set when it does. Spinning is then read-only, so cores can share the cache line.

acquire(lock_ptr):
  while (true):
    // Perform "test"
    load [lock_ptr] -> original_value
    if (original_value == UNLOCKED):
      // Perform "test-and-set"
      test_and_set(old, lock_ptr)
      if (old == UNLOCKED):
        break // lock acquired!
    // keep spinning, back to top of while loop

release(lock_ptr):
  store[lock_ptr] <- UNLOCKED
Remark. Spinning is a read-only operation now.
Problem. When the lock is released, the line must be invalidated on every waiter, and they all simultaneously read and try to write. The contention is shifted from steady state to release time but is still \(O(n)\) in the number of waiters.

Ticket lock [V7KP]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

A fair spin lock based on two counters. Each arriving thread takes a "ticket" by atomically incrementing next_ticket and then spins until now_serving equals its ticket. Release simply increments now_serving. Threads acquire the lock in FIFO order, which prevents starvation but does not by itself reduce contention.

acquire(lock_ptr):
  my_ticket = fetch_and_increment(lock_ptr->next_ticket)
  while (lock_ptr->now_serving != my_ticket); // spin

release(lock_ptr):
  lock_ptr->now_serving = lock_ptr->now_serving + 1
Problem. Still \(O(n)\) coherence traffic on release: incrementing now_serving invalidates the line on every waiter, and they all reload it to recheck their ticket.

Array-based lock [BJ7N]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

Each waiter is assigned its own slot in an array and spins only on that slot. Release just flips the next slot to "go-ahead", so the cache line touched by release is the one read by exactly one other thread. Coherence traffic per release is constant rather than \(O(n)\), at the cost of one slot per waiter.

  • Give each waiter its own "slot" to wait on.
  • Only need to set the next slot to "go-ahead" on release.
  • Constant coherence traffic.
Problem. \(O(n)\) memory for the lock array, and padding each slot to \(64\)b (one cache line) to avoid false sharing makes that cost real.

MCS lock [IN64]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

A scalable, list-based queue lock. The lock variable is a pointer to the tail of a linked list of per-thread nodes. Each thread enqueues its own node and spins on a flag in its own cache line. The releaser wakes only its direct successor, so coherence traffic per handoff is constant and memory cost is proportional only to threads currently waiting, not to the lock's fanout.

acquire(lock):
  I->next = null
  predecessor = fetch_and_store(lock, I)
  if predecessor != nil:                   // some node holds the lock
    I->must_wait = true
    predecessor->next = I                  // predecessor must wake us
    repeat while I->must_wait              // spin till lock is free

release(lock):
  if (I->next == null):                    // no known successor
    if compare_and_swap(lock, I, nil):     // make sure...
      return                               // CAS succeeded; lock freed
    repeat while I->next == nil            // spin to learn successor
  I->next->must_wait = false               // wake successor

Barriers [9QUX]

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

A barrier holds threads at a synchronization point until all \(P\) participants have arrived, then releases them simultaneously. The naive implementation uses a shared counter that each arriving thread decrements; the last arrival resets the counter and the rest spin until it returns to \(P\).

Naive central barrier

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios
global (shared) count : integer := P

procedure central_barrier
  if fetch_and_decrement(&count) == 1
    count := P
  else
    repeat until count == P
Problem. When count == P, a fast thread can reach the next barrier and decrement count again before the slow threads observe the reset. The slow threads then spin forever waiting for a value that has already passed.

Sense-reversing barrier

  • May 12, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

The fix is to give each thread a private local_sense that flips on each barrier. The global sense flag is flipped by the last arrival, and waiters spin on sense == local_sense, so a fresh barrier cannot be confused with a stale one.

global (shared) count : integer := P
global (shared) sense : bool := true
global (private) local_sense : bool := false

procedure central_barrier
  local_sense = !local_sense
  if fetch_and_decrement(&count) == 1
    count := P
    sense = local_sense
  else
    repeat until sense == local_sense

Notebook: Information Theory [004B]

  • February 1, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Definitions

  • February 1, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Definition. Entropy [0081]

  • Kellen Kanarios

We define the entropy of a random variable \(X \sim p_{X}\), where \(p_{X} : \mathcal {X} \to [0, 1]\) as \[H(X) = -\sum _{x \in |\mathcal {X}|} p_{X}(x) \log p_{X}(x)\]

Convexity and the fundamental inequalities

  • February 1, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Definition. Convex set [004C]

  • February 1, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

\(C\) is said to be convex if for any \(x_1, x_2 \in C\) and \(\lambda \in [0,1]\) then \(\lambda x_1 + (1 - \lambda )x_2 \in C\)

Definition. Convex Function [004D]

  • February 1, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

A function \(f : C \to \mathbb {R}\) is said to be convex if \(C\) is convex, and for any \(x_1, x_2 \in C\), \(\lambda \in [0,1]\), we have \[f(\lambda x_1 + (1 - \lambda )x_2) \leq \lambda f(x_1) + (1 - \lambda )f(x_2)\] and strictly convex if the inequality is strict.

Theorem. First-order condition [0034]

  • January 26, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

If \(f\) is first-order continuously differentiable, then \(f\) is convex iff \[f(\boldsymbol {y}) \geq f(\boldsymbol {x}) + \langle \nabla f(\boldsymbol {x}), \boldsymbol {y} - \boldsymbol {x} \rangle \]

Remark. This can be thought of as the tangent is always an underestimate of a convex function.

Theorem. Second-order condition [0035]

  • January 26, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

If \(f: \mathbb {R}^n \to \mathbb {R}\) is twice-differentiable, then \(f\) is convex if and only if \(\nabla ^2 f(\boldsymbol {x}) \succeq 0\).

Remark. This implies positive curvature or the common "bowl-shaped" interpretation.

Theorem. Jensen's Inequality [002G]

  • December 29, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

If \(\phi \) is a convex function on the range of \(X\) then \[\phi (\mathbb {E}[X]) \leq \mathbb {E}[\phi (X)]\]

Remark. Holds with equality if and only if
  1. \(f\) is linear on \(\mathcal {X}\)
  2. \(X\) is a constant almost surely.

Theorem. Gibb's inequality [004E]

  • February 1, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

\[D_{\mathrm {KL}}(P || Q) \geq 0\]

Proof. Note that \[ \begin {align*} D_{\mathrm {KL}}(P || Q) &= \mathbb {E}_P \left [\log \left (\frac {P(x)}{Q(x)}\right )\right ] \\ &= \mathbb {E}_P \left [-\log \left (\frac {Q(x)}{P(x)}\right )\right ] \\ \end {align*} \] By Jensen's Inequality, \[ \begin {align*} D_{\mathrm {KL}}(P || Q) &\geq -\log \left (\mathbb {E}_P \left [\frac {Q(x)}{P(x)}\right ]\right ) = 0 \end {align*} \]
Problem. When is \(D_{\mathrm {KL}}(P || Q) = 0\)?
Answer. Need \(Q(x) / P(x)\) to be constant almost surely. This occurs only when \(Q = P\). \[ Q(x) / P(x) = c \implies Q(x) = P(x) c \iff \sum Q(x) = c \sum P(x) \implies c = 1 \]

Theorem. Mutual information is nonnegative [TB4P]

  • July 11, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

For any pair of random variables, \(I(X;Y)\geq 0\), with equality iff \(X\) and \(Y\) are independent.

This is Gibb's inequality in disguise: mutual information is the KL divergence between the joint distribution and the product of marginals, \[I(X;Y)\ =\ D_{\mathrm {KL}}\left (P_{XY}\,\|\,P_X P_Y\right )\ \geq \ 0,\] and Gibbs' inequality forces every KL divergence to be nonnegative, vanishing exactly when the two arguments coincide — i.e. when \(P_{XY}=P_XP_Y\). It is the root fact from which Conditioning reduces entropy follows.

Theorem. Conditioning reduces entropy [H7J5]

  • July 11, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

Observing another variable can only lower entropy on average: \[H(X\mid Y)\ \leq \ H(X),\] with equality iff \(X\) and \(Y\) are independent.

Immediate from Mutual information is nonnegative and the identity \(I(X;Y)=H(X)-H(X\mid Y)\): since \(I(X;Y)\geq 0\), the conditional entropy cannot exceed the unconditional one. The caveat is that this holds on average over \(Y\) — a particular outcome \(Y=y\) can raise the entropy of \(X\); it is only the expectation \(H(X\mid Y)=\mathbb {E}_Y[H(X\mid Y=y)]\) that is guaranteed to drop.

Theorem. Entropy is subadditive [C6M8]

  • July 11, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

The joint entropy of a collection never exceeds the sum of the individual entropies: \[H(X_1,\dots ,X_n)\ \leq \ \sum _{i=1}^{n} H(X_i),\] with equality iff the \(X_i\) are mutually independent.

Expand the joint entropy by the chain rule, \(H(X_1,\dots ,X_n)=\sum _i H(X_i\mid X_1,\dots ,X_{i-1})\), and apply Conditioning reduces entropy term by term: each \(H(X_i\mid X_{<i})\leq H(X_i)\). Conditioning on the past can only shrink each term, so dropping the conditioning gives an upper bound.

Theorem. The uniform distribution maximizes entropy [6FHG]

  • July 11, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

Over a finite alphabet \(\mathcal {X}\), entropy is bounded by the log of the alphabet size, \[H(X)\ \leq \ \log |\mathcal {X}|,\] with equality iff \(X\) is uniform. No distribution on \(\mathcal {X}\) is more uncertain than the uniform one.

Again a face of Gibb's inequality: measuring \(P\) against the uniform law \(U(x)=1/|\mathcal {X}|\), \[D_{\mathrm {KL}}(P\,\|\,U)\ =\ \log |\mathcal {X}| - H(X)\ \geq \ 0,\] so \(H(X)\leq \log |\mathcal {X}|\), and Gibbs' equality condition \(P=U\) pins down the maximizer. This is the discrete base case of the general maximum-entropy principle: absent constraints, the flattest distribution wins.

Log-Sum Inequality [004F]

  • February 1, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

If \(a_{i},b_{i}>0\) for \(i\in \{1,\ldots ,n\}\); \(A=\sum _{i=1}^{n}a_{i},\quad B=\sum _{i=1}^{n}b_{i}\) Then, we have: \(\sum _{i=1}^{n}a_{i}\log a_{i}/b_{i}\geq A\log A/B\)

Proof. Define \(p_i = \frac {a_i}{A}\), \(q_i = \frac {b_i}{B}\), then \[ \begin {align*} D_{\mathrm {KL}}(p || q) &= \sum \frac {a_i}{A} \log {\frac {B a_i}{A b_i}} \\ &= \sum \frac {a_i}{A} \log {\frac {a_i}{b_i}} - \sum \frac {a_i}{A} \log {\frac {A}{B}} \\ &= \sum \frac {a_i}{A} \log {\frac {a_i}{b_i}} - \log {\frac {A}{B}} \\ \end {align*} \] By Gibb's inequality, \[ \begin {align*} \sum \frac {a_i}{A} \log {\frac {a_i}{b_i}} - \log {\frac {A}{B}} \geq 0 &\implies \sum \frac {a_i}{A} \log {\frac {a_i}{b_i}} \geq \log {\frac {A}{B}} \\ &\iff A \sum a_i \log {\frac {a_i}{b_i}} \geq A \log {\frac {A}{B}} \end {align*} \] as desired.

Consequences of Log-Sum Inequality. The log-sum inequality upgrades nonnegativity to convexity.

Theorem. KL divergence is jointly convex [WV2P]

  • July 11, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

The KL divergence \(D_{\mathrm {KL}}(P\,\|\,Q)\) is convex in the pair \((P,Q)\) jointly: for any two pairs and \(\lambda \in [0,1]\), \[D_{\mathrm {KL}}\left (\lambda P_1+(1-\lambda )P_2\ \|\ \lambda Q_1+(1-\lambda )Q_2\right )\ \leq \ \lambda D_{\mathrm {KL}}(P_1\|Q_1)+(1-\lambda )D_{\mathrm {KL}}(P_2\|Q_2).\]

This drops out of the Log-Sum Inequality applied termwise. Joint convexity is the strong statement: it immediately gives individual convexity in either argument (fix the other and set the two copies equal), and — via \(D_{\mathrm {KL}}(P\|U)=\log |\mathcal {X}|-H(P)\) against the fixed uniform law — it makes Entropy is concave a corollary.

Theorem. Entropy is concave [TVGU]

  • July 11, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

Entropy is a concave functional of the distribution: for \(\lambda \in [0,1]\), \[H(\lambda P_1+(1-\lambda )P_2)\ \geq \ \lambda H(P_1)+(1-\lambda )H(P_2).\] Mixing two distributions never destroys uncertainty — the entropy of a mixture is at least the mixture of entropies.

This is the mirror image of KL divergence is jointly convex. Writing \(H(P)=\log |\mathcal {X}|-D_{\mathrm {KL}}(P\,\|\,U)\) against the uniform law \(U\), concavity of \(H\) is exactly convexity of \(P\mapsto D_{\mathrm {KL}}(P\|U)\). Concavity is what makes the maximum in The uniform distribution maximizes entropy well-behaved — a concave objective on the simplex has a unique maximizer.

Estimation and the data processing inequality

  • February 1, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Theorem. Maximum Aposteriori Estimator [004Y]

  • February 9, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

The optimal estimator \(g^* : \mathcal {Y} \to \mathcal {X}\) is \[ \begin {align*} g^*(y) = \arg \max _{x \in \mathcal {X}} P_{X | Y}(x | y) \end {align*} \]

Proof. Recall that \[ \begin {align*} P_{e}(g) &= \sum _{x}\sum _y P_{XY}(x, y) \cdot \mathbf {1}_{x \neq g(y)} \\ &= \sum _{y} P_Y(y) \sum _{x} P_{X|Y}(x|y) \mathbf {1}_{x \neq g(y)} \\ &= \sum _{y} P_Y(y) (1 - P_{X|Y}(g(y)|y)) \\ \end {align*} \] To minimize this, we take \[ \begin {align*} g^*(y) = \arg \max _{x} P_{X|Y}(x | y) \end {align*} \] as desired.

Definition. Markov chain as conditional independence [UWYD]

  • July 11, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

The information-theoretic reading of a Markov chain: three variables form a chain \(U\to V\to W\) when \(W\) depends on \(U\) only through \(V\), \[P(W=w\mid U=u, V=v)\ =\ P(W=w\mid V=v).\] Equivalently, \(U\) and \(W\) are conditionally independent given \(V\), written \((U\perp W)\mid V\): \[P(U=u, W=w\mid V=v)\ =\ P(U=u\mid V=v)\,P(W=w\mid V=v).\]

Two consequences make this the right object for information theory. The relation is reversible — \(U\to V\to W \iff W\to V\to U\) — because conditional independence is symmetric in \(U\) and \(W\). And any estimator forms a chain \(X\to Y\to \hat X\): an estimate \(\hat X\) is computed from the observation \(Y\) alone, never touching \(X\) directly. That structure is exactly what the data processing inequality needs — you cannot manufacture information about \(X\) by processing \(Y\).

Theorem. Data Processing Inequality [004Z]

  • February 9, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Suppose that \(U \to V \to W\). Then \(I(U; W) \leq I(U; V)\).

Intuition. by data processing, we can never create new information about \(U\).

Proof. Use chain rule to expand \(I(U; (V, W))\) in two ways i.e. \[ \begin {align*} I(U; (V, W)) = I(U; V) + I(U; W | V) = I(U; W) + I(U; V | W) \end {align*} \] Since \(U \to V \to W\) is a Markov chain, the conditional independence \((U \perp W) \mid V\) makes \(I(U; W | V) = 0\). Therefore, \[ \begin {align*} I(U; V) &= I(U; W) + I(U; V | W) \\ &\geq I(U; W) \end {align*} \]

Fano's Inequality [0050]

  • February 9, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Suppose that \(X \iff Y \iff \hat {X}\) and let \(p_e = \mathbb {P}(X \neq \hat {X})\). Then, we have \[ \begin {align*} H(p_e) + p_e \log (|\mathcal {X}| - 1) \geq H(X \mid \hat {X}) \geq H(X \mid Y) \end {align*} \]

Asymptotic Equipartion Propery

  • February 1, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Theorem. Weak Law of Large Numbers [0051]

  • February 10, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

\(\{X_{n}:n\geq 1\}\): a sequence of i.i.d. r.v.s. with mean \(\mu \), variance \(\sigma ^{2}\). Then, \[\frac {1}{n}\sum _{i=1}^{n}X_{i}\stackrel {{p}}{{\rightarrow }}\mu \]

Proof. By Chebyshev's Inequality, \[ \begin {align*} \mathbb {P}(|\hat {X} - \mu | > a) \leq -\frac {\sigma ^2}{a^2} \end {align*} \] Since \(X_i\)'s are independent, \[\mathrm {Var}[\hat {X}] = \frac {1}{n^2} \sum _{i = 1}^{n} \mathrm {Var}[X_i]\] Therefore, \[ \begin {align*} \lim _{n \to \infty } \mathbb {P}(|\hat {X} - \mu | > a) \leq \lim _{n \to \infty }-\frac {\sigma ^2}{n a^2} \to 0 \end {align*} \]

Corollary. Weak law of large numbers for functions [0052]

  • February 10, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

\(\{X_{n}:n\geq 1\}\): a sequence of i.i.d. r.v.s. with mean \(\mu \), variance \(\sigma ^{2}\). Let \(f:\mathcal {X}\rightarrow \mathbb {R}\) be any function, such that \(\mathrm {Var}(f(X))=\sigma _{f}^{2}<\infty \). Then, \[\frac {1}{n} \sum _{i = 1}^{n} f(X_i) \stackrel {p}{\rightarrow } \mathbb {E}[f(X)]\]

Theorem. Continuous Mapping Theorem [0053]

  • February 10, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Let \(X_{n}\stackrel {{p}}{{\rightarrow }}X\), and \(f:\mathcal {X}\rightarrow \mathbb {R}\) denote a continuous function. Then, \[f(X_n) \stackrel {p}{\rightarrow } f(X)\]

Theorem. AEP Theorem [0054]

  • February 10, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Let \(\{X_{n}:n\geq 1\}\) denote i.i.d. \(\mathcal {X}\)-valued r.v. with distribution \(P_{X}\). Then, \[ \begin {align*} \lim _{n\to \infty }-\frac {1}{n}\log p_{X^{n}}(X_{1},\ldots ,X_{n}) &= \lim _{n \to \infty } -\frac {1}{n} \log \prod _{i = 1}^{n} P_X(X_i) \\ &= \mathbb {E}_{X \sim p_X}[-\log p_X(X)] \\ &= H(X) \end {align*} \] Equivalently, for large \(n\) \[ \begin {align*} \left |-\frac {1}{n} \log p_{X^n}(X^n) - H(X)\right | \leq \epsilon \end {align*} \] with high probability.

\(\epsilon \)-typical set [0055]

  • February 10, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Fix an \(\epsilon >0\). Then, an \(\epsilon \)-typical set with respect to the pmf \(p_{X}\), denoted by \(A_{\epsilon }^{(n)}\), is defined as \[\left \{X^n \in \mathcal {X}^n \mid \left |-\frac {1}{n} \log p_{X^n}(X^n) - H(X)\right | \leq \epsilon \right \}\] Alternatively, \[A_{\epsilon ^{(n)}} = \left \{X^n \in \mathcal {X}^n \mid 2^{-n(H(X) + \epsilon )} \leq p_{X^n}(X^n) \leq 2^{-n(H(X) - \epsilon )}\right \}\]

Remark. We have the following important properties
  1. \(\mathbb {P}(A_{\epsilon }^{(n)})\geq 1-\epsilon {\text { for large enough }}n\)
  2. \(|A_{c}^{(n)}|\leq 2^{n(H(X)+\epsilon )}\)
  3. \(|A_{\epsilon }^{(n)}|\geq (1-\epsilon )2^{n(H(X)-\epsilon )}\;\text {for large enough }\;n\;,\)
Together, this says that, depending on the entropy, the typical set can contain most of the probability despite being much smaller than the entire space.

interpretation. \(A_{\epsilon }^{(n)}\,\text { is a subset of }\,{\mathcal {X}}^{n}\)

  • Contains almost all the probability.
  • Consists of \(\approx 2^{nH(X)}\)
  • Almost equiprobable sequences.

Corollary. High probability sets [0056]

  • February 10, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Let \(B_{\epsilon }^{(n)}\subset \mathcal {X}^{n}\) be the smallest set with probability at least \(1-\epsilon \). Then, we must have \[\mathbb {P}(A_{\epsilon }^{(n)}\cap B_{\epsilon }^{(n)})\geq 1-2\epsilon \]

Fixed-rate lossless source coding

  • February 1, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Definition. Coding system [0059]

  • February 11, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

A coding system with parameters \((n, \theta )\) consist of an encoder and a decoder, where an encoder is a function \[ e: \mathcal {X}^n \to \{1, 2, \ldots , \theta \} \] and a decoder is a function \[f: \{1, 2, \ldots , \theta \} \to \mathcal {X}^n\]

Performance.

  • Rate = \(\frac {\log _2 \theta }{n}\)
  • Probability of decoding error = \(\mathbb {P}|\hat {X}^n \neq X^n|\)

Definition. Achievability [005A]

  • February 11, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

A positive real number \(R\) is said to be achievable if for all \(\epsilon > 0\) (perturbation parameter) there exists a block length \(N(\epsilon )\) such that \(\forall n > N(\epsilon )\) the following holds

  • There exists an \((n, \theta )\) coding system such that \(\frac {\log \theta }{n} < R + \epsilon \) and \(\mathbb {P}|\hat {X}^n \neq X^n| < \epsilon \).

Alternatively, a positive real number \(R\) is said to be achievable if there exists a sequence of coding systems \((n, \theta )\) such that \(\lim _{n \to \infty } \frac {\log \theta }{n} \to R\) and \(\lim _{n \to \infty } \mathbb {P}|\hat {X}^n \neq X^n| \to 0\).

Definition. Minimum rate of source coding [005B]

  • February 11, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

The minimum rate of source coding \(R_s\) is the infimum over all \(R\) such that \(R\) is achievable i.e. \[R_s \coloneqq \inf \{R \mid R \text { is achievable}\}\]

Theorem. Entropy as MRSC [005C]

  • February 11, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

\[R_s = H(P_X) = H(X) = -\sum _{x \in \mathcal {X}} P_X(x) \log P_X(x)\]

Proof.

First we will show that \(H(X)\) is achievable. Then we will have \(R_s \leq H(X)\) by definition. Idea: take \(\theta = 2^{n(H(X) + \epsilon )} + 1\). Give each element in typical set a unique index, everything else the same index. By properties of typical set, \(\mathbb {P}(\text {error}) < \epsilon \) for sufficiently large \(n\).

For the other direction, suppose we have some \(R\) achievable. By definition, for all \(\epsilon > 0\), for all sufficiently large \(n\) there exists a Coding system with parameters \((n, \theta )\) such that \[ \begin {align*} \frac {\log \theta }{n} < R + \epsilon , \quad \mathbb {P}(\text {error}) < \epsilon \end {align*} \] Use Fano's Inequality to show this is not possible.

Variable Rate Source Coding

  • February 1, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Definition. Variable-rate coding system [005K]

  • March 23, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

A coding system (variable rate) with parameter \(n\) and an encoder-decoder pair \((e,f)\), where \[e: \mathcal {X}^n \to \{0, 1\}^*, \quad \text {and} \quad f: \{0, 1\}^* \to \mathcal {X}^n\] We want the probability of error to be exactly zero; that is \(\mathbb {P}(X^n \neq f(e(X^n))) = 0\).

Definition. Rate of a variable-rate code [005L]

  • March 23, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Let \(\ell (x^n)\) denote the number of bits in \(e(x^n)\), where \(e\) is the encoding function. Then, the rate is \[\frac {\mathbb {E}[\ell (X^n)]}{n}\]

Remark. Although it is unclear here, the difference between variable and fixed rate source coding is that we allow our encoder and decoder to map to/from \(\{0,1\}^* \), where \(^*\) is variable i.e. our encoded versions of our alphabet can have varying lengths.

Definition. Singularity of a code [005U]

  • March 24, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

If \(e\) is many-to-one then we say that \(e\) is singular. Similarly, if \(e\) is one-to-one then \(e\) is non-singular.

Definition. Extensions and Unique Decodability [005M]

  • March 23, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Given a code with \(n = 1\), we define its extension \(e^*\); that maps \(\mathcal {X}^* \to \{0,1\}^* \) by concatenation. That is \[e^*(x_{1}, x_{2}, \ldots , x_m) = (e(x_{1}), e(x_{2}), \ldots , e(x_m))\] A code with encoder \(e\) is said to be uniquely decodable if \(e^*\) is non-singular.

Definition. instantaneous / prefix-free code [005N]

  • March 23, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

A code is said to be prefix-free or instantaneous if no codeword is a prefix of another.

Remark. Prefix free codes are uniquely decodable. To show unique-decodability, you simply have to provide a way to decode and show it is unique. For prefix-free, greedily decoding suffices.
Remark. Note that non-singular does not imply uniquely decodable. This is because for uniquely decodable we require the extension to be non-singular. For example, if we have that \(e(0) = 0, e(1) = 1, e(2) = 01\). Then \(e^*(01) = e^*(2)\). From this, we can see that uniquely decodable is a much stronger condition.

Theorem. Kraft's inequality [005O]

  • March 23, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Let \(e: \mathcal {X} \to \{0,1\}^* \) denote a prefix-free code with lengths \(\ell _i = \ell (x_i)\) for \(x_i \in \mathcal {X}\). Suppose \(|\mathcal {X}| = m\). Then the following must be true: \[\sum _{i=1}^{m} 2^{-\ell _i} \leq 1\]

Remark. The converse holds too: for any lengths \(\ell _i\) satisfying the inequality, one can construct a prefix-free code whose codewords have exactly those lengths.
Remark. Surprisingly, the constraint is no weaker for the larger class of uniquely decodable codes — every uniquely decodable code must also satisfy the inequality (McMillan). So allowing non-prefix-free-but-decodable codes buys nothing in achievable lengths.

Performance Limit of Variable Rate Source Codes

  • February 1, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Definition. Variable rate code [005P]

  • March 23, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

A variable rate code with parameter \(n\) is a prefix-free code \(e : \mathcal {X}^n \to \{0, 1\}^* \), and a decoder \(f : \{0,1\}^* \to \mathcal {X}^n \)

Definition. Variable rate achievability [005Q]

  • March 23, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

A nonnegative number \(R\) is said to be achievable if for all \(\epsilon > 0\), there exists an \(n(\epsilon )\) such that for all \(n \geq n(\epsilon )\), the following holds

  • There exists a variable rate code with \(\mathbb {E}[\ell (X^n)]/n = R\) and \(\mathbb {P}(X^n \neq f(e(X^n))) = 0\) for all \(n\).

Definition. Variable minimum rate [005R]

  • March 23, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

The minimum rate of variable rate source coding is \(R_s \coloneqq \inf \{R \mid R \text { is achievable}\} \)

Theorem. Optimal variable-rate source coding rate equals entropy [005S]

  • March 23, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

\(R_s = H(X)\)

Shannon Fano Elias Code [005T]

  • March 23, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Idea: map symbol \(x\) to interval in \((0,1)\) using the CDF of \(X\).

  • Assume \(x_{1} < x_{2} < \cdots < x_m\)
  • \(X \sim P_X\) is \(\mathcal {X}\)-valued r.v. with \(\mathbf {p}_X\)
  • CDF of \(X = F_X(x) \coloneqq \sum _{x_i \leq x} P(x_i)\)
  • Midpoint function: \(G(x) = \sum _{x_i < x} P_X(x) + \frac {P_X(x)}{2} = F_X(x) - \frac {P_X(x)}{2}\)
  • Codeword: \(e(x) = \left \lfloor G(x) \right \rfloor _{\ell (x)}=\) first \(\ell (x)\) bits of \(G(x)\)
  • \(\ell (x) = \left \lceil \log (\frac {1}{P_X(x)}) \right \rceil + 1\)

Huffman Code [005Z]

  • March 25, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Definition. Huffman Code [005W]

  • March 25, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Given \(x_1, \ldots x_m\) proceed as follows:

  1. Take two \(x_i, x_j\) with smallest \(p(x_i), p(x_j)\) append 0 and 1 to their respective codes.
  2. Combine them and treat them as a new value with \(p(x_i') = p(x_i) + p(x_j)\)
  3. Repeat

Theorem. Optimality of Huffman Code [0060]

  • March 25, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Let \(X \sim P_X\) be an \(\mathcal {X}\)-valued random variable. Define \[L_H = \mathbb {E}_{X \sim P_X}[\ell _H(x)]\] and \[L^*(P_X) = \min _{\ell _1, \ldots , \ell _m} \sum _{i = 1}^{m} P_X(x_i) \ell _i.\] Then \(L_H = L^*(P_X)\).

Remark. SFE satisfies \(L_H < H(X) + \frac {2}{n}\) i.e. it achieves the optimal rate as \(n \to \infty \). Huffman coding achieves optimality for all finite values \(n\).

Method of Types

  • February 1, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Definition. type or empirical distribution [005X]

  • March 25, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

The "type" \( \widehat {P}_{x^{n}} \) associated with \( x^{n}\in \mathcal {X}^{n} \) is the probability distribution on \( \mathcal {X} \) with pmf \( \widehat {\boldsymbol {p}}_{x^{n}} \), defined as \[ \widehat {\boldsymbol {p}}_{x^{n}}(a)=\frac {N(a|x^{n})}{n},\quad \text {where}\quad N(a|x^{n}):=\sum _{i=1}^{n}\mathbf {1}_{x_{i}=a}. \]

Definition. Set of all types [005Y]

  • March 25, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

For any \( n\geq 1 \), let \( \mathcal {P}_{n} \) denote the set of all possible types over the alphabet \( \mathcal {X} \) with denominator \( n \). That is, \[ \mathcal {P}_{n}=\{\widehat {P}_{x^{n}}:x^{n}\in \mathcal {X}^{n}\}. \]

Definition. Type classes [0061]

  • March 25, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

For any type \( P\in \mathcal {P}_{n} \), we can associate the type class \( T(P) \), defined as \[ T(P)=\{x^{n}:\tilde {P}_{x^{n}}=P\}. \]

Example. Let \(\mathcal {X} = \{1,2,3\}.\) For \(n = 2\), \(x^n = 12\) the type is \((\frac {1}{2},\frac {1}{2}, 0)\) and the type class is \(\{12, 21\} \).

Properties of types

  • February 1, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Proposition. The number of types is polynomial in \(n\) [0062]

  • March 25, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Suppose \( |\mathcal {X}|=m \), and \( \mathcal {P}_{n} \) denotes the set of all distinct types that can be constructed using elements of \( \mathcal {X}^{n} \). Then, we have_ \[ |\mathcal {P}_{n}|=\binom {n+m-1}{m-1}\leq (n+1)^{m}. \]

Remark. \(\mathcal {X}^n\) has exponentially many elements in \(n\). However, among the exponentially many elements they can only create polynomially many types in \(n\) for fixed \(m\).

Proposition. Size of a type class [0063]

  • March 25, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Let \( P\in \mathcal {P}_{n} \) denote a type with denominator \( n \). Then, the type class associated with \( P \), defined as \( T(P)=\{x^{n}:\hat {P}_{x^n}=P\} \) satisfies \[ |T(P)|=\prod _{i=1}^{n}\binom {n-N_{i-1}}{N_{i}},\quad \text {where}\quad N_{0}=0,\text {and}\ N_{i}=n\times P(a_{i}), \] for all \( a_{i}\in \mathcal {X}=\{a_{1},\ldots ,a_{m}\} \).

Lemma. Sequence probability depends only on its type [0065]

  • March 25, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Suppose \( X_{1},X_{2},\ldots ,X_{n}\stackrel {{i.i.d.}}{{\sim }}Q \) for an arbitrary distribution \( Q \) over the alphabet \( \mathcal {X} \). Then, we have \[ \mathbb {P}(X^{n}=x^{n}) \equiv Q^{n}(x^{n}) = 2^{-n(H(\widehat {P}_{x^{n}})+D_{2n}(\widehat {P}_{x^{n}})=Q)}. \] In other words, the probability that \( X^{n} \) takes a value \( x^{n} \) only depends on \( x^{n} \) through its type \( \widehat {P}_{x^{n}} \). Furthermore, types that are far away from \( Q \) in relative entropy are exponentially less probable than those that are close to \( Q \). An immediate consequence of this is that if \( Q\in \mathcal {P}_{n} \), and \( x^{n}\in T(Q) \), then \( Q^{n}(x^{n})=2^{-nH(Q)} \).

Theorem. Entropy bounds on type-class size [0064]

  • March 25, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Let \( P\in \mathcal {P}_{n} \), and \( T(P) \) denote its type class. Then, the following bounds hold: \[ (n+1)^{-m}2^{nH(P)}\leq |T(P)|\leq 2^{nH(P)}. \]

Theorem. The probability of a type class [0066]

  • March 25, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Let \( X^{n}=(X_{1},\ldots ,X_{n})\stackrel {{i.i.d.}}{{\sim }}Q \) for an arbitrary distribution \( Q \) on \( \mathcal {X} \). Then, we have the following: \[ (n+1)^{-m}2^{-nD_{KL}(P|Q)}\leq Q^{n}(T(P))\leq 2^{-nD_{KL}(P|Q)}. \] In other words, the probability that \( X^{n}\stackrel {{i.i.d.}}{{\sim }}Q \) lies in the type class of \( P\in \mathcal {P}_{n} \) is \( \approx 2^{-nD_{KL}(P||Q)} \).

Large Deviation Theory

  • February 1, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Theorem. Sanov's Theorem [0067]

  • March 25, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Let \( X^{n}\sim Q^{n} \) (i.e., the \( X \)'s are drawn i.i.d. from \( Q \)), and \( E\subset \Delta _{m} \) denote a subset of pmfs over the finite alphabet \( X=\{a_{1},\ldots ,a_{m}\} \). Let \( Q^{n}(E) \) denote the probability \( \mathbb {P}_{X^{n}\sim Q^{n}}(\widehat {P}_{X^{n}}\in E) \). Then, we have \[ Q^{n}(E)\leq 2^{-nD_{\mathbb {E}}(P^{n} \parallel Q)}, \] where \( P^{*}=\inf _{P^{n}\in E}D_{\mathbb {E}}(P^{*}\mid Q) \) is the closest element of \( E \) to \( Q \) in terms of relative entropy. Furthermore, if \( E \) is the closure of its interior, we also have \[ \operatorname *{lim}_{n\to \infty }-{\frac {1}{n}}\log Q^{n}(E)=D_{K L}(P^{*}\parallel Q). \]

Hypothesis testing

  • February 1, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Definition. Hypothesis testing problem [0069]

  • March 25, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

A binary hypothesis testing problem involves three components: the data \( X\sim P \), the null hypothesis class \( \mathcal {P}_{0} \), and the alternative distribution class \( \mathcal {P}_{1} \), and the goal is to use the data to decide between the null and alternative; that is, \[ \text {given}\ X\sim P,\quad \text {decide between}\quad H_{0}:P\in \mathcal {P}_{0},\quad \text {versus}\quad H_{1}:P\in \mathcal {P}_{1}. \] When \( {\cal P}_{0} \) and \( {\cal P}_{1} \) are singleton sets: that is \( {\cal P}_{0}=\{P_{0}\} \) and \( {\cal P}_{1}=\{P_{1}\} \) with known \( P_{0},P_{1} \), then the problem is called simple hypothesis testing, and this is what we will focus on mostly in this course: \[ \text {given}\ X\sim P,\quad \text {decide between} \quad H_{0}:P=P_{0},\quad \text {versus}\quad H_{1}:P=P_{1}. \]

Definition. Hypothesis test [006A]

  • March 25, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

A randomized hypothesis test is a channel which takes in the data \( X \) and outputs a \( \{0,1\} \) valued random variable \( Z \), where \( Z=i \) represents that the test declares that \( H_{i} \) is true, for \( i\in \{0,1\} \). \[ X\sim P\longrightarrow \boxed {P_{Z|X}}\longrightarrow Z \] In some cases, it is sufficient to focus on non-randomized or deterministic tests, for which \( P_{\mathbb {Z}|X=x} \) is a delta function. That is, for a given realization of \( X \), the output of the test is deterministic (it is equal to \( H_{0} \) or \( H_{1} \) with probability 1). Such deterministic tests can be equivalently represented by a function \( \Psi \), or a subset \( B \) of the domain \( X \): \[ \Psi (X)=\mathbf {1}_{B}(X)=\begin {cases}1,&\text {if}X\in B\\ 0,&\text {if}X\not \in B.\end {cases} \]

Definition. Hypothesis error [006B]

  • March 25, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

In any binary hypothesis testing problem, a test \( P_{Z|X|} \) can incur two types of errors: \[\begin {align*} \pi _{1|0}(P_{Z|X})&=\mathbb {P}_{H_{0}}\ (Z=1) \quad \text {(type-I error of false alarm rate)} \\ \pi _{01}(P_{Z|X})&=\mathbb {P}_{H_{1}}\ (Z=0) \quad \text {(type-II error or missed detection rate)} .\end {align*}\]

Notions of optimality

  • February 1, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Definition. Neyman-Pearson Framework [006C]

  • March 25, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Given \(\alpha \in (0,1)\). The optimal hypothesis test in the Neyman-Pearson framework is one that satisfies \[\operatorname *{min}_{P_{Z\mid X}}\;\mathbb {P}_{H_{1}}\left (Z=0\right ),\quad \mathrm {subject\,to}\quad \mathbb {P}_{H_{0}}\left (Z=1\right )\leq \alpha .\]

Definition. Bayesian Framework [006D]

  • March 25, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Assume we have prior distributions \((\pi _{0}, \pi _{1})\) over the two hypotheses and there exists costs \(C_{1|0}\), \(C_{0|1}\) associated with type I and type II error respectively. Then the Bayes optimal solution is the solution of \[ \operatorname *{min}_{P_{Z|X}}\pi _{0}C_{1|0}\mathbb {P}_{H_{0}}(Z=1)+\pi _{1}C_{0|1}\mathbb {P}_{H_{1}}(Z=0). \]

Definition. Minimax Optimal [006E]

  • March 25, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Find a test that minimzes the maximum of the two errors i.e. \[ \operatorname *{min}_{P_{Z|X}}\operatorname *{max}\left \{\mathbb {P}_{H_{0}}(Z=1),\ \mathbb {P}_{H_{1}}(Z=0)\right \}=\operatorname *{min}_{P_{Z|X}}\operatorname *{max}\left \{\pi _{1|0}(P_{Z|X}),\ \pi _{0|1}(P_{Z|X})\right \}. \]

Theorem. Optimal NP Test [0068]

  • March 25, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Consider a hypothesis testing problem with observations \( X^{n}\stackrel {\text {i.i.d.}}{\sim }P_{X} \), with the null \( H_{0} : P_{X} =P_{0} \) and alternative \( H_{1}:P_{X}=P_{1} \). Then, the optimal NP test with level \( \alpha \in (0,1] \) has the following structure: \[ \Psi _{NP}^{*}(X^{n})=\begin {cases}1,&\text { if }L(X^{n})>\tau \\ \gamma ,&\text { if }L(X^{n})=\tau \\ 0,&\text { if }L(X^{n})<\tau .\end {cases} \] In the above display, \( L(X^{n}) \) denotes the likelihood ratio \( p_{1}(X^{n})/p_{0}(X^{n}) \), and \( \gamma \in [0,1] \) and \( \tau \geq 0 \) are constants that ensure \( \pi _{1|0}(\Psi ^*_{NP})=\mathbb {P}_{H_{0}}(\Psi ^*_{NP}(X^{n})=1)=\alpha \). Namely, \[\tau = \inf \{\tau \mid \alpha \geq \mathbb {P}(L(X^n) > \tau )\}\] and \[\gamma = \frac {\alpha - \mathbb {P}(L(X^n) > \tau )}{\mathbb {P}(L(X^n) = \tau )}\]

Proposition. Optimal Bayesian Test [006F]

  • March 26, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Let \( X^{n}\stackrel {{i.i.d.}}{{\sim }}P_{X} \), and consider a binary hypothesis testing problem with \( H_{0}:P_{X}=P_{0} \) versus \( H_{1}:P_{X}=P_{1}. \) Let \( (\pi _{0},\pi _{1}) \) denote the prior distribution of the two hypotheses being true. Then, the Bayes optimal hypothesis test with uniform loss is defined as \[ \Psi _{D}^{*}(X^{n})=\operatorname *{argmin}_{\Psi }\pi _{0}\pi _{10}(\Psi )+\pi _{1}\pi _{01}(\Psi )=\begin {cases}1&\text { if }L(X^{n})>\pi _{0}/\pi _{1}\\ 0&\text {otherwise.}\end {cases}, \]

Error Exponents in Hypothesis Testing

  • February 1, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios
 In the previous section, we consider the optimal test in the non-asymptotic regime i.e. for finite \(n\) what test minimizes the error according to some notion of error. In this section, we ask the question of what is the rate that this error converges to \(0\). More specifically, if \(\text {error}_n \leq 2^{-nV}\) then what is the best (largest) \(V\) we can hope to achieve?

Definition. Optimal Error Exponent in NP setting [006G]

  • March 26, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

We say that an error exponent \( V \) is achievable with type-1 error \( \alpha >0 \), if there exist a sequence of tests \( (\Psi _{n}) \) with errors \( (\alpha _{n},\beta _{n}) \), satisfying \[ \alpha _{n}\leq \alpha ,\quad \text {and}\quad -\frac {1}{n}\log \beta _{n}\geq V. \] Let \( V_{\alpha }:=\sup \{V:V\text { is achievable with type-1 error }\alpha \} \). The optimal error exponent is defined as \( V^{*}=\inf \{V_{\alpha }:\alpha >0\}=\lim _{\alpha \downarrow 0}V_{\alpha } \).

Theorem. Stein's Lemma [006H]

  • March 26, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

For any \( \alpha >0 \), we have \( V_{\alpha }=D_{KL}(P_{0}\parallel P_{1}) \), which also implies that \( V^{*}=D_{KL}(P_{0}\parallel P_{1}) \). In other words, the relative entropy between \( P_{0} \) and \( P_{1} \) is the optimal type-II error exponent associated with a binary hypothesis testing problem with \( H_{0}:P_{X}=P_{0} \) and \( H_{1}:P_{X}=P_{1} \).

Definition. Optimal Bayesian Error Exponent [006I]

  • March 26, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Consider a binary Bayesian hypothesis testing problem with prior distribution \( (\pi _{0},\pi _{1}) \). Then, we say that an exponent \( V>0 \) is achievable, if there exist a sequence of tests \( \{\Psi _{n}:n\geq 1\} \), with probability of errors \( \{p_{n}(\Psi _{n})=\pi _{0}\psi _{0}(\Psi _{n})+\pi _{1}\psi _{01}(\Psi _{n}):n\geq 1\} \), such that \[ \liminf _{n\to \infty }\frac {1}{n}\log \left (\frac {1}{p_{n}}\right )=\liminf _{n\to \infty }\frac {1}{n}\log \left (\frac {1}{\pi _{0}\psi _{10}(\Psi _{n})+\pi _{1}\psi _{01}(\Psi _{n})}\right )\geq V. \] The optimal Bayesian error exponent for binary hypothesis testing with distributions \( P_{0} \) and \( P_{1} \) is defined as \( V^{*}\equiv V^{*}(P_{0},P_{1})=\sup \{V>0:V\text {is achievable}\} \).

Theorem. Optimal Bayesian Error Exponent [006J]

  • March 26, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

For any \( \lambda \in [0,1] \) let \( P_{\lambda } \) denote the distribution over \( \mathcal {X} \) such that \( P_{\lambda }(x)\propto P_{0}^{\lambda }(x)\times P_{1}^{\lambda -1}(x) \). Let \( \lambda ^{*}\in [0,1] \) denote the value at which \( D_{KL}(P_{\lambda ^{*}}\parallel P_{0})=D_{KL}(P_{\lambda ^{*}}\parallel P_{1}) \). Then, the optimal error exponent in the Bayesian hypothesis testing problem with distributions \( P_{0} \) and \( P_{1} \) satisfies: \[ V^{*}(P_{0},P_{1})=D_{KL}(P_{\lambda ^{*}}\parallel P_{0})=D_{KL}(P_{\lambda ^{*}}\parallel P_{1}). \]

Definition. Fixed rate universal code [006K]

  • March 30, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

A sequence of fixed-rate source codes \( \{(e_{n},f_{n}):n \geq 1\} \) with parameters \( \{(n,2^{nR_{n}}):n \geq 1\} \) is said to be universal with rate \( R \) if the following are true:

  1. The limiting rate satisfies \( \lim _{n\to \infty }R_{n}\leq R \).
  2. For any distribution \( P \) over the alphabet \( \mathcal {X} \) with \( H(P)<R \), the probability of error, \( \mathbb {P}_{X^{n}\sim P^{n}} \left (f_{n}(e_{n}(X^{n})) \neq X^{n}\right )=0\).

Construction of universal fixed rated code [OGCSN]

  • July 11, 2026
  • with contributions from Kellen Kanarios

To construct, a universal fixed rate code, we need a code that satisfies (i) and (ii) from Fixed rate universal code.

  • Let \( |\mathcal {X}|=m \), and for any \( n\geq 1 \) and a given \( R>0 \), define \( R_n=R-m \frac {\log n+1}{n}\)
  • Consider the subset \(A_n \subset \mathcal {X}^n\), defined as \[A_n = \{x^n \in \mathcal {X}^n \mid H(\hat {P}_{x^n} \leq R_n)\} \]
  • Then just define the encoder in the usual way i.e. for encoder \(e_n\) give every \(x^n \in A_n\) a unique code and everything else map to the same code.
  • Then the decoder can easily identify everything in \(A_n\) and it suffices to show that in the limit the probabilities concentrate around \(A_n\).

Theorem. The type-based fixed-rate code is universal [006L]

  • March 30, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

The code constructed above is a universal fixed-rate code, such that \( \lim _{n\to \infty }\mathbb {P}_{X^{n}\sim P^{n}}(f_{n}(e_{n}(X^{n}))\neq X^{n})=0 \) for all \( P \) with \( H(P)<R \).

Variable-Rate Universal Source Coding

  • February 1, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Proposition. Redundancy of a mismatched code is the KL divergence [006M]

  • March 30, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Suppose \( X\sim Q \), but we encode it using an optimal code for \( P \). Then, the redundancy incurred is approximately equal to \( D_{KL}(Q\parallel P) \).

Proof. We know that the optimal codeword for \( P \) has lengths approximately equal to \( \ell _{P}(x)\approx \log (1/p(x)) \), where \( p \) denotes the pmf of \( P \). Whereas the optimal codeword for \( Q \) assigns lengths \( \ell _{Q}(x)\approx \log (1/q(x)) \). Then, the redundancy is \[ \begin {align*} \mathcal {R}(Q,P)&=\mathbb {E}_{X\sim Q}[\ell _{P}(X)-\ell _{Q}(X)]\approx \mathbb {E}_{X\sim Q}[-\log (p(X))+\log (q(X))] \\ &=\mathbb {E}_{X\sim Q}\left [\log \left (\frac {q(X)}{p(X)}\right )\right ]=D_{\mathrm {KL}}(Q\parallel P) .\end {align*} \]

Definition. Minimax Redundancy [006N]

  • March 30, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Let \( \mathcal {X} \) denote an alphabet of size \( m \), and let \( \mathcal {P}=\{P_{1},\ldots ,P_{k}\} \) denote a collection of \( k \) distinct distributions on \( \mathcal {X} \) (every \( P_{i} \) can be identified with a pmf in \( \Delta _{m} \)). Now, consider the following two-player game:

  • Player 1, representing adversary, chooses a distribution \(P\) from the set \(\mathcal {P}\)
  • Player 2, representing source coder, constructs a code according to the distribution \(Q \in \Delta _m\) without knowledge of \(P\)
The goal of Player 2is to identify a \(Q\) that minimizes the worst-case redundancy \[{\mathcal {R}}^{*}\equiv {\mathcal {R}}^{*}({\mathcal {P}}):=\operatorname *{min}_{Q\in \Delta _{m}}\operatorname *{max}_{P\in {\mathcal {P}}}{\mathcal {R}}(P,Q)=\operatorname *{min}_{Q\in \Delta _{m}}\operatorname *{max}_{P\in {\mathcal {P}}}D_{\mathrm {KL}}(P\parallel Q).\]

Remark. It will turn out the optimal choice of \(Q\) is the centroid associated with the \(k\) elements of \(\mathcal {P}\).

Theorem. Redundancy-Capacity Theorem [006O]

  • March 30, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

\(\mathcal {R}^*(\mathcal {P}) = C(\mathcal {P}) \coloneqq \sup _{\pi \in \Delta _k} I(W; X)\), where \(I(W; X)\) is the mutual information between \(W \sim \mathbf {\pi } \) and the output \(X\), with \(X | (W = i) \sim P_i\). The optimal \(Q^*\) is \(Q_{\pi }^* = \sum _{i = 1}^{k} \pi _i^* P_i\).

Lempel-Ziv Algorithm [006P]

  • March 30, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

The algorithm proceed as follows:

  1. Initalize the dictionary to an empty set
  2. Make one pass through the stream of data
  3. Add every new "phrase" to the dictionary
  4. Encode only the elements of the dictionary

Channel Coding

  • February 1, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

The channel coding problem has three components — a channel encoder, a noisy channel, and a channel decoder. We pass a message \(W\) to the encoder to get \(X^n\), which the noisy channel turns into \(Y^n\); the decoder must recover \(W\) from \(Y^n\). The encoder's job is to reduce redundancy while keeping codewords far enough apart that the channel noise cannot confuse them.

Definition. Channel coding problem [006S]

  • April 1, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

A code with parameter \((M, n)\) i.e. (number of messages, number of channel users) consists of an encoder decoder pair

  • \(e : [M] \to \mathcal {X}^n\)
  • \(f: \mathcal {Y}^n \to [M]\)
  • The rate is \(\log _{2}\frac {M}{n} = \) (log of the number of distinct pairs)/(number of channel users)
  • \(P_{e, \text {max}} = \max _{1 \leq i \leq m}\mathbb {P}(f(Y^n) \neq i \mid W = i)\) - worst case over all transmitted messages prob of error.
  • \(P_{e} = \frac {1}{M}\sum _{i = 1}^{M} \mathbb {P}(f(Y^n) \neq i \mid W = i)\) - average prob of error.

Definition. Discrete memoryless channel [006T]

  • April 1, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

We have the following components

  • \(\mathcal {X}\) - input alphabet \(|\mathcal {X}| < \infty \)
  • \(\mathcal {Y}\) - output alphabet \(|\mathcal {Y}| < \infty \)
  • \(P_{Y|X}\) - transition prob matrix

Definition. Achievable rates [006U]

  • April 1, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

A rate \(R \geq 0\) is achievable if

  1. \(\lim _{n\to \infty } P_{e, \text {max}} = 0\)
  2. \(\lim _{n \to \infty } \frac {\log _{2}M_n}{n} = R\)

Definition. Channel capacity [006V]

  • April 1, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

The channel capacity is the maximum achievable rate i.e. \[C = \sup \{R \geq 0 \mid R \text { is achievable}\} \]

Remark. We make the following simplifying assumptions (for now)
  • We assume no feedback. This means that if we have \(W \to X_{1} \to Y_{1} \to X_{2} \to \cdots \) then \(X_i\) does not use the knowledge of previous channel outputs i.e. \(Y^{i - 1} \leftrightarrow (W, X^{i - 1}) \leftrightarrow X_i\).
  • Random encoding: \(X^n(i)\) is drawn i.i.d from some specified distribution \(P_X\).

Definition. Information channel capacity [006W]

  • April 1, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

The information channel capacity of a DMC \((\mathcal {X}, \mathcal {Y}, P_{Y|X})\) is defined as \[C(P_{Y|X}) = \max _{P_X}I(X; Y)\]

Theorem. Shannon's coding theorem [006X]

  • April 1, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Non rigorous: says that channel capacity and information channel capacity are equal.

Definition. Symmetric Channels [006Y]

  • April 1, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

A channel is symmetric if

  1. each row is a permutation of the first row
  2. each column is a permutation of the first column
It is weakly symmetric if it satisfies \(1\) but the columns only sum to the same value.

Remark. The capacity of a symmetric or weakly symmetric channel is \(\log (|\mathcal {Y}|) - H(r)\), where \(r\) is the first row of \(P_{Y|X}\).

Definition. General definition of channel [0070]

  • April 12, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

A channel (with memory) is a sequence of conditional distributions:_ \[ P_{Y_{1}|X_{1}},P_{Y_{2}|Y_{1},X^{2}},P_{Y_{3}|Y^{2},X^{3}},\ldots ,P_{Y_{n}|Y^{n-1},X^{n}}, \] where \( P_{Y_{n}|Y^{n-1},X^{n}} \) is a collection of distributions on \( \mathcal {Y} \) one for every \( (y^{n-1},x^{n})\in \mathcal {Y}^{n-1}\times \mathcal {X}^{n} \).

Definition. Encoder [0071]

  • April 12, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

An encoder (with feedback) with parameter \( (n,\Theta ) \) for a channel is a vector of mappings \( e_{i} \) for \( i=1,2,\ldots ,n \), where \[ e_{i}:\{1,2,\ldots ,\Theta \}\times \mathcal {Y}^{i-1}\to \mathcal {X}. \]

Remark. We can decompose the joint distribution \(P_{X^n, Y^n}\) in terms of the what depends on the channel and the what depends on the encoder. Namely, \[P_{X^n, Y^n} = \prod _{i=1}^{n} P_{X_i | X^{i - 1}, Y^{i - 1}} \prod _{i=1}^{n} P_{Y_i | X^{i}, Y^{i - 1}}\] Note that the first term depends only on the encoder and the second term depends only on the channel.

    Assumptions.
