  • The encoder is a deterministic function of \(\mathbf {W}\)
  • The channel does not have access to the message \(\mathbf {W}\).

Definition. Differential Entropy [007C]

  • April 22, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

The differential entropy \( h(X) \) of a continuous random variable \( X \) with density \( f(x) \) is defined as \[ h(X)=-\int _{S}f(x)\log f(x)\,dx, \] where \( S \) is the support set of the random variable.

AEP for Continuous Random Variables

  • February 1, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Definition. Typical Set for Continuous Random Variables [007E]

  • April 22, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

For \( \epsilon >0 \) and any \( n \), we define the typical set \( A_{\epsilon }^{(n)} \) with respect to \( f(x) \) as follows: \[ A_{\epsilon }^{(n)}=\left \{(x_{1},x_{2},\ldots ,x_{n})\in S^{n}:\left |-\frac {1}{n}\log f(x_{1},x_{2},\ldots ,x_{n})-h(X)\right |\leq \epsilon \right \}, \] where \( f(x_{1},x_{2},\ldots ,x_{n})=\prod _{i=1}^{n}f(x_{i}) \).

Definition. Volume of set [007D]

  • April 22, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

The volume Vol(\( A \)) of a set \( A\subset \mathcal {R}^{n} \) is defined as \[ \text {Vol}(A)=\int _{A}dx_{1}\,dx_{2}\,\cdots \,dx_{n}. \]

Theorem. Properties of the typical set for continuous variables [007F]

  • April 22, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

The typical set \( A_{\epsilon }^{(n)} \) has the following properties:

  1. \( \Pr \left (A_{\epsilon }^{(n)}\right )>1-\epsilon \) for \( n \) sufficiently large.
  2. \( \mathrm {Vol}\left (A_{\epsilon }^{(n)}\right )\leq 2^{n(h(X)+\epsilon )} \) for all \( n \).
  3. \( \mathrm {Vol}\left (A_{\epsilon }^{(n)}\right )\geq (1-\epsilon )2^{n(h(X)-\epsilon )} \) for \( n \) sufficiently large.

Discrete Approximation

  • February 1, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Definition. Discrete Approximation of Differential Entropy [007G]

  • April 22, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

For, \[ p_{i}=\int _{i\,\Delta }^{(i+1)\,\Delta }f(x)\,d x=f(x_{i})\Delta \,. \] We define the discrete approximation of the differential entropy as \[\begin {align*} H(X^{\Delta })&\coloneqq -\sum _{-\infty }^{\infty }p_{i}\log p_{i} \\ &=-\sum _{-\infty }^{\infty }f(x_{i})\Delta \log (f(x_{i})\Delta ) \\ &=-\sum \Delta f(x_{i})\log f(x_{i})-\sum f(x_{i})\Delta \log \Delta \\ &=-\sum \Delta f(x_{i})\log f(x_{i})-\log \Delta , .\end {align*}\]

Remark. Taking \(\Delta = 2^{-n}\), then \(X^{\Delta }\) is an "\(n\)-bit" approximation of \(X\).

Definition. Information dimension [007H]

  • April 22, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Let \( X \) be any real valued random variable, and let \( X^{(1/n)} \) denote the discretization of \( X \) with parameter \( \Delta =1/n \). Then, the information dimension associated with \( X \) is defined as \[ d=\lim _{n\to \infty }\frac {H(X^{(1/n)})}{\log n}, \]

Notebook: Stochastic Processes [003Z]

  • January 30, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Markov Chains

  • January 30, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Notebook: Stochastic Processes › Definitions

  • January 30, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Definition. Markov Chain [0040]

  • January 30, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

We say a stochastic process \(X_n\) is a Markov chain (MC) with transition matrix \(p : \mathcal {S} \times \mathcal {S} \to [0, 1]\) if for any \(n \in \mathcal {N}\) and \(x\), \(y\), \(x_{0}, \ldots x_{n - 1}\) \[ \mathbb {P}(X_{n + 1} = y \mid X_{n} = x, X_{n - 1} = x_{n - 1}, \ldots , X_0 = x_0) = \mathbb {P}(X_{n + 1} = y \mid X_n = x) = p(x, y) \] whenever conditional probability is well-defined.

Remark. Moreover, any matrix \(p : \mathcal {S} \times \mathcal {S} \to [0, 1]\) satisfying \(\sum _{y \in \mathcal {S}} p(x, y) = 1\) is called a stochastic matrix. Given any stochastic matrix \(p\), one can create a MC.
Remark. This is called a temporally homogeneous discrete Markov chain because \(p\) does not depend on time.

Theorem. \(m\)-step transitions are powers of the transition matrix [0041]

  • January 30, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

The \(m\)th step transition probability \(\mathbb {P}(X_{n + m} = y \mid X_{n} = x)\) is the \(m\)th power of transition matrix \(p\), computed at \((x,y)\).

Definition. Chapman-Kolmogorov Equation [0042]

  • January 30, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

\(p^{m + n}(x,y) = \sum _{z \in S} p^{m}(x,z) p^{n}(z,y)\).

Proof. Note that \[ \begin {align*} p^{m + n}(x, y) &= \mathbb {P}(X_{m+n} = y \mid X_{0} = x) \\ &= \sum _{z} \mathbb {P}(X_{m+n} = y, X_{m} = z \mid X_{0} = x) \\ &= \sum _{z} \mathbb {P}(X_{m+n} = y \mid X_m = z, X_{0} = x) \mathbb {P}(X_m = z \mid X_0 = x) \\ &= \sum _{z} \mathbb {P}(X_{m+n} = y \mid X_m = z) \mathbb {P}(X_m = z \mid X_0 = x) \\ &= \sum _{z} p^{n}(z, y) p^{m}(x, z) \end {align*} \]

Definition. Stopping time [0043]

  • January 30, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

We say \(T : \Omega \to \mathbb {N} \cup \{\infty \}\) is a stopping time with respect to \(\{X_n\}\) if \(\{T = k\}\) can be determined by \(\{X_0, \ldots , X_{k}\}\).

Notebook: Stochastic Processes › Classification of States

  • January 30, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Theorem. Strong Markov Property [0044]

  • January 30, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Let \(T\) be a stopping time with respect to \(X_n\). For any \(k \in \mathbb {N}\), \(x \in S\), given \(\{T < \infty , X_{T} = x\}\), \(X_{T + k}\) is independent of \(\{X_0, \ldots , X_{T}\}\). Moreover, \[\mathbb {P}(X_{T + k} \mid T < \infty , X_T = x) = p^k(x,y)\]

Definition. Transient and recurrent states [0045]

  • January 30, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

We say \(x \in S\) is recurrent if \(\rho _{xx} = 1\) and transient if \(\rho _{xx} < 1\).

Definition. Return time [0048]

  • January 30, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

We say \(T_y\) is a return time if \(T_y = \inf \{n \geq 1 \mid X_n = y\}\), \(\rho _{xy} = \mathbb {P}(T_y < \infty )\). We write the \(k\)th return time as \[T_{y}^{k} = \min \{n > T_y^{k - 1} \mid X_{n} = y\}\] with \(T_y^{1} \coloneqq T_{y}\).

Definition. Communicates [0046]

  • January 30, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

We say \(x\) communicates with \(y\) and denote it by \(x \to y\) if \(\rho _{xy} > 0\).

Lemma. Transitivity of communcation [0047]

  • January 30, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

If \(x \to y\), \(y \to z\), then \(x \to z\).

Proof. By assumption, there exists \(m, n \leq t\) such that \(p^{m}(x,y) > 0\) and \(p^n(y,z) > 0\). Then \[ \begin {align*} p^{m + n}(x, z) &= \sum _{k \in S} p^{m}(x, k)p^{n}(k,z) \\ &\geq p^{m}(x,y)p^{n}(y,z) \quad \quad (\text {Strong Markov})\\ &> 0 \end {align*} \]

Lemma. Geometric tail bound on hitting times [0049]

  • January 30, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Suppose \(\mathbb {P}_x(T_{y} \leq k) \geq a > 0\) for all \(x \in S\). Then \(\mathbb {P}_x(T_y \geq mk) \leq (1-a)^m\), \(m \in \mathbb {N}\).

Proof. Define \(X_{n}^{(1)} = X_{k + n}, \ldots , X_{n}^{(m-1)} = X_{(m-1)k + n}\). Then define \(T_{y}^{(1)}, \ldots , T_{y}^{(m-1)}\) as the corresponding hitting times of state \(y\). We have \[ \begin {align*} \mathbb {P}_x(T_y > km) &= \mathbb {P}_{x}(T_y > k, T_y^{(1)} > k, \ldots , T_y^{(m-1)} > k) \\ &\leq \mathbb {P}_x(T_y > k, \ldots , T_y^{(m-1)} > k \mid X_{k} \neq y, \ldots , X_{(m-1)k} \neq y) \\ &= \mathbb {P}_{x}(T_y > k)\mathbb {P}_x(T_y^{(1)} > k \mid X_{k} \neq y) \cdots \mathbb {P}_x(T_y^{(m-1)} > k \mid X_{(m-1)k} \neq y) \\ &= \mathbb {P}(T_y > k)\left (\sum _{z \neq y}\underbrace {\mathbb {P}_z(T_y > k)}_{\leq (1-a)} \mathbb {P}_x(X_{k} = z \mid X_{k} \neq y)\right ) \\ &\leq (1-a)^{m}\underbrace {\sum _{z \neq y}\mathbb {P}_x \left (X_k = z \mid X_k \neq y \right )}_{=1} \end {align*} \]

Theorem. States in a finite closed irreducible set are recurrent [0058]

  • February 10, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

If \(C\) is a finite closed and irreducible set, then all states in \(C\) are recurrent.

Proof. Let \(x \in C\). It suffices to show that \(\rho _{xx} = 1\). Recall that \[ \begin {align*} \rho _{xx} &= \mathbb {P}_{x}(T_x < \infty ) \\ &= \end {align*} \]

Theorem. Decomposition Theorem [0057]

  • February 10, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

If the state space \(S\) is finite, then \(S\) can be written as a disjoint union \(T\cup R_{1}\cup \cdots \cup R_{k}\), where \(T\) is a set of transient states and the \(R_{i},\ 1\leq i\leq k\), are closed irreducible sets of recurrent states.

Notebook: Optimization Theory [0033]

  • January 26, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Convex Optimization Background

  • January 26, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Theorem. First-order condition [0034]

  • January 26, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

If \(f\) is first-order continuously differentiable, then \(f\) is convex iff \[f(\boldsymbol {y}) \geq f(\boldsymbol {x}) + \langle \nabla f(\boldsymbol {x}), \boldsymbol {y} - \boldsymbol {x} \rangle \]

Theorem. Second-order condition [0035]

  • January 26, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

If \(f: \mathbb {R}^n \to \mathbb {R}\) is twice-differentiable, then \(f\) is convex if and only if \(\nabla ^2 f(\boldsymbol {x}) \succeq 0\).

Operations that preserve convexity [DER0]

  • July 6, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

The following operations preserve convexity

  • Nonnegative weighted sums. if \(f_1, \ldots , f_n\) are convex and \(\alpha _1, \ldots , \alpha _n > 0\) then we have \(f(\boldsymbol {x}) = \sum _{i = 1}^{n} \alpha _i f_i(\boldsymbol {x})\) is convex
  • Pointwise maximum. if \(f_1, \ldots , f_n\) are convex then \(f(\boldsymbol {x}) = \max _i \{f_i(\boldsymbol {x})\}\) is convex

Definition. Strong convexity [0036]

  • January 26, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

A function \(f\) is \(\mu \)-strongly convex if and only if \[g(\boldsymbol {x}) = f(\boldsymbol {x}) - \frac {\mu }{2} ||\boldsymbol {x}||_2^2\] is convex, \(\forall \boldsymbol {x} \in \mathrm {dom} f\).

Definition. Smooth Problem [0037]

  • January 26, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

A problem is smooth if both the objective function and the constraints are differentiable.

Definition. Subgradient [0038]

  • January 26, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Let \(f: \mathbb {R}^n \to \mathbb {R}\) be convex. A subgradient of \(f\) at \(\boldsymbol {x}_0\) is any \(\boldsymbol {u}\) satisfying \[f(\boldsymbol {x}) \geq f(\boldsymbol {x}_0) + \langle \boldsymbol {u}, \boldsymbol {x} - \boldsymbol {x}_0 \rangle \]

Definition. Subdifferential [0039]

  • January 26, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

The subdifferential is the set of all subgradient's of \(f\) at \(\boldsymbol {x}_0\). \[\partial f(\boldsymbol {x}_0) \coloneqq \{\boldsymbol {u} | f(\boldsymbol {x}) \geq f(\boldsymbol {x}_0) + \langle \boldsymbol {u}, \boldsymbol {x} - \boldsymbol {x}_0 \rangle \}\]

Useful Miscellanous Math Background

  • January 26, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

In this section, we cover many random math facts / tools that come up / are useful in later optimization problems.

Basic Matrix Analysis [DER1]

  • July 6, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

A hub for the linear-algebra facts that recur throughout the optimization notes — inner products and traces, positive-definiteness, and the family of matrix norms built on the singular values.

Definition. Matrix inner product [WWMR]

  • July 11, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

The inner product on \(\mathbb {R}^{m\times n}\), obtained by treating a matrix as a long vector and summing the entrywise products: \[\langle \mathbf {X},\mathbf {Z}\rangle \ :=\ \sum _{i=1}^{m}\sum _{j=1}^{n}X_{ij}Z_{ij},\] the direct generalization of the vector case \(\langle \mathbf {x},\mathbf {z}\rangle =\sum _i x_i z_i\). It is the inner product whose induced norm is the Frobenius norm, \(\|\mathbf {X}\|_F=\sqrt {\langle \mathbf {X},\mathbf {X}\rangle }\).

It has a compact trace form (see Matrix trace): \[\langle \mathbf {X},\mathbf {Z}\rangle \ =\ \mathrm {tr}(\mathbf {X}^{\top }\mathbf {Z})\ =\ \mathrm {tr}(\mathbf {X}\mathbf {Z}^{\top }).\]

Definition. Matrix trace [PDX6]

  • July 11, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

For a square matrix \(\mathbf {M}\in \mathbb {R}^{n\times n}\), the trace is the sum of its diagonal entries: \[\mathrm {tr}(\mathbf {M}):=\sum _{i=1}^{n}M_{ii}.\]

Two identities make it the workhorse of matrix calculus. It is invariant under cyclic permutation of a product, \[\mathrm {tr}(\mathbf {A}\mathbf {B})=\mathrm {tr}(\mathbf {B}\mathbf {A}),\qquad \mathrm {tr}(\mathbf {A}_{1}\mathbf {A}_{2}\cdots \mathbf {A}_{n})=\mathrm {tr}(\mathbf {A}_{\pi (1)}\cdots \mathbf {A}_{\pi (n)})\] for any cyclic permutation \(\pi \) of \(\{1,\dots ,n\}\); and it computes the matrix inner product, \[\langle \mathbf {X},\mathbf {Z}\rangle =\mathrm {tr}(\mathbf {X}^{\top }\mathbf {Z})=\mathrm {tr}(\mathbf {X}\mathbf {Z}^{\top }).\]

Definition. Positive (semi)definiteness [003A]

  • January 26, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

A symmetric matrix \(\mathbf {A}\in \mathbb {R}^{n\times n}\) is positive definite if \(\mathbf {x}^{\top }\mathbf {A}\mathbf {x}>0\) for all nonzero \(\mathbf {x}\in \mathbb {R}^{n}\). It is positive semidefinite (p.s.d.) if \(\mathbf {x}^{\top }\mathbf {A}\mathbf {x}\geq 0\) for all \(\mathbf {x}\in \mathbb {R}^{n}\).

Fact. Singular Value Decomposition [003B]

  • January 26, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Let \(\mathbf {A}\in \mathbb {R}^{m\times n}\) with \(\operatorname {rank}(\mathbf {A})=r\). There exist \(\sigma _{1}\geq \sigma _{2}\geq \cdots \geq \sigma _{r}>0\), and matrices \[\mathbf {U}\in \mathbb {R}^{m\times r},\ \mathbf {V}\in \mathbb {R}^{n\times r},\] with orthonormal columns \((\mathbf {U}^{\top }\mathbf {U}=\mathbf {I},\ \mathbf {V}^{\top }\mathbf {V}=\mathbf {I})\) such that \[\mathbf {A}\ =\ \mathbf {U}\mathbf {\Sigma }\mathbf {V}^{\top }\ =\ \sum _{i=1}^{r}\sigma _{i}\mathbf {u}_{i}\mathbf {v}_{i}^{\top }\,,\quad \mathbf {\Sigma }\ =\ \operatorname {diag}\left (\sigma _{1},\cdots ,\sigma _{r}\right ).\]

Relationship between eigen decomposition and SVD

  • The columns of \(\boldsymbol {V}\) are eigenvectors of \(\boldsymbol {A}^T\boldsymbol {A}\)
  • The columns of \(\boldsymbol {U}\) are eigenvectors of \(\boldsymbol {A}\boldsymbol {A}^T\)
  • \({{\sigma _{i}({\mathbf {A}})}}={\sqrt {\lambda _{i}({{{\mathbf {A}}{\mathbf {A}}^{\mathsf {T}}}})}}={\sqrt {\lambda _{i}({\mathbf {A}}^{\mathsf {T}}{\mathbf {A}})}}\)

Theorem. Best rank-\(r\) approximation [003C]

  • January 26, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Let \(\mathbf {A}\in \mathbb {R}^{m\times n}\) with SVD, \(\mathbf {A}=\sum _{i=1}^{\min \{m,n\}}\sigma _{i}\mathbf {u}_{i}\mathbf {v}_{i}^{\top }\). Then an optimal solution to the best rank-\(r\) approximation problem \[\min _{\mathbf {X}}||\mathbf {X}-\mathbf {A}||_{2}\,,\quad \text {s.t.}\quad \text {rank}(\mathbf {X})\ \leq \ r\] is the truncated SVD of the following form \[\boxed {\widehat {\mathbf {A}}_{r}\ =\ \sum _{i=1}^{r}\sigma _{i}\mathbf {u}_{i}\mathbf {v}_{i}^{\top }}\,,\ \ \text {if}\ \ \sigma _{r}>\sigma _{r+1}\,,\,\text {it is unique.}\]

Proof. Suppose \(m \geq n\), then we observe that \[\left |\left |{\widehat {\mathbf {A}}}_{r}-{\mathbf {A}}\right |\right |_{2}\;=\;\left |\left |\sum _{i=r+1}^{n}\sigma _{i}\mathbf {u}_{i}\mathbf {v}_{i}^{\mathsf {T}}\right |\right |_{2} \leq \sigma _{r+1}\] Second, to prove our result, we want to show that \[\left |\left |\mathbf {B}_{r}-\mathbf {A}\right |\right |_{2}\ \geq \ \sigma _{r+1},\quad \forall \mathbf {B}_{r}\ \text {with}\ \mathrm {rank}(\mathbf {B}_{r})=r.\] We suppose \(\ \mathbf {B}_{r}=\widetilde {\mathbf {U}}\widetilde {\mathbf {V}}^{\top },\quad \widetilde {\mathbf {U}}\in \mathbb {R}^{m\times r},\ \widetilde {\mathbf {V}}\in \mathbb {R}^{n\times r}.\) Note \[ \begin {align*} ||\mathbf {B}_r - A||_2^2 &= \sup _{||\boldsymbol {z}||_2 \leq 1} ||(\mathbf {B}_r - \mathbf {A})\mathbf {z}||_2^2 \\ &\geq ||(\mathbf {B}_r - \mathbf {A})\mathbf {w}||_2^2 \end {align*} \] Since \(\widetilde {V}\) has \(r\) columns, then there must be a nontrivial linear combination of the first \(r{+}1\) columns of \(V\), i.e., \[\mathbf {w}=\gamma _{1}\mathbf {v}_{1}+\cdots +\gamma _{r+1}\mathbf {v}_{r+1}\] such that \(\widetilde {V}^{\top }\mathbf {w}=\mathbf {0}\,.\) Wlog, we can scale \(\left |\left |\mathbf {w}\right |\right |_{2}=1\) and \(\,\sum _{i=1}^{r+1}\gamma _{i}^{2}=1\) Then \[||\mathbf {A}-\mathbf {B}_{k}||_{2}^{2}\geq ||(\mathbf {A}-\mathbf {B}_{k})\,\mathbf {w}||_{2}^{2}=||\mathbf {A}\mathbf {w}||_{2}^{2}=\gamma _{1}^{2}\sigma _{1}^{2}+\cdots +\gamma _{r+1}^{2}\sigma _{r+1}^{2}\geq \sigma _{r+1}^{2}.\]

Definition. Matrix Operator Norm [003G]

  • January 27, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Let \(\mathbf {A}\in \mathbb {R}^{m\times n}\). If \(\left |\left |\cdot \right |\right |_{a}\) and \(\left |\left |\cdot \right |\right |_{b}\) are norms on \(\mathbb {R}^{n}\) and \(\mathbb {R}^{m}\), we have \[\left |\left |\mathbf {A}\right |\right |_{a\to b}\ :=\ \sup _{\left |\left |\mathbf {x}\right |\right |_{a}\leq 1}\left |\left |\mathbf {A}\mathbf {x}\right |\right |_{b}.\] It satisfies the three criteria for norm, and is submultiplicative \[||\boldsymbol {A}\boldsymbol {B}||_{a\to b}\ \leq \ ||\boldsymbol {A}||_{a\to b}\cdot ||\boldsymbol {B}||_{a\to b};\]. \[||{\cal A}||_{2\to 2}\;=\;\sigma _{1}({\cal A})\;\;(\mathrm {{spectral\ norm}},\;\mathrm {{write\ as}}\;||{\cal A}||\;\;);\;\] \[\begin {array}{l}{{||{\boldsymbol {A}}||_{1\to b}\ =\ \operatorname *{max}_{j=1,\cdots ,n}||{\boldsymbol {A e}}_{j}||_{b}}}\\ {{||{\boldsymbol {A}}||_{a\to \infty }\ =\ \operatorname *{max}_{i=1,\cdots ,m}||{\boldsymbol {e}}_{i}^{*}{\boldsymbol {A}}||_{b}^{*}\,,\ \ ||{\boldsymbol {v}}||_{b}^{*}\ :=\ \operatorname *{sup}_{||{\boldsymbol {u}}||_{b}\leq 1}\langle {\boldsymbol {u}},{\boldsymbol {v}}\rangle \,,}}\end {array}\]

Definition. Unitary Invariant Matrix Norm [003H]

  • January 27, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Let \(\mathbf {A}\in \mathbb {R}^{m\times n}.\) We say the matrix norm is unitary invariant if \[\left ||\mathbf {A}\right ||_{\sharp }\ =\ \left ||\mathbf {PAQ}\right ||_{\sharp },\quad \forall \ \mathbf {P}\in O(m),\ \mathbf {Q}\in O(n).\]

The two most common examples are both unitary invariant because they depend on \(\mathbf {A}\) only through its singular values: the Spectral norm (\(\|\sigma (\mathbf {A})\|_\infty \)) and the Frobenius norm (\(\|\sigma (\mathbf {A})\|_2\)).

Definition. Spectral norm [3U43]

  • July 11, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

The operator norm of a matrix induced by the Euclidean norm on both sides, \(\|\mathbf {A}\|_{2\to 2}\), usually written just \(\|\mathbf {A}\|\). It equals the largest singular value, \[\|\mathbf {A}\|\ =\ \sup _{\|\mathbf {x}\|_2\leq 1}\|\mathbf {A}\mathbf {x}\|_2\ =\ \sigma _1(\mathbf {A}),\] i.e. the most any unit vector can be stretched by \(\mathbf {A}\). As a Schatten norm it is the \(p=\infty \) case, \(\|\mathbf {A}\|_{\mathrm {S}_\infty }=\|\sigma (\mathbf {A})\|_\infty \), sitting opposite the Frobenius norm (\(p=2\)) in that family.

Definition. Frobenius norm [4M9J]

  • July 11, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

The entrywise Euclidean norm of a matrix — treat \(\mathbf {A}\) as a long vector and take its 2-norm: \[\|\mathbf {A}\|_F\ =\ \sqrt {\sum _{i,j}A_{ij}^2}\ =\ \sqrt {\langle \mathbf {A},\mathbf {A}\rangle }\ =\ \sqrt {\mathrm {tr}(\mathbf {A}^{\top }\mathbf {A})}.\] It is the norm induced by the matrix inner product, and in terms of singular values it is the \(p=2\) Schatten norm, \(\|\mathbf {A}\|_F=\sqrt {\sum _i\sigma _i^2(\mathbf {A})}\) — the Euclidean length of the singular-value vector, dual in that family to the spectral norm (\(p=\infty \)).

Definition. Schatten \(p\)-norm [003I]

  • January 27, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Let \(\mathbf {A}\in \mathbb {R}^{m\times n}\). For any \(p \in [1,+\infty ]\), the function \[\left ||\mathbf {A}|\right |_{\mathrm {S}_{p}}\,:=\,\left ||\mathbf {\sigma }(\mathbf {A})|\right |_{p}\] is a norm on \(\,\mathbb {R}^{m\times n}\).

Taylor Expansion and Lipschitz Functions [DER2]

  • July 6, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Definition. Lipschitz Continous [003J]

  • January 27, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Suppose that

  • \(f:\mathcal {X}\mapsto \mathcal {Y}\) with \(\mathcal {X}\) and \(\mathcal {Y}\) being open sets;
  • \(||\cdot ||_{\mathcal {X}}\) and \(||\cdot ||_{\mathcal {Y}}\) are norms on \(\mathcal {X}\) and \(\mathcal {Y}\), respectively.
\(f(\cdot )\) is Lipschitz continuous over \(\mathcal {X}\) if \(\exists L<\infty \,,\) such that \[||f(\boldsymbol {y})-f(\boldsymbol {x})||_{\mathcal {Y}}\ \leq \ L(\boldsymbol {x})\,||\boldsymbol {y}-\boldsymbol {x}||_{\mathcal {X}}\,,\quad \forall \boldsymbol {x},\ \boldsymbol {y}\in \mathcal {X}.\]

Definition. Smooth Function [003K]

  • January 27, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

A differentiable function \(f(\mathbf {x})\) is called smooth iff it has a Lipschitz continuous gradient, i.e., iff \(L< \infty \) such that \[||\nabla f(\mathbf {x})-\nabla f(\mathbf {z}) ||_{2}\ \leq L ||\mathbf {x}-\mathbf {z} ||_{2},\quad \forall \mathbf {x},\ \mathbf {z}\in \mathbb {R}^{n}\]

Theorem. Mean Value Theorem I [003L]

  • January 27, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Let \(f:\mathbb {R}^{n}\mapsto \mathbb {R}\) be continuously differentiable. For any fixed \(\mathbf {x}\) and \(\mathbf {y}\), we have \[\,f(\mathbf {y})\,=\,f(\mathbf {x})\,+\,\langle \nabla f(\mathbf {z}(t_{L})),\mathbf {y}-\mathbf {x}\rangle \,\] for some \(\mathbf {z}(t_{L})=(1-t_{L})\cdot \mathbf {x}+t_{L}\cdot \mathbf {y}\) with \(t_{L}\in (0,1)\,\).

Remark.
  • If \(f:\mathbb {R}^n\mapsto \mathbb {R}\) is continuously differentiable, \[\begin {aligned} |f(\pmb {x})-f(\pmb {y})| &\leq & \sup _{\pmb {z} \in \mathbb {R}^n} ||\nabla f(\pmb {z})||_2 \, ||\pmb {x}-\pmb {y}||_2 \end {aligned}\] so that the Lipschitz constant of \(f\) is \(\sup _{\pmb {z} \in \mathbb {R}^n} ||\nabla f(\pmb {z})||_2\).
  • If \(f:\mathbb {R}^{n}\mapsto \mathbb {R}\) is twice continuously differentiable, \[||\nabla f(\boldsymbol {x})-\nabla f(\boldsymbol {y})||_{2}\ \leq \ \sup _{\boldsymbol {z}\in \mathbb {R}^{n}}||\nabla ^{2}f(\boldsymbol {z})||_{2}\,||\boldsymbol {x}-\boldsymbol {y}||_{2}\] so that the Lipschitz constant of \(\nabla f\) is \(\sup _{\boldsymbol {z}\in \mathbb {R}^{n}}||\nabla ^{2}f(\boldsymbol {z})||_{2}\)

Theorem. Mean Value Theorem II [003M]

  • January 27, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Let \(f:\mathbb {R}^{n}\mapsto \mathbb {R}\) be twice continuously differentiable. For any fixed \(\mathbf {x}\) and \(\mathbf {y}\), we have \[\nabla f(\mathbf {y})\;=\;\nabla f(\mathbf {x})\,+\,\int _{0}^{1}\nabla ^{2}f(\mathbf {z}(t))\cdot (\mathbf {y}-\mathbf {x})dt\] for some \(\mathbf {z}(t)=(1-t)\mathbf {x}+t\mathbf {y}\) with \(t\in (0,1)\,,\) and that \[\boxed {f(\mathbf {y})\;=\;f(\mathbf {x})+(\nabla f(\mathbf {x}),\mathbf {y}-\mathbf {x})+\frac {1}{2}(\mathbf {y}-\mathbf {x})^{\top }\nabla ^{2}f(\mathbf {z}(t_{Q}))(\mathbf {y}-\mathbf {x})}\] for some \(t_{Q}\in (0,1)\,.\)

Theorem. First-order approximation [003N]

  • January 27, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Let \(f:\mathbb {R}^{n}\to \mathbb {R}\) be continuously differentiable, and \(\nabla f(\mathbf {x})\) is \(\gamma _{L}(\mathbf {x}_{0})\)-Lipschitz continuous at \(\mathbf {x}_{0}\), then \[\left |f(\mathbf {x})-\widehat {f_{L}}(\mathbf {x};\mathbf {x}_{0})\right |\ \leq \ \frac {\gamma _{L}(\mathbf {x}_{0})}{2}\,||\mathbf {x}-\mathbf {x}_{0}||_{2}^{2}\,,\] where we define \[\widehat {f_{L}}(\mathbf {x};\mathbf {x}_{0})\ :=\ f(\mathbf {x}_{0})\ +\ \langle \nabla f(\mathbf {x}_{0}),\mathbf {x}-\mathbf {x}_{0}\rangle \,.\]

Theorem. Second-order approximation [003O]

  • January 27, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Let \(f:\mathbb {R}^{n}\to \mathbb {R}\) be twice continuously differentiable, and suppose \(\nabla ^{2}f(\mathbf {x})\) is \(\gamma _{Q}(\mathbf {x}_{0})\)-Lipschitz continuous at \(\mathbf {x}_{0}\), then \[\left |f(\mathbf {x})-\widehat {f}_{Q}(\mathbf {x};\mathbf {x}_{0})\right |\ \leq \ \frac {\gamma _{Q}(\mathbf {x}_{0})}{6}\left ||\mathbf {x}-\mathbf {x}_{0}\right ||_{2}^{3},\] where we define \[\widehat {f}_{Q}(\mathbf {x};\mathbf {x}_{0}):=f(\mathbf {x}_{0})+\langle \nabla f(\mathbf {x}_{0}),\mathbf {x}-\mathbf {x}_{0}\rangle \] \[+\frac {1}{2}(\mathbf {x}-\mathbf {x}_{0})^{\top }\nabla ^{2}f(\mathbf {x}_{0})(\mathbf {x}-\mathbf {x}_{0}).\]

Optimality Conditions [DER3]

  • July 6, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Local conditions that a minimizer of a (not necessarily convex) \(f\) must satisfy, phrased in terms of its gradient and Hessian at the candidate point. They pin an optimum down to a stationary point and then use curvature to separate minima from maxima and saddles.

Definition. Stationary point [003P]

  • January 27, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

A point \(\mathbf {x_{\star }}\) is a stationary point of \(f(\cdot )\), if \[\nabla f(\mathbf {x_{\star }})\ =\ \mathbf {0}.\] Here, \(\mathbf {x_{\star }}\) is also known as a critical point.

Theorem. First-order optimality condition [I6DT]

  • July 11, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

If \(\mathbf {x_\star }\) is a local minimizer of \(f:\mathbb {R}^n\to \mathbb {R}\) and \(f\) is continuously differentiable in an open neighborhood of \(\mathbf {x_\star }\), then \(\mathbf {x_\star }\) is a stationary point: \[\nabla f(\mathbf {x_\star })\ =\ \mathbf {0}.\]

This is only necessary: a stationary point may be a maximizer or saddle, which is what the second-order conditions disambiguate. Under convexity it becomes sufficient as well — a stationary point of a convex function is a global minimizer.

Theorem. Second-order optimality conditions [U3L4]

  • July 11, 2026
  • Kellen Kanarios

Let \(f:\mathbb {R}^n\to \mathbb {R}\) be twice continuously differentiable in an open neighborhood of \(\mathbf {x_\star }\).

Necessary. If \(\mathbf {x_\star }\) is a local minimizer, then \[(i)\ \nabla f(\mathbf {x_\star })=\mathbf {0};\qquad (ii)\ \nabla ^2 f(\mathbf {x_\star })\succeq \mathbf {0}.\] That is, it is a stationary point whose Hessian is positive semidefinite.

Sufficient. If instead \(\nabla f(\mathbf {x_\star })=\mathbf {0}\) and \(\nabla ^2 f(\mathbf {x_\star })\succ \mathbf {0}\) (Hessian strictly positive definite), then \(\mathbf {x_\star }\) is a strict local minimizer. The gap between \(\succeq \) and \(\succ \) is exactly the ambiguity the first-order condition alone cannot resolve.

Remark. Let \(f:\mathbb {R}^{n}\mapsto \mathbb {R}\) be a convex function, then
  • A local minimizer of \(f\) is also its global minimizer. If \(f\) is strictly convex, the global minimizer is unique
  • A point is a global minimizer of \(f\) iff \[\mathbf {0}\ \in \ \partial f(\mathbf {x_{\star }}).\]
If \(f\in \mathcal {C}^{1}\), then \(\nabla f(\mathbf {x_{\star }})=\mathbf {0}\) implies that \(\mathbf {x_{\star }}\) is a global minimizer.

Constrained Optimality [DER4]

  • July 6, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Consider a smooth contained problem with \[\min _{\mathbf {x}}f(\mathbf {x}),\quad \text {s.t.} \quad r_{i}(\mathbf {x})\ =\ 0,\ 1\leq i\leq p,\] \[h_{j}(\mathbf {x})\ \leq \ 0,\ 1\leq j\leq q.\] Consider its Lagrangian function \[\mathscr {L}(\mathbf {x},\mathbf {u},\mathbf {v})\ =\ f(\mathbf {x})+\sum _{i=1}^{p}u_{i}\cdot r_{i}(\mathbf {x})+\sum _{j=1}^{q}v_{j}\cdot h_{j}(\mathbf {x}).\]

Definition. KKT Conditions [4IPN]

  • Kellen Kanarios
  • Stationary \[\nabla _{\mathbf {x}}{\mathcal {L}}(\mathbf {x_*},\mathbf {u_*},\mathbf {v_*})\;=\;\mathbf {0}\]
  • Primal Feasibility: \[\begin {array}{l}{{r_{i}(x_{\star })\ =\ 0,\ 1\leq i\leq p,}}\\ {{h_{j}(x_{\star })\ \leq \ 0,\ 1\leq j\leq q.}}\end {array}\]
  • Dual Feasibility: \[v_{\star }\geq 0.\]
  • Complimentary slackness: \[h_{j}(x_{\star })\cdot v_{\star j}\ =\ 0,\quad \forall \ 1\leq j\leq q.\]

Rate of Convergence [DER5]

  • July 6, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Want to find \[\operatorname *{min}_{\mathbf {x}}\ f(\mathbf {x}),\quad \text {s.t.}\quad \mathbf {x}\ \in \ \mathcal {C}.\] Solve the problem via iterative methods of optimization, which produce a sequence of points \[\mathbf {x}_{1},\ \mathbf {x}_{2},\ \cdots ,\mathbf {x}_{k},\cdots \] starting from an initialization \(\mathbf {x}_{0}\).

Definition. First-order convergence [003R]

  • January 27, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

First-order function value convergence \[||\nabla f(\mathbf {x}_k)||_2 \; \leq \; \varepsilon .\]

  • For convex functions, this also means convergence to the global minimizer
  • For nonconvex functions, this only means convergence to a Stationary point (e.g., a local minimizer or a saddle point).

Definition. \(Q\)-convergence [003Q]

  • January 27, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

We say the \(Q\)-convergence is of order \((p \geq 1)\) and with factor \((\gamma > 0)\), if \(\exists k_{0}\), such that \(\forall k\geq k_{0}\): \[\boxed {\min _{\mathbf {x_{*}}\in \mathcal {X}}||\mathbf {x_{k}}-\mathbf {x_{*}}||_{2}\ \leq \ \gamma \cdot \left (\min _{\mathbf {x_{*}}\in \mathcal {X}}||\mathbf {x_{k-1}}-\mathbf {x_{*}}||_{2}\right )^{p}.}\]

Definition. \(R\)-convergence [003S]

  • January 27, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

We say the \(R\)-convergence is of order \(p\;(\geq 1)\), \[\min _{\mathbf {x}_{*}\in \mathcal {X}}||\mathbf {x}_{k}-\mathbf {x}_{*}||_{2}\;\leq \;\rho _{k},\] if the sequence \(\{\rho _{k}\}_{k\geq 1}\) is \(Q\)-convergence of the order \(p\).

Iterative Methods

  • January 26, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios
 Assume.
  • \(f: \mathbb {R}^n \to \mathbb {R}\) is a Convex Function and a Smooth Function.
  • The gradient \(\nabla f(\mathbf {x})\) is \(L\)-Lipschitz.

Convergence of Gradient Descent [DER6]

  • July 6, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Lemma. Sufficient Value [004L]

  • February 3, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

With step size \(\tau _{k}=1/L\,,\) the gradient descent iterates \(\{\mathbf {x}_{k}\}_{k\geq 1}\) generated by \[\mathbf {x}_{k+1}\ =\ \mathbf {x}_{k}\ -\ \tau _{k}\cdot \nabla f(\mathbf {x}_{k})\] satisfy the following: \[f(\mathbf {x}_{k+1})\ \leq \ f(\mathbf {x}_{k})\ -\ \frac {1}{2L}\,||\nabla f(\mathbf {x}_{k})||_{2}^{2}\] \[<\ f(\mathbf {x}_{k})\quad (||\nabla f(\mathbf {x}_{k})||_{2}\neq 0)\]

Proof. Because \(\nabla f\) is Lipschitz, by 1st-order Taylor approximation \[f(\mathbf {x}^{\prime })\leq f(\mathbf {x})+\langle \nabla f(\mathbf {x}),\mathbf {x}^{\prime }-\mathbf {x}\rangle +\frac {L}{2}\left ||\mathbf {x}^{\prime }-\mathbf {x}\right ||_{2}^{2},\quad \forall \ \mathbf {x}^{\prime },\mathbf {x}\] Now take \(\mathbf {x}' = \mathbf {x} - \tau \nabla f( \mathbf {x})\). Then \[ \begin {align*} f(\mathbf {x}^{\prime }) &\leq f(\mathbf {x})+ \tau ||\nabla f( \mathbf {x} )||_2^2 + \frac {\tau ^2 L}{2} ||\nabla f( \mathbf {x})||_2^2 \\ &= f( \mathbf {x}) - \tau (1 - \frac {\tau L}{2})||\nabla f( \mathbf {x})||_2^2 \end {align*} \] Take \(\tau = \frac {1}{L}\), such that \[ \begin {align*} f( \mathbf {x}) - \tau (1 - \frac {\tau L}{2})||\nabla f( \mathbf {x})||_2^2 &= f( \mathbf {x}) - \frac {1}{2L}||\nabla f( \mathbf {x})||_2^2 \end {align*} \]

Theorem. Sublinear convergence of GD [004M]

  • February 3, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Suppose \(f:\mathbb {R}^{n}\mapsto \mathbb {R}\) is convex and smooth with its gradient \(L\)-Lipschitz. If we run GD for \(k\) iterations with a fixed step size \(\tau =1/L\), it will yield a sequence \(\left \{\mathbf {x}_{k}\right \}_{k\geq 1}\) such that \[f(\mathbf {x}_{k})-f(\mathbf {x}_{\star })\ \leq \ \frac {L}{2k}\left ||\mathbf {x}_{0}-\mathbf {x}_{\star }\right ||_{2}^{2}\ =\ O(\frac {1}{k}),\] Moreover, as \(k\to +\infty \), then \(\mathbf {x}_{k}\to \mathbf {x}_{\star }\).

Proof. Given \(f\) is convex and smooth, and \(\mathbf {x}_*\) is an optimal solution, then \[f(\mathbf {x}_{\star })\geq f(\mathbf {x})+\nabla f(\mathbf {x})^{\top }(\mathbf {x}_{\star }-\mathbf {x})\] Flipping the inequality yields \[f(\mathbf {x})\leq f(\mathbf {x}_{\star })+\nabla f(\mathbf {x})^{\top }(\mathbf {x}-\mathbf {x}_{\star })\] By previous lemma, taking \(\mathbf {x}' = \mathbf {x} - \frac {1}{L} \nabla f( \mathbf {x})\) yields \[f(\mathbf {x}^{\prime })\leq f(\mathbf {x})-{\frac {1}{2L}}\left ||\nabla f(\mathbf {x})\right ||_{2}^{2}\] such that \[f(\mathbf {x}^{\prime })\leq f(\mathbf {x}_{\star })+\nabla f(\mathbf {x})^{\top }(\mathbf {x}-\mathbf {x}_{\star })-{\frac {1}{2L}}\left ||\nabla f(\mathbf {x})\right ||_{2}^{2}\] Equivalently, \[f(\mathbf {x}^{\prime })-f(\mathbf {x}_{\star })\leq {\frac {L}{2}}\left [{\frac {2}{L}}\nabla f(\mathbf {x})^{\top }(\mathbf {x}-\mathbf {x}_{\star })-{\frac {1}{L^{2}}}\left ||\nabla f(\mathbf {x})\right ||_{2}^{2}\right ]\] Completing the square, \[ \begin {align*} f(\mathbf {x}^{\prime })-f(\mathbf {x}_{\star })&\leq \frac {L}{2}\left [\frac {2}{L}\nabla f(\mathbf {x})^{\top }(\mathbf {x}-\mathbf {x}_{\star })-\frac {1}{L^{2}}\left ||\nabla f(\mathbf {x})\right ||_{2}^{2}\right ] \\ &=\frac {L}{2}\left [\left ||\mathbf {x}-\mathbf {x}_{\star }\right ||_{2}^{2}-\left ||\mathbf {x}-\frac {1}{L}\nabla f(\mathbf {x})-\mathbf {x}_{\star }\right ||_{2}^{2}\right ] \\ &=\frac {L}{2}\left [\left ||\mathbf {x}-\mathbf {x}_{\star }\right ||_{2}^{2}-\left ||\mathbf {x}^{\prime }-\mathbf {x}_{\star }\right ||_{2}^{2}\right ] \end {align*} \] Let \(\mathbf {x}_{k}=\mathbf {x}_{k-1}-\frac {1}{L}\nabla f(\mathbf {x}_{k-1})\), then we have \[ \begin {align*} \sum _{i=1}^{k}\left (f(\mathbf {x}_{i})-f(\mathbf {x}_{\star })\right ) &\leq \frac {L}{2}\sum _{i=1}^{k}\left [\left ||\mathbf {x}_{i-1}-\mathbf {x}_{\star }\right ||_{2}^{2}-\left ||\mathbf {x}_{i}-\mathbf {x}_{\star }\right ||_{2}^{2}\right ] \\ &=\frac {L}{2}\left [\left ||\mathbf {x}_{0}-\mathbf {x}_{\star }\right ||_{2}^{2}-\left ||\mathbf {x}_{k}-\mathbf {x}_{0}\right ||_{2}^{2}\right ] \\ &\leq \frac {L}{2}\left ||\mathbf {x}_{0}-\mathbf {x}_{\star }\right ||_{2}^{2} \end {align*} \] Therefore, given \(f(\mathbf {x}_{k})-f(\mathbf {x}_{\star })\leq f(\mathbf {x}_{i})-f(\mathbf {x}_{*}) \text { for } \forall i\leq k\,,\) \[k\left (f(\mathbf {x}_{k})-f(\mathbf {x}_{\star })\right )\leq \sum _{i=1}^{k}\left (f(\mathbf {x}_{i})-f(\mathbf {x}_{\star })\right )\leq \frac {L}{2}\left ||\mathbf {x}_{0}-\mathbf {x}_{\star }\right ||_{2}^{2}\]

Theorem. Suboptimality of Gradient Descent [004N]

  • February 3, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Suppose the iterates \(\{\mathbf {x}_{i}\}_{i\geq 0}\) is generated from a black-box model: \[\mathbf {x}_{k+1}\ =\ \mathcal {F}_{k}\left (\left \{\mathbf {x}_{i}\right \}_{i=0}^{k},\ \left \{f(\mathbf {x}_{i})\right \}_{i=0}^{k},\ \left \{\nabla f(\mathbf {x}_{i})\right \}_{i=0}^{k}\right )\]

For every positive \(L\) and \(R\), there exists a convex differentiable \(f\) with \(\nabla f\) \(L\)-Lipschitz, and an initial point \(\mathbf {x}_{0}\) satisfying \(||\mathbf {x}_{0}-\mathbf {x}_{\star }||_{2}\leq R\), such that \[f(\mathbf {x}_{k})-f(\mathbf {x}_{\star })\ \geq \ c\frac {LR}{k^{2}}\ =\ \Omega (\frac {1}{k^{2}}),\] where \(\ c>0\) is some numerical constant.

Accelerated Gradient Descent [DER7]

  • July 6, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Nesterov's Method [004O]

  • February 3, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Generate two sequences \(\{\mathbf {x}_{k}\}_{k\geq 1}{\mathrm {~and~}}\{\mathbf {p}_{k}\}_{k\geq 1}\) \[ \begin {align*} {\mathbf {\mathit {p}}}_{k+1}\ &=\ {\mathbf {\mathit {x}}}_{k}\ +\ \beta _{k}\cdot ({\mathbf {\mathit {x}}}_{k}-{\mathbf {\mathit {x}}}_{k-1}), \\ x_{k+1} &= p_{k+1}-\alpha \nabla f(p_{k+1}) \end {align*} \]

  • Not a descent method.
  • With properly chosen \(\alpha \) and \(\beta \) can achieve optimal convergence rate \(O(1/k^2)\)

Theorem. Convergence of accelerated GD [004P]

  • February 3, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Suppose \(f:\mathbb {R}^{n}\mapsto \mathbb {R}\) is convex and smooth with its gradient \(L\)-Lipschitz. The iterates \(\{\mathbf {x}_{k}\}_{k\geq 1}\) generated by the accelerated GD method satisfy \[f(\mathbf {x}_{k})-f(\mathbf {x}_{\star })\ \leq \ \frac {L}{2(k+1)^{2}}\ ||\mathbf {x}_{0}-\mathbf {x}_{\star }||_{2}^{2}\ =\ O(\frac {1}{k^{2}}).\] Moreover, as \(k\to +\infty \), then \(\mathbf {x}_{k}\to \mathbf {x}_{\star }\).

Line Search [DER8]

  • July 6, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Definition. Exact Linesearch [004Q]

  • February 3, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

For \[x_{k+1}\ =\ x_{k}\ -\ \tau _{k}\cdot \nabla f(x_{k})\] Choose step size by solving \[\tau _{k}\;=\;\arg \operatorname *{min}_{t\geq 0}\;f({\mathbf {x}}_{k}-t\cdot \nabla f({\mathbf {x}}_{k}))\]

Remark. Way to computationally expensive to use in practice.

Definition. Armijo Condition [004R]

  • February 3, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

For some \(c_1 \in (0,1)\), \[f( \mathbf {x}_k + \tau _k \cdot \mathbf {d}_k) < f( \mathbf {x}_k) + c_1 \cdot \tau _k \cdot \mathbf {d}_{k}^{\top } \nabla f(\mathbf {x}_k)\]

Definition. Wolfe condition [004S]

  • February 3, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

For some \(0 < c_1 < c_2 < 1\), \[f(\mathbf {x}_{k}+\tau _{k}\cdot \mathbf {d}_{k})\ <\ f(\mathbf {x}_{k})+c_{1}\cdot \tau _{k}\cdot \mathbf {d}_{k}^{\intercal }\nabla f(\mathbf {x}_{k})\] \[d_{k}^{\top }\nabla f({\mathbf {x}}_{k}+\tau _{k}{\mathbf {d}}_{k})\ \geq \ c_{2}{\mathbf {d}}_{k}^{\top }\nabla f({\mathbf {x}}_{k})\]

Remark. Wolfe condition requires evaluating gradient of function each update to \(\tau _k\). However, Armijo Condition only requires evaluating gradient once. Therefore, it is primarily used in practice.
Remark. Backtracking is not used in optimizing DNNs because even function evaluation is very expensive.

Theorem. Convergence of GD with backtracking [004T]

  • February 3, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Let the function \(f(\pmb {x})\) be \(\mu \)-strongly convex and \(L\)-smooth. With backtracking linesearch, the GD method converges with \[f(\pmb {x}_k)-f(\pmb {x}_\star )\ \le \ \left (1-\min \left \{2c_1\mu ,\dfrac {2\alpha c_1\mu }{L}\right \}\right )^k(f(\pmb {x}_0)-f(\pmb {x}_\star ))\] where \(\pmb {x}_\star \) is the unique minimizer.

How function properties affect convergence [DER9]

  • July 6, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Theorem. Linear convergence for strong convexity [004U]

  • February 3, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Let \(f:\mathbb {R}^{n}\mapsto \mathbb {R}\) be smooth and \(\mu \)-strongly convex, and suppose its gradient \(\nabla f(\mathbf {x})\) is \(L\)-Lipschitz. Choose a fixed step size \(\tau _{k}\equiv \tau =\frac {2}{\mu +L}\), then \[\left ||\mathbf {x}_{k}-\mathbf {x}_{\star }\right ||_{2}\ \leq \ \left (\frac {\kappa -1}{\kappa +1}\right )^{k}\left ||\mathbf {x}_{0}-\mathbf {x}_{\star }\right ||_{2},\] where \(\kappa =\frac {L}{\mu }\) is the condition number, and \(\mathbf {x}_{\star }\) is the minimizer.

Remark. Strong convexity is a sufficient but not necessary condition for linear convergene of gradient descent.

Newton and Quasi-Newton Method [DERA]

  • July 6, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Newton's Method [004V]

  • February 3, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Consider second-order taylor approximation of \(f\) \[ f(\mathbf {x}_{k}+\mathbf {d})\ \approx \ \underbrace {f(\mathbf {x}_{k})+\mathbf {d}^{\top }\nabla f(\mathbf {x}_{k})+{\frac {1}{2}}\mathbf {d}^{\top }\nabla ^{2}f(\mathbf {x}_{k})\mathbf {d}}_{Q_k( \mathbf {d})}\] Then setting \(\nabla Q_k( \mathbf {d})\) and solving for \(\mathbf {d}\), we get \[ \nabla f( \mathbf {x}_k) + \frac {1}{2}\nabla ^2 f( \mathbf {x}_k) \mathbf {d} = 0 \] Thus, take Newton's direction \[\mathbf {d}_{k}^{N}=-\left (\nabla ^{2}f(\mathbf {x}_{k})\right )^{-1}\nabla f(\mathbf {x}_{k}).\]

Remark. Some notes
  • Fast convergence. Newton's method usually has local quadratic convergence.
  • Expensive. requires storing and inverting \(\nabla ^2 f( \mathbf {x})\).
  • Unstable. \(\nabla ^2 f \succ 0\) might not hold for general nonlinear problems.

Quasi-Newton's Method [004W]

  • February 3, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Get around computing and inverting hessian by using a surrogate i.e. \[\mathbf {d}_{k}^{QN}=-\underbrace {\mathbf {H}_k}_{\text {surrogate for } \left (\nabla ^{2}f(\mathbf {x}_{k})\right )^{-1}} \nabla f(\mathbf {x}_{k}).\] Want \[{\mathbf {H}}_{k+1}^{-1}\cdot \underbrace {({\mathbf {x}}_{k+1}-{\mathbf {x}}_{k})}_{{\mathbf {s}}_{k}}\;=\;\underbrace {\nabla f({\mathbf {x}}_{k+1})-\nabla f({\mathbf {x}}_{k})}_{{\mathbf {y}}_{k}}\] BFGS rank-2 update: \[{\mathbf {H}}_{k+1}\ =\ {\mathbf {H}}_{k}+{\frac {\left ({\mathbf {s}}_{k}^{\top }{\mathbf {y}}_{k}+{\mathbf {y}}_{k}^{\top }{\mathbf {H}}_{k}{\mathbf {y}}_{k}\right ){\mathbf {s}}_{k}{\mathbf {s}}_{k}^{\top }}{\left ({\mathbf {s}}_{k}^{\top }{\mathbf {y}}_{k}\right )^{2}}}-{\frac {{\mathbf {H}}_{k}{\mathbf {y}}_{k}{\mathbf {s}}_{k}^{\top }+{\mathbf {s}}_{k}{\mathbf {y}}_{k}^{\top }{\mathbf {H}}_{k}}{{\mathbf {s}}_{k}^{\top }{\mathbf {y}}_{k}}}\]

Optimization on the Riemmanian Manifold [DERB]

  • July 6, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Definition. Smooth Manifold [0073]

  • April 14, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

We say \( \mathcal {M}\subseteq \mathbb {R}^{n} \) is an embedded smooth submanifold of codimension \( k \) if there exists a smooth function \( g:\mathbb {R}^{n}\mapsto \mathbb {R}^{k} \), such that \[ \mathcal {M}\equiv \left \{\boldsymbol {x}\in \mathbb {R}^{n}\mid g(\boldsymbol {x})=\boldsymbol {0}\right \}, \] \[ \mathrm {D}g(\boldsymbol {x}):\mathbb {R}^{n}\mapsto \mathbb {R}^{k}\text { has rank }k,\ \forall \boldsymbol {x}\in \mathcal {M}. \]

Definition. Riemannian Manifold [0072]

  • April 14, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

A Riemannian manifold \( (\mathcal {M},\langle \cdot ,\cdot \rangle ) \) is a smooth manifold \( \mathcal {M} \) equipped with positive-definite inner product \( \langle \cdot ,\cdot \rangle \) on the tangent space \( \mathrm {T}_{\pmb {x}}\mathcal {M} \) at each point \( \pmb {x}\in \mathcal {M} \).

Definition. Tangent Space [0075]

  • April 14, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

The tangent space \( \mathrm {T}_{\pmb {x}}\mathcal {M} \) at a point \( \pmb {x}\in \mathcal {M} \) is defined as \[T_x \mathcal {M} = \ker Dg(\mathbf {x})\] which is a linear subspace of \( \mathbb {R}^{n} \).

Definition. Tangent Bundle [0074]

  • April 14, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

The tangent bundle of \( {\cal M} \) is the disjoint union of all tangent spaces: \[ {\rm T}{\cal M}\ =\ \{(\mathbf {x},\mathbf {v})\ \mid \ \mathbf {x}\in {\cal M},\ {\rm and}\ \mathbf {v}\in {\rm T}_{\mathbf {x}}{\cal M}\} \]

Definition. Retraction [0076]

  • April 14, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

A retraction is a smooth map \( R:\mathrm {T}\mathcal {M}\mapsto \mathcal {M} \) such that if \( h(t)=R(\mathbf {x},t\mathbf {v})=R_{\mathbf {x}}(t\mathbf {v}) \), then \( h(0)=\mathbf {x} \) and \( h^{\prime }(0)=\mathbf {v} \).

Definition. Riemannian Metric [0079]

  • April 14, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

A choice of inner product \(\langle \cdot , \cdot \rangle _x\) for each tangent space \(\mathrm {T}_x \mathcal {M}\) defines a Riemannian metric on \(\mathcal {M}\).

Claim. Riemmanian Metric [0078]

  • April 14, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

\( \langle \cdot ,\cdot \rangle _{\textbf {x}} \) defines a Riemmanian Metric on \( \mathcal {M} \) if for any two smooth vector fields \(\mathbf {V}\) and \(\mathbf {W}\), the function \( \textbf {x}\mapsto \left \langle \mathbf {V}(\textbf {x}),\mathbf {W}(\textbf {x})\right \rangle _{\textbf {x}} \) is smooth.

Definition. Riemannian Gradient [0077]

  • April 14, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Let \( f:\mathcal {M}\mapsto \mathbb {R} \) be smooth on the Riemannian manifold \( \mathcal {M} \). The Riemannian gradient \( \,\operatorname {grad}f(\boldsymbol {x})\, \) of \( f \) at \( \boldsymbol {x}\in \mathcal {M} \) is the unique tangent vector at \( \boldsymbol {x} \) such that \[ \mathrm {D}f(\boldsymbol {x})[\boldsymbol {v}]\ =\ \langle \operatorname {grad}f(\boldsymbol {x}),\boldsymbol {v}\rangle _{\boldsymbol {x}}\,,\quad \forall \,\boldsymbol {v}\in \mathrm {T}_{\boldsymbol {x}}\mathcal {M}. \] By extending the definition of \( f \) to the ambient Euclidean space \( \mathbb {R}^{n} \), we can show that \[\mathrm {grad} f(\mathbf {x}) = \mathcal {P}_{T_x \mathcal {M}}(\nabla f(\mathbf {x}))\] where \( {\mathcal {P}}_{\mathrm {T}_{\mathbf {x}}\mathcal {M}}\) is the orthogonal projector from \(\mathbb {R}^n\) to \(T_x \mathcal {M}\)

Definition. Riemannian Hessian [007A]

  • April 14, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

Let \( f:{\cal M}\mapsto \mathbb {R} \) be smooth on the Riemannian Manifold \( {\cal M} \). The Riemannian Hessian \( {\rm Hess}\,f(\mathbf {x}):{\rm T}_{\mathbf {x}}{\cal M}\mapsto {\rm T}_{\mathbf {x}}{\cal M} \) of \( f \) at \( \mathbf {x}\in {\cal M} \) is a mapping from \( {\rm T}_{\mathbf {x}}{\cal M} \) to \( {\rm T}_{\mathbf {x}}{\cal M} \) with \[ {\rm Hess}\,f(\mathbf {x})[\mathbf {v}]\ :=\ \widetilde {\nabla }_{\mathbf {v}}\,{\rm grad}\,f(\mathbf {x}). \]

  • Here, \(\tilde {\nabla }\) is the Riemannian connection.
  • On a Riemannian submanifold embedded in \(\mathbb {R}^n\) \[\tilde {\nabla }_{\mathbf {u}} V = \mathcal {P}_{T_{\mathbf {x}} \mathcal {M}}(\mathrm {D}V(\mathbf {x})[\mathbf {u}])\] and \[\mathrm {Hess}\,f(\mathbf {x})[\mathbf {v}]\ =\ {\mathcal {P}}_{\mathrm {T}_{\mathbf {x}}{\mathcal {M}}}\,(\mathrm {D}\,\mathrm {grad}\,f(\mathbf {x})[\mathbf {v}])\]

Definition. Riemannian Gradient Descent [007B]

  • April 14, 2025
  • Kellen Kanarios

For \(f : \mathcal {M} \mapsto \mathbb {R}\), \[ x_{k+1}\ =\ R_{{\mathbf {x}}_{k}}(-\tau _{k}\operatorname {grad}f({\mathbf {x}}_{k})) \]

Can do backtracking line search i.e. while \[f(R_{x_{k}}(-\tau \,{\rm grad}\,f(\mathbf {x}_{k}))) > f(\mathbf {x}_{k}) - c_{1}\tau \, | |{\rm grad}\,f(\mathbf {x}_{k})| |_{2}^{2} \] do \[ \tau \ \leftarrow \ \alpha \cdot \tau \]

Dynamic Programming

  • November 24, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios
Remark. Value Iteration as Linear Algebra. During my brief stint at IPAM, I spent a lot of time focused on solving large systems for discretized PDEs. From this, I learned about iterative methods, such as Gauss-Jacobi and Gauss-Seidel. Interestingly, these methods can be used to interpret some of RL's most fundamental algorithms. If we define the following matrices, \[ \begin {align*} R &= \begin {bmatrix} \mathbb {E}[r(s = 1)]\\ \vdots \\ \mathbb {E}[r(s = n)] \end {bmatrix}, \quad P = \begin {bmatrix} P_{\pi }(s'=1 | s=1) & \cdots & P_{\pi }(s'=n | s=1) \\ \vdots & \ddots & \vdots \\ P_{\pi }(s'=1 | s=n) & \cdots & P_{\pi }(s'=n | s=n) \end {bmatrix}, \\ V_k &= \begin {bmatrix} V_k(s = 1)\\ \vdots \\ V_k(s = n) \end {bmatrix} \end {align*} \] Then we can write the value function \(V_{\pi }\) as the solution to the linear system \[ V_{\pi } = R + PV_{\pi } \iff V_{\pi } = (1 - P)^{-1} R \] This is just inverting a matrix! Updating one state with the canonical update \[ \begin {align*} v_{k + 1}(s) = \sum _{a}\pi (a|s)\sum _{s^{\prime },r}p(s^{\prime },r\,|\,s,a)\Big [r+\gamma v_{k}(s^{\prime })\Big ] \end {align*} \] Is actually just one iteration of Gauss-Seidel!
Remark. (Number of updates as pseudo discount factor).

In my opinion, the most important part of this chapter is now that when we update say a \(Q\) function for a specific state action pair \((s,a)\), then this update can affect the value of the \(Q(s', a')\) due to the reuse of the internal parameters.

When in the continuous state space regime i.e. \(s_i \in [0,1]\), there is no amount of features that would overfit the transition kernel. Therefore, it is just about trying to learn as much as possible about the underlying relations of the state dimensions.

    In the \(2\)-dimensional case, the feature vector \(c_i = [1,1]\) would provide information on how \(s_1\) and \(s_2\) relate not just their specific values. You want the learning algorithm to understand the relationships, so that it can make better use of the internal parameters when learning something like a \(Q\) function. This is basically just SVD but instead of classification you are learning a \(Q\) function. The compression

Borel's Normal Number Theorem

  • October 30, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

Theorem. Additivity of Intervals [000S]

  • November 20, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

We have the following properties

  1. If \(\bigcup _{k}I_{k}\subset I\), and the \(I_{k}\) are disjoint, then \(\sum _{k}|I_{k}|\leq |I|\).
  2. If \(I\subset \bigcup _{k}I_{k}\) (the \(I_{k}\) need not be disjoint), then \(|I|\leq \sum _{k}|I_{k}|\).
  3. If \(I=\bigcup _{k}I_{k}\), and the \(I_{k}\) are disjoint, then \(|I|=\sum _{k}|I_{k}|\).

Remark. The important takeaway from Additivity of Intervals is that extending (2) from finite to countable unions requires Heine-Borel theorem, illustrating that this extension is non-trivial. This will be important to remember as we continue to extend the Lebesgue measure to \(\sigma \)-algebras.
This section aims to motivate why we need to approach probability from a measure theoretic point of view. As a concrete example, Dyadic Intervals sets up the dyadic-interval representation of an infinite coin toss, and Exercise 1.1 makes precise why no discrete probability space can carry that sequence.

Example. Dyadic Intervals [000L]

  • November 14, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

Represent coin toss as dyadic expansion consider the set

\[\left [\omega :\ d_{i}(\omega )=u_{i},\,i=1,\ldots ,n \right ]=\Big (\sum _{i=1}^{n} \frac {u_{i}}{2^{i}},\ \sum _{i=1}^{n}\frac {u_{i}}{2^{i}} + \frac {1}{2^{n}}\Big ).\]

More stuff

I think my main takeaway from this is that infinite sequences break discrete probability because each discrete event will have probability zero. This is not actually a problem because in an infinite sequence the occurence of any such sequence "feasibly" has zero probability. However, we still want to reason about probabilistic things like the convergence of the average of the sequence. Since discrete probability is broken, we cannot even use it for these things. This is why we need to use measure theory to allow for zero probability sequences, but still provide information about these more "general" events. Who knows if this intuition is right though?

Remark.
  • After a bit more digging, the main culprit here is the countable subadditivity condition of measure.
  • In a discrete probability space, the entire space is at most countable. Therefore, if each individual event has zero probability then the countable union i.e. the whole space will have zero probability.
  • In measure theoretic probability, we are able to accumulate non-zero probability mass from an uncountable union of zero measure events.
  • I guess the canonical example of this is that each point on a line has length 0, but the line itself (an uncountable union of points) has non-zero length.
  • In the dyadic intervals example, each event of an arbitrary discrete probability space can be identified with a point on the line of probability zero.

Probability Measures

  • October 30, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

Definition. \(\sigma \)-field [000O]

  • November 20, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

A class of subsets \(\mathscr {F}\) of \(\Omega \) is a \(\sigma \)-field if the following holds

  1. \(\Omega \in \mathscr {F}\)
  2. If \(A \in \mathscr {F}\) then \(A^c \in \mathscr {F}\)
  3. If \(A_1, A_2, \ldots \in \mathscr {F}\) then \(\bigcup _{i = 1}^{\infty } A_i \in \mathscr {F}\)

It seems, in this section, we aim to answer why certain decisions were made regarding what can be analyzed. For example,

Question. Why do we only allow consider countable unions in the definition of a \(\sigma \)-field?
Answer. Because this is what god intended.

Definition. Probability Measure [000P]

  • November 20, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

A set function is a real-valued function defined on some class of subsets of \(\Omega \). A set function \(P\) on a field \(\mathscr {F}\) is a probability measure if it satisfies these conditions:

  1. \(0\leq P(A)\leq 1\) for \(A\in \mathscr {F}\);
  2. \(P(\varnothing )=0\), \(P(\Omega )=1\);
  3. if \(A_{1},A_{2},\ldots \) is a disjoint sequence of \(\mathscr {F}\) sets and if \(\bigcup _{k=1}^{\infty }A_{k}\in \mathscr {F}\), then \[P\Big (\bigcup _{k=1}^{\infty }A_{k}\Big )=\sum _{k=1}^{\infty }P(A_{k}).\]

Definition. Probability Space [000Q]

  • November 20, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

If \(\mathscr {F}\) is a \(\sigma \)-field in \(\Omega \) and \(P\) is a probability measure on \(\mathscr {F}\), the triple \((\Omega ,\mathscr {F},P)\) is called a probability measure space, or simply a probability space. A support of \(P\) is any \(\mathscr {F}\)-set \(A\) for which \(P(A)=1\).

Theorem. Continuity and countable subadditivity of a probability measure [000R]

  • November 20, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

Let \(P\) be a probability measure on a field \(\mathscr {F}\).

  1. Continuity from below: If \(A_{n}\) and \(A\) lie in \(\mathscr {F}\) and \(\mathscr {A}_{n}\uparrow A\), then \(P(A_{n})\uparrow P(A)\).
  2. Continuity from above: If \(A_{n}\) and \(A\) lie in \(\mathscr {F}\) and \(A_{n}\downarrow A\), then \(P(A_{n})\downarrow P(A)\).
  3. Countable subadditivity: If \(A_{1},A_{2},\ldots \) and \(\bigcup _{k=1}^{\infty }A_{k}\) lie in \(\mathscr {F}\) (the \(A_{k}\) need not be disjoint), then \[P\left (\bigcup _{k=1}^{\infty }A_{k}\right )\leq \sum _{k=1}^{\infty }P(A_{k})\]

Existence and Extension

  • October 30, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

Here we will see how we can define a probability measure on an algebra \(\mathscr {F}_0\) and get a unique extension to the \(\sigma \)-field \(\mathscr {F} = \sigma (\mathscr {F}_0)\). I guess the key here is uniqueness?

Definition. Outer Measure [000T]

  • November 21, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

For each subset \(A\) of \(\Omega \), \[P^{*}(A)=\mathrm {inf}\,\sum _{n}P(A_{n}),\] where \(A \subset \bigcup _{n = 1}^{\infty } A_n.\)

Remark. Taking \(P_*(A) = 1 - P^*(A^c)\) gives the inner measure. This is just analogously approximating from the inside.
Intuition is that the sets that are "measurable" should be sets that can be arbitrarily approximated from the inside and outside, or equivalently \[P^*(A) + P^*(A^c) = 1\]
  1. My intution for this is that if a set is not arbitrarily well approximated by countable other sets from our algebra then we cannot hope to get an accurate measure from our measure defined on only the algebra.
  2. The countableness is just from the definition of the \(\sigma \)-field.

Then we can use \(P^*\) as our measure. To do this, we will need to show that \(P^*\) is countable additive as one might expect. The rest of the properties of a Probability Measure are trivial.

Proof.

Suppose that \(A \subset \bigcup _{n = 1}^{\infty } A_n\). By the definition of Outer Measure, we can find some \(B_{nk}\), such that \(A_n \subset \bigcup _{k = 1}^{\infty } B_{nk}\) where \[\sum _{k} P(B_{nk}) < P^*(A_n) + \epsilon 2^{-k}\] Now take \(C_n = \bigcup _{k} B_{nk}\), so that \(\bigcup _{n} A_n \subset \bigcup _{n} C_n\) and \[P^*\left (\bigcup _n A_n\right ) < \sum _{n} P(C_n) = \sum _{n} \sum _{k} P(B_{nk}) < \sum _{n} P^*(A_n) + \epsilon \]

Since \(\bigcup _{n} A_n = A\), we have achieved the desired result.

It turns out that for some reason only enforcing \(A\) and its complement to sum to \(1\) is not restrictive enough? From this, we end up with the final condition

Definition. \(P^*\)-measurable [000U]

  • November 21, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

\(A\) is \(P^*\)-measurable if for every \(E \subset \Omega \), we have that \[P^*(A \cap E) + P^*(A^c \cap E) = P^*(E)\]

You then only need to show two things:

  1. The class of \(P^*\)-measurable sets contains \(\mathscr {F_0}\),
  2. The class of \(P^*\)-measurable sets is a \(\sigma \)-field.
From these, we can conclude that \(P^*\) is at least defined on a \(\sigma \)-field containing \(\sigma (\mathscr {F}_0)\). Restricting \(P^*\) to \(\sigma (\mathscr {F}_0)\), then yields the desired extension.

Uniqueness

  • October 30, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

We now want to show that the extension we developed in the previous section is actually unique. The first question we have is what do we mean by unique? To answer this, we need to remember what we have done so far.

  1. We have a Probability Measure \(P\), defined on an algebra \(\mathscr {F}_0\).
  2. We then extended \(P\) to \(\sigma (\mathscr {F}_0)\) via \(P^*\), so that for every \(A \in \mathscr {F}_0\) we have \(P(A) = P^*(A)\) and \(P^*\) is defined on all of \(\sigma (\mathscr {F}_0)\)
From this, our only restriction is that \(P^*(A) = P(A)\) for \(A \in \mathscr {F}_0\). Therefore, uniqueness here means that if \(P^*(A) = Q(A)\) for some \(Q\) and every \(A \in \mathscr {F}_0\). Then \(P^*(A) = Q(A)\) for every \(A \in \sigma (\mathscr {F}_0)\).

Definition. \(\pi \)-system [0018]

  • November 26, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

A class \(\mathcal {P}\) of subsets of \(\Omega \) is a \(\pi \)-system if it is closed under the formation of finite intersections: \[A,B\in \mathcal {P} \implies A\cap B\in \mathcal {P}.\]

Definition. \(\lambda \)-system [0019]

  • November 26, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

A class \(\mathscr {L}\) is a \(\lambda \)-system if it contains \(\Omega \) and is closed under the formation of complements and of finite and countable disjoint unions:

  1. \(\Omega \in \mathscr {L}\);
  2. \(A\in \mathscr {L}\) implies \(A^{c}\in \mathscr {L}\);
  3. \(A_{1},A_{2},\ldots ,\in \mathscr {L}\) and \(A_{n}\cap A_{m}=\varnothing \) for \(m\neq n\) imply \(\bigcup _{n}A_{n}\in \mathscr {L}\).

I was a bit unsure that a \(\lambda \)-system is not a \(\sigma \)-field, but the author provided an example that I will walk through to understand the distinction

Example. Consider a four point space \(\Omega = \{x_1, x_2, x_3, x_4\}\). We can define a \(\lambda \)-system by taking \(\mathscr {L} = \emptyset , \Omega ,\) and the six two point sets. The union of any two disjoint two point sets will be \(\Omega \). However, a non-disjoint union of two disjoint sets can result in a three point set, which is not in \(\mathscr {L}\). These non-disjoint unions would be covered in a \(\sigma \)-field.

To remedy this disconnect, we have the following lemma:

Lemma. A \(\pi \)-system that is also a \(\lambda \)-system is a \(\sigma \)-field [001A]

  • November 26, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

A class that is both a \(\pi \)-system and a \(\lambda \)-system is a \(\sigma \)-field.

In our example, we could take the intersection of say \(\{x_1, x_2\}\) and \(\{x_2, x_3\}\) to get \(\{x_2\}\). We could then take a disjoint union of \(\{x_1, x_3\}\) and \(\{x_2\}\) to get \(\{x_1, x_2, x_3\}\).

Theorem. Dynkin's \(\pi \text {-}\lambda \) [001B]

  • November 26, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

f \(\mathscr {P}\) is a \(\pi \)-system and \(\mathscr {L}\) is a \(\lambda \)-system, then \(\mathscr {P}\subset \mathscr {L}\) implies \(\sigma (\mathscr {P})\subset \mathscr {L}\).

Now we will see how this allows us to prove our desired result i.e. the uniqueness of our extension of a probability on an algebra via the outer measure.

Theorem. Uniqueness of Extension [001C]

  • November 26, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

Suppose that \(P_{1}\) and \(P_{2}\) are probability measures on \(\sigma (\mathscr {P})\), where \(\mathscr {P}\) is a \(\pi \)-system. If \(P_{1}\) and \(P_{2}\) agree on \(\mathscr {P}\), then they agree on \(\sigma (\mathscr {P})\).

Proof. Take \(\mathscr {L}\) to be the class of sets such that \(A \in \mathscr {L} \implies P_1(A) = P_2(A)\). Since \(\mathscr {P}\) is a \(\pi \)-system, from Theorem 9, it is sufficient to show that \(\mathscr {L}\) is a \(\lambda \)-system. Since \(\Omega \in \mathscr {P}\), \(\Omega \in \mathscr {L}\). If \(A \in \mathscr {L}\), then \[P_1(A^c) = 1 - P_1(A) = 1 - P_2(A) = P_2(A^c).\] Therefore, \(A^c \in \mathscr {L}\). Finally, if \(A_1, A_2, \ldots \) disjoint then \[ P_1\left (\bigcup _{n = 1} A_n\right ) = \sum _{n = 1} P_1(A_n) = \sum _{n = 1}P_2 (A_n) = P_2\left (\bigcup _{n = 1}A_n\right ) \] Thus, \(\mathscr {L}\) is a \(\lambda \)-system. From Theorem 9, \(\sigma (\mathscr {P}) \subset \mathscr {L}\) and \(P_1\) and \(P_2\) agree on all of \(\sigma (\mathscr {P})\).

An (important?) note is that this works due to our definition of a Probability Measure. Namely, countable additivity is really a condition on countable disjoint unions, meaning that the \(\sigma \)-field induced by a Probability Measure does not make full use of the generality allowed under arbitrary countable unions because it must satisfy countable additivity.

For completeness and future use, we introduce here the notion of monotone classes and Halmo's useful theorem.

Definition. Monotone Class [001D]

  • November 26, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

A class \(\mathscr {M}\) of subsets of \(\Omega \) is monotone if it is closed under the formation of monotone unions and intersections:

  1. \(A_{1},A_{2},\ldots \in \mathscr {M}\) and \(A_{n}\uparrow A\) imply \(A\in \mathscr {M}\);
  2. \(A_{1},A_{2},\ \ldots \in \mathscr {M}\) and \(A_{n}\downarrow A\) imply \(A\in \mathscr {M}\).

Theorem. Halmo's Monotone Class Theorem [001E]

  • November 26, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

If \(\mathscr {F}_{0}\) is a field and \(\mathscr {A}\) is a monotone class, then \(\mathscr {F}_{0}\subset \mathscr {A}\) implies \(\sigma (\mathscr {F}_{0})\subset \mathscr {A}\).

Also for completeness, we introduce completeness lol.

Definition. Completeness of a measure [001F]

  • November 26, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

A probability measure \((\Omega , \mathcal {F}, P)\) is complete if for \(A \subset B\) if \(P(B) = 0\) then \(P(A) = 0\).

You might be wondering when this is not the case because we have subadditivity. However, it is not the case that \(P(A) > 0\) but instead that \(A\) is not measurable. As an example,

Example. Vitali Sets [001G]

  • November 26, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

If we consider \([0,1]\), then we can partition \([0, 1]\) into equivalent classes as \(x \sim y\) if \(x - y = r\) for some \(r \in \mathbb {Q} \cap [0, 1]\). Denote each of these as \(V_{r}\). By the Axiom of Choice, we can choose one representative from each \(V_r\). We call the set of these representatives \(V\). Therefore, we can write \([0, 1]\) as a countable union via \([0,1] = \bigcup _{r \in \mathbb {Q}} V + r\). For the Lebesgue measure \(\lambda \), each \(\lambda (V + r) = \lambda (V)\). Since each \(V + r\) is disjoint, we have \[\lambda \left (\bigcup _{r \in \mathbb {Q}} V + r\right ) = \sum _{r \in \mathbb {Q}} \lambda (V)\] If \(\lambda (V) = 0\), then \(\lambda ([0,1]) \leq \lambda (V) = 0\) which is a contradiction. Otherwise \(\lambda ([0,1]) = \sum _{r \in \mathbb {Q}} k = \infty \) for some \(k \in \mathbb {R}\). Thus, \(V\) is not Lebesgue measurable.

Exercise 3.2 makes the characterization sharp: a set is \(P^*\)-measurable iff its outer and inner measures agree.

Denumerable Probabilities

  • October 30, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios
 The results of this section concern infinite sequences of events in a probability space. They will be illustrated by examples in the unit interval. By this will always be meant the triple (\(\Omega \), \(\mathscr {F}\), \(P\)) for which \(\Omega \) is (0,1), \(\mathscr {F}\) is the \(\sigma \)-field \(\mathscr {B}\) of Borel sets there, and \(P(A)\) is for \(A\) in \(\mathscr {F}\) the Lebesgue measure \(\lambda (A)\) of \(A\).

Definition. Conditional Probability [001J]

  • December 2, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

\[P(A | B) = \frac {P(A \cap B)}{P(B)}\]

Proposition. Chain Rule of Probability [001I]

  • December 2, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

\[P(A \cap B \cap C) = P(A)P(B | A)P(C | B \cap A)\]

Definition. limsup of sets [001M]

  • December 26, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

For a sequence \(A_1, A_2, \ldots \) of sets, we define \[\lim \sup _{n} A_n = \bigcap _{n = 1}^{\infty } \bigcup _{k = n}^{\infty } A_k\] In english, an element \(\omega \) is in \(\lim \sup _{n} A_{n}\) if \(\omega \) is in \(A_{n}\) infinitely often.

Definition. liminf of sets [001N]

  • December 26, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

For a sequence \(A_1, A_2, \ldots \) of sets, we define \[\lim \inf _{n} A_n = \bigcup _{n = 1}^{\infty } \bigcap _{k = n}^{\infty } A_k\] In english, an element \(\omega \) is in \(\lim \inf _{n} A_{n}\) if \(\omega \) is in all but finitely many \(A_{n}\).

Theorem. Fatou's lemma for events [001K]

  • December 2, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

For each sequence \(\{A_{n}\}\), \[P\left (\lim \inf _{n}A_{n}\right )\leq \lim _{n}\inf _{n}P\left (\,A_{n}\right ) \leq \lim _{n}\sup _{n}P\left (\,A_{n}\right )\leq P\left (\lim _{n}\sup _{n}A_{n}\right ).\]

Proof. We define \(B_n \coloneqq \bigcup _{k = n}^{\infty } A_k\) and \(B_n\) as \(C_n = \bigcap _{k = n}^{\infty } A_k\). Note that \(\{B_n\}\) is a monotonically decreasing sequence to \(\lim \sup _n A_n\), and \(C_n\) monotonically increases to \(\lim \inf _{n} A_n\). Therefore, we can apply continuity from below / above such that \[ \begin {align*} \inf _n P(A_n) \geq P(C_n) \iff \lim _n \inf _n P(A_n) &\geq \lim _n P(C_n) \\ &= P(\lim _n C_n) \\ &= P(\lim \inf _n A_n) \end {align*} \] Similarly, \[ \begin {align*} \sup _n P(A_n) \leq P(B_n) \iff \lim _n \sup _n P(A_n) &\leq \lim _n P(B_n) \\ &= P(\lim _n B_n) \\ &= P(\lim \sup _n A_n) \end {align*} \]

Definition. Independence [001O]

  • December 26, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

Independence evolves in the following manner:

  1. Events \(A\) and \(B\) are independent if \(P(A \cap B) = P(A)P(B)\).
  2. A finite collection of events are independent if \[P(A_{k_1} \cap \cdots \cap A_{k_j}) = P(A_{k_1}) \cdots P(A_{k_j})\]
  3. A infinite collection of sets is defined to be independent if every finite subcollection is independent.
  4. Classes \(\mathscr {A}_1, \ldots , \mathscr {A}_n\) are independent if for any events \(A_1 \in \mathscr {A}_1, \ldots , A_n \in \mathscr {A}_n\), \(A_1, \ldots , A_n\) are independent.

Theorem. Independent \(\pi \)-systems generate independent \(\sigma \)-algebras [001P]

  • December 27, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

If \(\mathscr {A}_1, \ldots , \mathscr {A}_n\) are independent and each \(\mathscr {A}_i\) is a \(\pi \)-system then \(\sigma (\mathscr {A}_1), \ldots , \sigma (\mathscr {A}_n)\) are independent.

Proof. We have sequence of independent classes and want to show that their respective \(\sigma \)-algebras are also independent. The idea is to take the class of events \(B_1\) that are independent with \(\mathscr {A}_2, \ldots , \mathscr {A}_n\), and show that \(\sigma (\mathscr {A}_1)\) is a subset of this class. To do this, we use Dynkin's \(\pi \text {-}\lambda \) by noticing that this class is a \(\lambda \)-system that contains the \(\pi \)-system \(\mathscr {A}_1\).

Meaning in english. In the book, they give the example of even and odd numbered coin tosses. We want to know when a new event only depends on even or odd coin tosses. Concretely, we know that even and odd coin tosses are independent, and we want to know if, for example, the outcome of every fourth coin toss is independent of odd tosses. We can do this rigorously by showing that this event is in the \(\sigma \)-algebra of even coin tosses. Then by Theorem 5.7, we know that this event is also independent of odd coin tosses.

Theorem. Array Method [001R]

  • December 27, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

Suppose that the array \[ \begin {array}{ccc} A_{11} & A_{12} & \cdots \\ A_{21} & A_{22} & \cdots \\ \vdots & \vdots & \end {array} \] of events is independent. If \(\mathscr {F}_1\) is the \(\sigma \)-algebra generated by the first row then \(\mathscr {F}_1, \mathscr {F}_2, \ldots \) are independent.

Example. Can write events of flip \(i\) as \(H_i\) such that the following array satisfies the above \[ \begin {array}{ccc} H_{2} & H_{4} & \cdots \\ H_{1} & H_3 & \cdots \\ \end {array} \] Then row 1 and row 2 are independent from above. Therefore, we have shown that even and odd numbered flips' \(\sigma \)-field's are independent.

Notebook: Probability and measure › Subfields

  • October 30, 2024

Remark. Intuition of subfields are spaces with partial information. My best memory of this coming up in probability was conditional expectation. Esssentially, we need to consider the subfield of events conditioned on some event that already occured.

Definition. \(\mathscr {A}\)-equivalent points [001S]

  • December 27, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

We say that points \(\omega \) and \(\omega '\) are \(\mathscr {A}\) equivalent if for every \(A \in \mathscr {A}\), \[I_A(\omega ) = I_A(\omega ')\]

From this definition, we can conclude an observer with the information of \(\sigma (\mathscr {A})\) has only the information of the equivalence class but cannot identify a specific point from this class.

Notebook: Probability and measure › The Borel-Cantelli Lemmas

  • October 30, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

Theorem. Borel-Cantelli One [001Q]

  • December 27, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

If \(\sum _{n}P(A_n)\) converges, then \(P(\lim \sup _n A_n) = 0\).

Proof. Recall that \(\lim \sup _n A_n \subset \bigcup _{k = n}^{\infty } A_k\). Therefore, \[P(\lim \sup _n A_n) \leq P\left (\bigcup _{k = n}^{\infty } A_k\right ) \leq \sum _{k = n}^{\infty } P(A_k)\] Taking \(n \to \infty \) yields the desired result.

Theorem. Borel-Cantelli Two [001T]

  • December 27, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

If \(\{A_n\}\) is an independent sequence of events and \(\sum _{n}P(A_n)\) diverges, then \(P(\lim \sup _n A_n) = 1\).

Proof. Consider the complement. By independence, we have \[P(\bigcap _{k = n}^{n + m} A_k^c) = \prod _{k = n}^{n + m}(1 - P(A_k)) \leq \exp (-\sum _{k = n}^{n + m} P(A_k)),\] where the last inequality comes from \(1 - x \leq e^{-x}\). Since the series diverges, taking \(m \to \infty \) yields the desired result.

Notebook: Probability and measure › Zero-one Law

  • October 30, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

Definition. Tail \(\sigma \)-field [001U]

  • December 27, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

For a sequence \(A_1, A_2, \ldots \) of events in a probability space \((\Omega , \mathscr {F}, P)\) consider the \(\sigma \)-fields \(\sigma (A_n, A_{n+1}, \ldots )\) then their intersection \[\mathscr {T} = \bigcap _{n = 1}^{\infty } \sigma (A_n, A_{n+1}, \ldots )\] is the tail \(\sigma \)-field associated with the sequence \(\{A_n\}\).

Example. The \(\lim \sup _n A_n\) and \(\lim \inf _n A_n\) are both tail events.

Theorem. Kolmogorov's Zero-one Law [001V]

  • December 27, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

If \(A_1, A_2, \ldots \) is an independent sequence of events, then for each event \(A\) in the Tail \(\sigma \)-field, \(P(A)\) is either \(0\) or \(1\).

Random Variables

  • October 30, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

Definition. Simple Random Variable [001W]

  • December 28, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

Let \((\Omega , \mathscr {F}, P)\) be an arbitrary probability space, and let \(X\) be a real-valued function on \(\Omega \). \(X\) is a simple random variable if it has finite range and if \[\left [\omega \mid X(\omega ) = x \right ] \in \mathscr {F}\] for each \(x\) in its finite range.

Example. Consider the function \[X = \sum _{i} x_i I_{A_i}\] If \(A_i\) are finite partition of \(\Omega \) into \(\mathscr {F}\)-sets, then inverse image is at most countable union of the A_i's, which is an \(\mathscr {F}\)-set. Therefore, this function is measurable.

Theorem. Finite Sequence Measurability [0022]

  • December 28, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

Let \(X_1, \ldots , X_n\) be Simple Random Variable's

  1. The \(\sigma \)-field \(\sigma (X_1, \ldots , X_n)\) consists of the sets \[\left [(X_1, \ldots , X_n) \in H\right ] = \left [\omega \mid (X_1(\omega ), \ldots , X_n(\omega )) \in H\right ]\] for \(H \subset \mathbb {R}^n\).
  2. A simple random variable \(Y\) is measurable \(\sigma (X_1, \ldots , X_n)\) if and only if \[Y = f(X_1, \ldots , X_n)\] for some \(f: \mathbb {R}^n \to \mathbb {R}\).

Definition. \(\sigma \)-field of RV [001X]

  • December 28, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

The \(\sigma \)-field generated by a Simple Random Variable is the smallest \(\sigma \)-field such that the Simple Random Variable is measurable. For a sequence of Simple Random Variable's, the \(\sigma \)-field generated by them is the smallest \(\sigma \)-field, such that each of the Simple Random Variable's is measurable.

Notebook: Probability and measure › Convergence of Random Variables

  • October 30, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios
 In probability, rather than standard convergence, we actually can consider convergence with high probability or almost surely (should come up later). Formally, we care about measuring the probability of the event \(\{\omega \mid \lim _n X_n(\omega ) \to X(\omega )\}\). The first question we must ask is whether this set is measurable. They do not really cover this in the textbook, but it is an important trick that comes up a lot.
Proof. We consider the complement. Note that the sequence of random variables not converging can be written as \(\forall \epsilon , \exists n\) such that \(|X_n(\omega ) - X(\omega )| \geq \epsilon \). For a fixed \(\epsilon \), this is the \(\lim \sup \) of the sequence \(A_n \coloneqq [|X_n - X| \geq \epsilon ]\). We know that \(\lim \sup \) of measurable sequence is measurable because it is a countable intersection of countable unions. Therefore, the last thing we need is to show for all \(\epsilon \). This can be done just using rationals because \(\mathbb {Q}\) is dense in \(\mathbb {R}\). Then we have a countable union of countable sets as desired.

Almost Sure Convergence [001Z]

  • December 28, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

A Simple Random Variable converges almost surely if \[P(X_n \to X) = 1\]

Convergence in Probability [0020]

  • December 28, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

A Simple Random Variable converges in probability if for every \(\epsilon \) \[\lim _{n \to \infty } P(|X_n - X| > \epsilon ) = 0\]

Remark. By Theorem 5.5, almost sure convergence \(\implies \) convergence in probability. Converse is NOT true.
Example. Can make array of rows, where the \(n\)th row consists of \(n\) intervals that evenly divide \([0,1]\). Then \(P(A_n) \to 0\) but \([A_n \text { i.o}] = [0,1]\)

Definition. Independence of Random Variable [0021]

  • December 28, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

A sequence of random variables \(X_1, X_2, \ldots \) is independent if the classes \(\sigma (X_1), \sigma (X_2), \ldots \) are independent.

Notebook: Probability and measure › Existence of Independent Sequences

Definition. Distribution [0023]

  • December 28, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

The distribution of a simple random variable \(X\) is the probability measure \(\mu \) defined for all subsets \(A\) of the line by \[\mu (A) = P[X \in A]\]

Theorem. Existence of independent sequences with prescribed distributions [0024]

  • December 28, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

Let \(\{\mu _n\}\) be a sequence of probability measures on the class of all subsets of the line, each having finite support. There exists some probability space \((\Omega , \mathscr {F}, P)\) an independent sequence \(\{X_n\}\) of Simple Random Variable's such that \(X_n\) has distribution \(\mu _n\).

Guess for english translation: I think what this is saying is that rather than try to define a probability space for a random variable, we can instead just pick probability measures and know that the Simple Random Variable come for free? i.e. (from the book) we can just define a trivial space \(\Omega = \{1, 0\}\) and probability measure \(P(1) = p\), \(P(0) = 1 - p\) and know that we can get a Bernoulli random variable? In this case \(X(1) = 1\), \(X(0) = 0\) such that \(P(X = 1) = p\) and \(P(X = 0) = 1 - p\)?

Remark. In the text, they say that the underlying probability space does not really matter. Basically, all calculations are determined by the distribution in range space. So I guess that is also what this theorem is getting at?

Notebook: Probability and measure › Expected Value

  • October 30, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

Definition. Expected Value [0029]

  • December 28, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

A Simple Random Variable of the form \(X = \sum _{i} x_i I_{A_i}\) is assigned expected value as \[\mathbb {E}[X] = \mathbb {E}\left [\sum _i x_i I_{A_i} \right ] = \sum _i x_i P(A_i)\] More generally, if \(f(X) = \sum _i f(x_i) I_{A_i}\) then \[\mathbb {E}\left [f(X)\right ] = \sum _i f(x_i) P(A_i) = \sum _x f(x) P[X = x]\] A useful identity is as follows: \[\mathbb {E}[X] = \int _0^{\infty } P[X \geq x] \mathrm {d}x\]

Definition. \(k\)th Moment [002A]

  • December 28, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

We define the \(k\)th moment of \(X\) as \[\mathbb {E}[X^k] = \sum _y y P[X^k = y]\]

Definition. Uniformly Bounded [002B]

  • December 28, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

If \(\exists K\) such that \(X_n(\omega ) \leq K\) for every \(\omega \) and \(n\) then the \(X_n\) are uniformly bounded.

Theorem. Bounded convergence theorem [002C]

  • December 28, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

If \(\{X_n\}\) is uniformly bounded, and if \(X = \lim _n X_n\) with probability \(1\), then \(\mathbb {E}[X] = \lim _n \mathbb {E}[X]\)

Notebook: Probability and measure › Inequalities

  • October 30, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

Theorem. Markov's Inequality [002E]

  • December 29, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

\[P[X \geq \alpha ] \leq \frac {1}{\alpha } \mathbb {E}[X]\]

Theorem. Chebyshev's Inequality [002F]

  • December 29, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

\[P[|X - m| \geq \alpha ] \leq \frac {1}{\alpha ^2} \mathrm {Var}[X]\]

Theorem. Jensen's Inequality [002G]

  • December 29, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

If \(\phi \) is a convex function on the range of \(X\) then \[\phi (\mathbb {E}[X]) \leq \mathbb {E}[\phi (X)]\]

Theorem. Holder's Inequality [002H]

  • December 29, 2024
  • Kellen Kanarios

Suppose that \[\frac {1}{p} + \frac {1}{q} = 1, \quad p > 1,\ q > 1\] Then Holder's inequality is \[\mathbb {E}[XY] \leq \left (\mathbb {E}[|X|^p]\right )^{1/p} \cdot \left (\mathbb {E}[|Y|^q]\right )^{1/q}\]
